"If he's moving discretionary money through a secondary authorization," Damian said at last, "then the timing is worse than I thought."

"Because of the money."

"Because of the speed." He rubbed a hand over his mouth. "A secondary line means somebody wants committee looking at the thing after it's already alive. Four hundred thousand isn't one bribe and it isn't petty theft. That's infrastructure. Shipping, movement, warehousing, maybe components if Krueger-Schiff is handling the freight end."

"So not a man lining his pockets."

"No." He looked out through the trees. "A man building a pipe."

"Bretz thinks Hartig reports up."

"He does." Damian's thoughts were already making their own map. *Hartig doesn't have the courage for a structure this exposed without cover. Undersecretary's office. Somebody above him wants deniability.* "Nobody builds a pipe like that for personal amusement."

"Good. I hate when corruption is boring."

"This one may still be boring. It just might also get people killed."

The words sat in the cold between them. Anya flexed her toes against the gravel hard enough to feel each stone through her stocking.

"Your freedom cost you a dinner invitation."

"My freedom cost me a dinner invitation, a niece with strong opinions about clavicles, and forty-five minutes of charts on horse feed." He loosened his tie another inch. On him it looked almost reckless. "I think Ernst Hassler carries them for comfort."

"What kind of person brings charts to cognac."

"An Ostanian man with money and no one left who can stop him."

"That's bleak."

"You didn't sit through the presentation."

She tilted her head back against the bench and looked at the branches above them.

"Margarethe likes you."

"Margarethe likes disruption in a tailored jacket."

"Still counts."

"I'm choosing to believe you don't mean that as an insult."

"I haven't decided."

That got a tired half laugh from him.

"You were good in there," he said after a moment.

"So were you."

"I nearly left you to die with Kaiser."

"Kaiser was the least dangerous thing in that room."

"You say that because he liked you."

"He has—" She stopped herself. "He liked me. I'm counting it."

"Even after the theft."

"Especially after the theft."

He turned to look at her. "You did let that run a second longer than most people would have."

There was no bite in it. He was only looking at her, waiting.

She shrugged one shoulder.

"He was enjoying himself."

"That explains Kaiser, not you."

"It does if you respect art."

He stared at her for one beat, then laughed despite himself. Short. Quiet. Real.

"Fine," he said. "Art."

"Thank you."

"I didn't say it was good art." But he was trying not to smile when he said it.

For one brief stretch the pressure sat under the bench instead of between them, mixed up with her shoes, the dead leaves, and Hartig's name.

"Still," he said, looking back toward the fountain, "I owe you one."

"You owe me shoes without weapons-grade heels."

"That's fair."

"And maybe dinner somewhere without horse charts."

He made the mistake of imagining Margarethe's niece at the exact wrong moment. The thought came through in a miserable, well-dressed blur. *Pearls. Questions about family. Somebody saying promising jawline as if he's livestock.*

That one got her. The laugh came out before she could manage it, loose and too loud in the cold, the real one with the hitch at the end.

Damian went still.

His thoughts came in bright involuntary fragments. *Eden courtyard. Autumn sports day. Chalk on the lane. East wall. Her laugh getting out before she could stop it.*

He sat there listening to the sound of it fade and then said, "There was a girl at Eden who laughed like that. Autumn relay. Baton over the east wall. She went up the linden after it because nobody else was moving fast enough."

The memory hit sharp enough to make her grip the heel straps tighter. She could feel chalk dust and damp bark against her palms, and Damian below the wall, furious about the handoff and even more furious that she had beaten a teacher to the baton.

"You remember a lot for somebody who lost," she said.

He looked at her then. "I remember enough."

Too much room opened under that sentence. She bent over her shoes instead of his face.

"I'm not asking the school question again tonight," he said. "I'm asking for something else."

She looked up.

"In there, you got ahead of the room. Ahead of Bretz. Ahead of me." He rubbed a thumb over his palm, thinking through the words as he went. "If you're about to push past what we agreed because you've seen a line I haven't, give me warning. Touch my wrist. Say my name. I don't care. Just don't make me catch up in public."

That was new enough to hurt.

"You want notice before I do something annoying."

"I want a second to choose it with you instead of after you've done it."

The fountain kept breathing in the dark.

She could have lied cleanly. She said, "I can do that."

His shoulders loosened a fraction. "All right."

"We should go," she said. "My feet are freezing."

"Yeah."

He stood, hooked both heels by their straps, and held out his free hand. She took it. Gravel bit through her stockings on the walk back to the car, and he didn't let go.


# Chapter 6

The next evening, twenty-four hours after the Bretz operation had run long and cold and left Hartig's name stuck to the back of her teeth, Anya hit the landing outside her parents' apartment and paused with the spare key between her fingers. Yor was cooking. Garlic, sugar, butter, and heat had pushed past partnership into open hostility, a scorched sweetness that stuck to the stairwell walls and would probably still be there tomorrow.

She'd spent the day at her cover office moving paper from one pile to another and deleting four versions of a message to Damian. One said Hartig's name and nothing else. One pretended to answer the dinner invitation he'd rather fake his own death than accept. None of them were safe now that the look he'd given her on the bench had an Eden memory attached to it. By lunch she'd settled on the only part she could control: first thing tomorrow she would run Hartig through whatever public-event, transport, and procurement files her cover desk could touch before Damian tried to push the lead through his side. Tonight she wasn't putting any of it in writing. She was still hearing Damian on the park bench, still feeling the shape of his hand from the walk back to the car, still running Hartig through every corridor of her head until the name stopped sounding like a person and started sounding like a door. None of it had worked. By then she was tired enough for home to feel less like a comfort than a place where she had to keep too many parts of herself from spilling.

Anya let herself in with the spare key she'd carried since she was nine. The apartment hit her the way it always did: warm, cluttered, faintly of Bond's fur beneath whatever Yor had done to the kitchen. Loid's shoes were lined up by the door, toes to the wall, exactly parallel. Yor's were kicked off at different angles, one on its side. Bond was asleep on the couch with his head hanging off the armrest, white fur covering the cushions in the thin permanent layer no amount of vacuuming ever fully beat. A grocery sack sat by the kitchen entrance with leeks sticking out of the top. Someone had meant well. Someone else had added heat and optimism.

"Anya!" Yor appeared from the kitchen doorway, flushed and holding a wooden spoon like a weapon she'd forgotten to put down. "You're early. I was going to have everything ready. The stew isn't quite, well, it's almost, it's getting there, I think."

*Too much heat again. Bottom's gone. Maybe the middle survived. No, that is also gone. Oh no.*

"Smells great, Mama."

"Does it? Really?" Yor's face brightened with a hope that made Anya's chest hurt. "Loid said it smelled 'interesting,' which I think means he was being polite."

"It means it smells interesting," Loid said from inside the kitchen, where he was wiping down the counter with the unhurried efficiency of a man who had already finished his own preparations and was now managing the aftermath of someone else's. He came out drying his hands on a dish towel. "Welcome home."

He hugged her. Brief and warm, his arms tightening once before letting go. The thought under it hit in pieces. *Too light. Eyes bruised. Not sleeping. Ask later. Don't start at the door.*

Anya pulled back and smiled at him. "I'm fine, Papa."

"I didn't say anything."

"You were about to."

That got a real corner of a smile out of him, though the assessment stayed in his face a beat longer than the smile. Then it softened and he was her father again. "I cleaned up the leftover chicken. There's a plate on the counter. I made rice too."

Yor made a small sound of affront. "The stew is fine."

There was a clatter, then a smaller clatter, then a pause.

"Sorry," Yor called. "Fine. Everything's fine."

Anya dropped her bag by the door and crossed to the couch. Bond opened one eye, registered her, and closed it again. His tail moved once against the cushion, a single thump of acknowledgment that counted as his whole greeting now that his joints were what they were. Her fingers found the warm place behind his ears and his thoughts drifted through her like bathwater. *girl. home. floor cold. sleep.* His head tipped harder into her palm.

She sat on the arm of the couch and let the apartment settle around her. The television murmured low, a news broadcast about trade negotiations that nobody was admitting to watch. A stack of newspapers sat by Loid's chair, dotted with the small pencil marks he used to cross-reference sources and flag discrepancies. To anyone else they looked like idle habit. Anya had known since she was seven that they were an indexing system he kept by reflex, and she had learned that while he taught her long division. Yor's cardigan was draped over a chair. Two of Bond's rubber toys sat by the bookshelf in a row too orderly for a dog to have arranged. Loid's hand, tidying without noticing.

Loid settled into his chair and picked up the newspaper. The thought beneath the rustle came broken and quick. *Border patrol again. Southeastern corridor. If her sector... no. Not at dinner.*

The worry wasn't new. He carried it the way he carried everything else: quietly, efficiently, pretending it was only vigilance when some part of it had become love years ago and he no longer knew where the line sat.

"The news said the trade talks were stalling," she said, because it was the kind of thing a daughter could say without meaning anything by it.

"They'll restart. They always do." He turned a page. *Geopolitics at dinner. Casual, or not casual.*

Dinner was the stew, plus the rice Loid had made without announcing himself as backup, plus the chicken plate on the counter under foil like a diplomatic alternative nobody needed to mention unless absolutely necessary. Yor served the stew with a hopefulness that bordered on prayer, ladling it into bowls with extra care as if gentleness could retroactively improve the cooking. Anya ate it. The stew was thick and combative and tasted like three different recipes had argued over the same pot and nobody had won. Her mother had spent the afternoon on it, though, and love was the only ingredient that had survived the heat.

"Mrs. Kaplan complained about the pipes again," Yor said, blowing on her spoon. "She said the water pressure on the fourth floor was, what was her word, 'insulting.' She said the water pressure was insulting to her personally."

"It was low last week," Loid said. "I adjusted the valve in the basement."

"She didn't seem to know that. She was telling Bond about it in the hallway for about ten minutes. I think she believes he's the building superintendent."

"Bond was in the hallway?"

"He got out when I was bringing in the groceries. He lay down in front of her door and she started talking. I didn't want to interrupt."

Anya laughed. It came out real and easy and louder than the joke deserved, but she had not laughed in days and the release was involuntary.

Loid's thought brushed past her, rough as static. *There. Good. When did she stop doing that so often. Stop.*

She looked down at her bowl.

"How's work, Mama? Besides the pipes."

"Oh, fine, fine." Yor waved her spoon. "Busy, but you know. They reorganized the whole records system at city hall, which was, um. An experience. I don't think Mr. Henning has recovered. He kept trying to file things in the old cabinets and they'd already been removed, so he stood in front of an empty wall holding folders. I felt terrible for him."

"Did you help?"

"I helped him find a chair and made tea. That seemed like the right amount."

Yor smiled, and the smile was real. Then the thought crossed under it, quick and bright enough to sting.

*On his knees. Wrong room. Hands empty. Please, I have a... briefing said armed. Window there, not door. Wrong.*

Yor's fingers tightened on the spoon. "Anyway. The new system is fine. It takes getting used to."

A man on his knees. Empty hands. Wrong room.

Her hand went to the bread because it needed somewhere to go. If she said anything now, she'd have to say how she knew.

"This bread's good," she said.

"The place on Kirchner Street," Loid said. "The woman with the, what do you call it, firm customer service approach."

"She's mean," Yor said.

"She's efficient."

"She's efficiently mean. She told me I was buying too much rye."

"Were you?"

"That's not the point, Loid."

Yor stood to get more bread and nearly knocked over the salt. Loid caught it without looking. The movement was so automatic it would have been invisible to anyone who had not spent a childhood watching him do six things at once and call it normal.

"You should take food back with you tonight," Yor said. "Not the stew unless you want to, although if you want to I can pack it, but maybe the chicken is safer. Or the rice. I made too much rice. Well. Loid made too much rice."

"I didn't make too much rice," Loid said. "I made the correct amount of insurance."

Anya snorted.

"I eat, you know."

"I know," Yor said quickly. "I mean when you get home late and don't feel like cooking and then you decide crackers are a meal, which they aren't."

Loid glanced at her bowl, then at her face, not even trying to hide the parental arithmetic. "Crackers are not a meal."

"They're a category."

"They are not."

"Sometimes they are."

Yor made a helpless little gesture with the spoon. *She looks tired enough to fall asleep sitting up. Don't say that. Feed her first.*

"You both do realize I'm an adult," Anya said.

"We do," Loid said. "It's inconvenient."

That got Yor laughing. Bond opened his eyes again at the sound and this time pushed himself upright with the offended groan of an old dog having to negotiate with his own hips. His nails clicked across the floor in an uneven rhythm as he made his way toward the kitchen. Loid got up and uncovered the backup plate. Yor immediately apologized to Bond for the stew, as if he'd been counting on it and been personally let down.

"You didn't want this," she told him. "That's all right. I understand."

Bond accepted two pieces of chicken with solemn gratitude. *chicken. good. knees bad. girl here. good.* He left one shred behind, either full or making a point.

The anchor on television had moved from stalled trade talks to a list of upcoming diplomatic events in the same calm voice people used to read weather. A transport delegation. A party conference. Renovation delays at some state venue the camera showed only in passing before moving on. Loid muted it with the remote without looking away from Bond.

"How's your place?" Yor asked. "Is the upstairs radiator still making that knocking sound?"

"Only when it wants attention."

"That sounds expensive."

"It sounds haunted."

"Haunted is cheaper," Loid said.

"That is not how home repair works."

"It's how landlords work."

Anya ate more stew because it gave her a job.

Yor was already telling a story about a colleague at city hall whose birthday cake had arrived with the wrong name on it. Heinrich, apparently, had taken the whole cake home before anyone else could touch it because the icing error made it "administratively contaminated." Loid asked if that was an official city policy. Yor said it was now. Anya laughed in the right places and let the rhythm of the table carry her a little longer than she deserved.

When the bowls were empty, Loid stood to clear them. Anya stood too because sitting still had started to feel suspicious, even to herself.

"Leave it," Yor said. "You're visiting."

"I'm visiting the sink," Anya said, taking the towel Loid handed her.

"Dry, then," he said. "No heroic interventions."

That was how the next ten minutes went: plates passed hand to hand, Yor talking about Mrs. Kaplan and the building committee and a woman at city hall who had declared the new filing labels anti-clerical, Loid making small corrective noises that were either agreement or factual amendment, Anya slotting forks into the drawer by muscle memory. Bond resumed his place by the couch after one hopeful detour back toward the chicken. If someone had photographed the kitchen then, it would have looked like the safest room in Berlint.

Then Bond woke up.

Not the slow surfacing of an old dog working himself out of sleep, not the stiff-legged resettling she'd watched. This was different. His head came up rigid, eyes fixed on nothing, ears flat to his skull. For a second the years fell off him.

"Bond?" Yor half turned from the sink, hands wet.

Anya was already inside it. Bond's mind cracked open and the images came hard, not thoughts or words but raw visual data hitting faster than she could organize it.

A building. Through Bond it came as a wide stone facade with columns, pale government stone, ceremonial steps. Then the angle changed. An upper terrace. Iron railing. Carved balustrade overlooking grounds torn up in ugly strips, grass and ornamental trees displaced. Men in stiff grey-blue formal SSS dress moved fast across the terrace. One was running. Behind them, below them, fire climbed with the hard orange color of accelerant. Stone cracked. Heat shoved at the image. Under the fuel stink came a sweeter rot, cut flowers left too long in water and then baked under lights. Bond couldn't name any of it. His body only knew wrong.

Bond whined. The image shredded. Another one flashed, briefer this time: the entrance from ground level, people spilling through the portico, pressure in the air like a blow before sound.

Then it collapsed. Bond blinked twice, confused by his own alertness, and let his head drop back to the couch arm. His tail gave one uncertain wag. He sighed.

The whole episode had lasted maybe eight seconds.

"Just a dream," Loid said, settling back in his chair. He was watching Bond with the casual attentiveness he gave to everything nearby, the kind that looked like ease and was surveillance. The thought under it came clipped. *Precog. Strong one. Too sharp. What did you get?*

Anya's fingers had dug into the couch cushion. Bond's visions blurred fast once they passed, but she had the bones of it: a government building, a terrace, SSS uniforms, fire that did not look accidental, and that stray sweet stink of flowers going hot. When Bond's visions came this sharp, they were never nothing.

On the television, the muted anchor had moved to a shot of suited men shaking hands under flags. The ticker was too far away to read from the couch.

Telling Loid would blow open the oldest secret she had. Keeping her mouth shut meant hoping Bond had finally gotten one wrong. Neither felt possible.

"Papa." The casualness in her voice was borrowed from him, that flat register that made important information sound incidental. "At work somebody mentioned extra security at some government events. Maybe nothing. I thought you might've heard."

Loid looked at her over the top of his newspaper.

For one second she was his daughter making dinner conversation. Then his gaze recalibrated and she felt the shift like a draft through a door.

*Disclaimer first. Too careful. Government events, vague on purpose. From where?*

He set the newspaper down. "I haven't heard anything specific. Which events?"

"I don't know. It was general. Security posture, heightened levels, that kind of thing. I wasn't really listening." Too many extra words. Anya could hear them stacking up and couldn't stop.

*Threat might be real. Source isn't what she said. She knows I heard that.*

"I'll mention it to a few people," Loid said. "See if anything matches."

He picked the newspaper back up, but he folded the top corner first, marking the page before he hid behind it again.

*Off-source. Revisit later.*

Anya took another bite of stew and tasted nothing.

Yor asked if anyone wanted tea. She cut pears that weren't ripe enough to matter. She resumed the birthday-cake story from the point where Heinrich had carried the contaminated cake out like evidence. Loid half smiled behind the paper. Bond had gone back to dreaming about pigeons in the yard. Anya bit into a pear and hit grit.

Anya dried the last plate and put it in the rack. Her hands were steady. That felt insulting.

---

The phone rang at half past eight. Not the kitchen landline. The other one, in the drawer by the hallway bookshelf, with the ringtone her family politely treated as normal.

"Excuse me," Anya said, and stepped into the hall. Through the doorway she could still see the kitchen: Yor wiping down the counter now that the dishes were done, humming off-key, the back of her head moving a little with the tune. Loid was stacking the rice containers in exact descending size order because he could not help himself. He did not look up as she left, but his thought reached her anyway. *Secure line. Again. More often lately. Since the sector change.*

The line connected and Sylvia's voice was there, flat and stripped to function.

"This is a routine follow-up on your assessment of the Ostanian operative in the diplomatic corps sector."

"Sure." Anya leaned against the hallway wall. Dish soap and burned sugar hung in the air behind her. "Go ahead."

"Another agency, I will not specify which, has flagged activity in the same sector you assessed in your initial report. They are identifying communication and movement patterns consistent with a coordinated operation. Their analysis does not match your conclusions."

The stew, which had already been sitting badly, felt like it was trying to leave her body by force.

"What kind of patterns?"

"Movement intercepts, financial flows, communication timing. Their analysts are reading the sector as high-activity and potentially high-threat. Your assessment characterized it as mid-level factional maneuvering with limited strategic significance."

"That was my read," Anya said. Through the doorway she watched her mother hold a plate to the light, checking for spots, turning it carefully. The thoroughness Yor gave to a white dinner plate.

"I am not questioning your read." The absence of accusation in Sylvia's voice had its own pressure. "I am informing you that the picture is moving in a direction your assessment did not anticipate. If your evaluation has changed since filing, now is the appropriate time to update it."

It hadn't changed. That was the problem. Damian's operation had gotten more dangerous, not less, and she still wasn't going to hand him to WISE because the alternative was watching them crush the only man inside that machine who had said no to it with his whole body. She pressed her shoulder harder into the wall.

"It hasn't changed. The sector is factional noise. Internal Ostanian positioning. It looks coordinated because factions coordinate. That's what they do."

Silence. Anya could picture Sylvia at her desk: shoulders squared over the file, coffee long cold, fluorescent light flattening everything in sight. She couldn't read thoughts through a phone line. Without that access, Sylvia's pauses felt enormous.

"Noted." Precise, neutral, and therefore worse. "I will include your reaffirmation in the file. If your assessment changes, contact me directly. Not through the regular reporting chain."

"Understood."

"How is your family?"

The question arrived with no transition, the way Sylvia asked personal questions, as if they belonged on the same checklist as movement intercepts.

"They're good. My mom made stew."

"Edible?"

"Interesting."

A breath that might have been amusement, or might have been Sylvia breathing out. "Good night, Anya."

The line went dead.

Another agency had found the seam she'd left in the file. The lie she'd pushed into WISE was coming back with teeth. Sylvia's voice had stayed flat, but the instruction to call direct sat hot in her palm long after the receiver went quiet.

Anya put the phone back in the drawer and returned to the kitchen. Yor had finished the dishes and was packing the chicken into a glass container with the stubborn optimism of a woman who refused to let anyone she loved leave empty-handed. Loid was reading again, or acting like it.

"Everything okay?" he asked without looking up.

"Fine. Work stuff."

The thought under the page came in hard little strikes. *Work stuff. Secure line. Eight-thirty. Doorway instead of table. Too much weight. I know that stance.*

"I'm going to lie down for a bit," Anya said. "Long week."

"Of course." Yor turned immediately, already reaching for the kettle she'd put back on without anybody noticing. "Chamomile. For sleeping. If you want it. You don't have to drink it. I thought it might help."

"You can stay the night," Loid said, still on the page, which was how she knew he'd been paying attention to every line of her body since she came back from the hall. "The bed's made."

"Thanks." The word caught more than she wanted it to. "Maybe I will."

Yor pressed the mug into her hands a minute later. "And take the chicken when you leave tomorrow. Or tonight. Whenever. It's already packed."

"Thanks, Mama."

Tea in hand, Anya went down the hall to her old bedroom.

The room was smaller than she remembered, which meant she was bigger, which meant time had done that mean trick it always did in old rooms: left everything where it was and made her too big for it. The bed was the same narrow frame from when she was six. Her desk still stood by the window. The drawers held pencil stubs, a protractor, a notebook from her last year at Eden Academy with handwriting that looked like a stranger's because it was a child's. Two glow-in-the-dark stars still clung to the headboard. In the dark they kept their faint dirty green.

Her old headband sat on the shelf. Black, horned, a little ridiculous, retired years ago for simpler clips except on nights she was back here and reached for it without thinking. The plastic was scuffed and slightly bent from daily use. Anya put it on and sat on the bed with the tea cooling on the desk.

Through the wall, her parents' voices moved low and warm, a conversation between people who had been together long enough to imply half of what they meant. Anya did not reach for the words. She had taken enough from this apartment tonight.

Still, the night would not leave her alone. Sylvia's measured follow-up. Loid's quiet *Revisit later.* Yor's flash of empty hands and the wrong room. Bond's terrace on fire. All of it wrapped around the same lie she had told for Damian and kept telling.

Anya set the tea down, pulled the old school notebook from the drawer, and flipped to a blank page in the back. Before Bond's vision could rot any further, she drew what she had: columns, steps, terrace, iron railing, fire breaking stone from below. Then she went back and added the other thing, the detail that made less sense than the flames. She wrote it in the margin because she did not know how to draw a smell: flowers in bad water. Hot stone. The lines came out rough and mean. By the time she finished, the edges were already starting to blur again.

She folded the page once and slid it under her pillow.

That lasted maybe half a minute.

Anya sat back up. From the hall came the small ordinary sounds of the apartment settling for night: cupboard door, bathroom tap, Yor laughing once at whatever Loid said too low for the words to carry. If she lay down now, she would not sleep. She would listen to Sylvia saying not through the regular reporting chain and Loid thinking off-source and Bond pushing heat and flowers and stone into her skull again.

The paper had already warmed under the pillow.

She pulled the paper back out, tucked it inside the notebook, and stepped into the hall in her socks.

The living room lights were off. The kitchen light was down to one lamp over the stove. Her parents' bedroom door had closed. On the coffee table Loid had left the evening papers folded with his pencil still clipped to one section. He would call it careless if anyone asked. It wasn't. He left things that way when some part of him wanted to come back to them before morning.

Anya sat on the edge of the couch beside Bond's sleeping bulk and opened the section he'd marked.

There it was halfway down the second page, small enough to miss if you weren't already looking for trouble: a story about summit preparations at the International Conference Center running behind schedule because the front grounds were still under renovation. Beneath the article sat a grainy photograph. Pale stone frontage. Ceremonial steps. Upper terrace behind iron railing. The grounds below torn up in ugly strips where new security barriers and landscaping were still unfinished.

Anya's stomach dropped so hard Bond twitched in his sleep beside her.

It wasn't proof. Bond's visions never came with labels. The angle in the photograph was wrong. The fire wasn't there. The men weren't running yet. But the bones matched. Enough of them did. Enough for her hands to start shaking after the recognition instead of before it.

She looked down the hall toward her parents' room.

If she took the paper to Loid right now, he would ask how she got from a dog freezing on the couch to a marked article in the Metro section. He would ask gently. That would somehow be worse. If she called Sylvia back tonight, direct line like instructed, she would be reporting an impossible source the same hour another agency was already checking her file for rot. If she did nothing, she was back to hoping Bond had seen the wrong building, the wrong terrace, the wrong dead flowers cooking under summit lights.

Bond let out a soft sleep-noise and pressed his nose harder into the cushion. *warm. pack. safe.*

Anya swallowed. "Yeah," she whispered, because there was nobody awake to hear it and because sometimes agreeing with a dog was the most honest thing available.

She took the pencil from the paper, carried the notebook back to her room, and wrote on the back of the drawing in small block letters:

International Conference Center?
Check venue files.
Do not tell Papa unless you can explain it.

She stared at the last line until it felt stupid and true at the same time.

Then she folded the page smaller than before and slid it into the passport sleeve of her work satchel, behind her cover identification and in front of the old receipt she kept there to make the pocket look used. Not under the pillow this time. Not in the drawer. In the bag she would take with her in the morning.

That was the real choice, and her body knew it before her head caught up. Under the pillow the sketch could still pretend to be a nightmare. In the satchel it was a task. Tomorrow she would go in early, pull whatever public-event and venue files she could justify, and find out whether Bond's burning terrace belonged to the summit. If it did, then the warning she'd thrown at Loid over dinner would not be enough, and neither would silence.

Anya set the satchel by the bedroom door so she could not conveniently forget it. Then she lay down under the old blanket and watched the two stars over the headboard glow their dirty green.

The folded page was no longer under her cheek. It was in the bag waiting by the door, and that was worse, because bags meant morning and morning meant choices that stayed made.

When sleep finally came, it came late and thin.


# Chapter 7

The conference room on the third floor of the National Unity Party headquarters smelled like old carpet and fresh coffee, and Damian Desmond was lying.

Not badly. He'd been lying in rooms like this since he was fifteen, since his father first brought him to a donors' reception and said, "Smile at the man in the grey suit. He funds our eastern district campaigns." Donovan Desmond hadn't needed to explain what kind of smile. Damian had understood already. You learned to lie early in this family, the way other children learned to ride bicycles.

"The Westalian delegation's posture at the trade summit was instructive," Damian said, standing at the front of a table of eight party loyalists. He kept his hands at his sides. "Their willingness to revisit border tariffs suggests they're testing our resolve. If we concede on agricultural inspection protocols, we signal flexibility. If we're perceived as flexible, we're perceived as weak."

Nods around the table. Deputy Chairman Holtz, who had known Donovan for thirty years and still considered himself a kingmaker, made notes in a leather notebook. Beside him, Petra Lang from the defense policy committee watched Damian with the fixed attention she gave people she hadn't decided whether to use or sideline.

"I'd recommend we draft a counter-position paper before the next session," Damian continued. "Something that reframes the tariff question as a security concern rather than an economic one. The language matters."

"Your father would have just told them no," Holtz said, not looking up.

"My father had leverage I don't have yet," Damian said. He kept his voice warm, a little rueful. "But the principle is the same. You don't negotiate from a posture of accommodation."

Holtz looked up. "You sound like him."

Damian accepted the compliment the way it was intended, as a leash lightly fitted. "I had a good teacher."

None of it was what he believed. The tariff question was routine economic maneuvering, and framing it as a security concern was the sort of escalatory language the Renewal Front fed on. Every time a trade dispute became a sovereignty dispute, the men who wanted war got to accuse everyone else of yielding ground. Damian had drafted the counter-position paper already, in his head. It disgusted him. He would write it this afternoon and it would be good, precise, persuasive, exactly the kind of thing a hawkish young Desmond would produce. Every sentence would be a small betrayal of the work he was actually trying to do.

The meeting went another forty minutes. Budget allocations for the eastern district campaigns. A proposed media response to the Westalian cultural exchange program, which Lang wanted to frame as soft-power infiltration. Damian contributed where expected, deflected where he could, and watched the room the way his father had taught him: not for what people said but for who looked at whom when someone else was talking. Holtz deferred to Lang on media strategy but resented her for it. Lang wanted a committee chairmanship and was building alliances Holtz hadn't noticed yet. A younger member named Fitz, who Damian had met twice, kept checking his watch, bored or anxious, hard to tell.

None of them were Renewal Front, as far as Damian could determine. That would have been simpler. They had organized their whole professional lives around Ostanian strength and Westalian threat, and they would lose status, funding, and purpose if those assumptions were dismantled. The Front didn't need to recruit people like Holtz and Lang. It only had to speak a little hotter than they already did.

Damian smiled at Fitz on the way out. "Good meeting," he said. Fitz looked relieved that someone had spoken to him.

He had almost reached the corridor before Lang's voice caught him.

"Desmond. A minute."

Holtz kept walking. Fitz disappeared around the corner with the speed of a junior man who had decided not to be visible.

Lang tucked her notebook under one arm. Up close, she smelled faintly of cigarette smoke under expensive soap. "Thursday. Eight o'clock. Small conference room off the defense committee offices."

Demetrius had said Thursday as if the invitation were still theoretical. Damian kept his face neutral. "I was planning to come."

"I know. Your brother made sure of that." She watched him for a beat. "What I need from you is not another trade memo in a different jacket. I need language that makes infrastructure sound like defense and defense sound like common sense."

"That's usually possible."

"Of course it is. The question is whether you're willing to be useful in the less polite rooms." Her gaze dropped to the folder in his hand, then came back to his face. "There will be contractors there. Procurement staff. Men who think every customs dispute should end with uniforms. If somebody says something ugly, don't look at them like it's new. People notice."

Damian let the pause sit just long enough to count as courtesy. "I'll try to conceal my delicate nature."

Lang's mouth moved a fraction. "Bring the tariff draft. Holtz wants to see whether you can make a routine disagreement sound like national peril."

"He'll be glad to learn I can."

"He'll be glad to learn you're trainable." She stepped aside. "Thursday."

---

Demetrius had chosen the restaurant, which meant the Kaiserhof and the window table overlooking the ministry gardens, where half the room could see who had chosen it. Lunch with Demetrius was never just lunch. It was another way of putting the family in order.

He was already seated when Damian arrived, ten minutes early because Demetrius was always fifteen minutes early and would note the difference. His older brother wore a dark suit that fit too cleanly to ignore. His hair was combed in the same severe style their father had worn in ministry photographs. At twenty-four, Demetrius looked older than that. He had for years. The resemblance to Donovan was the kind of thing people mentioned in front of him as if it were praise. Demetrius treated it as praise because, for him, it was.

"You're early," Demetrius said.

"You're earlier."

"A call ran short. Kessler at Vanguard Defense. Procurement's being rearranged." He set down his water glass. "Sit."

The waiter brought menus. The Kaiserhof had taken every solid Ostanian staple and pushed it up into ministerial prices: soup clarified into consommé, sausages pressed into terrines, bread arriving with explanation. Damian ordered the schnitzel because it was the plainest thing on the menu, which only made it feel like a statement.

"Holtz says the meeting went well," Demetrius said.

"Holtz says that whenever I manage not to set the room on fire."

"He said you sounded like Father."

"He says that too."

"Because that comparison keeps you useful to him." Demetrius picked up the menu once more, though he was going to order the duck. He always ordered the duck. "Look, the party's fine with you at the moment. You show up, you do clean work, you carry the name. That's the minimum and you're meeting it. But Holtz told me you've been selective."

"Selective."

"Don't do that." Demetrius looked up. "Lang's working group. The security liaison committee. The rooms that matter once the speeches end. You keep finding reasons not to be in them."

"I've been focused on trade."

"Trade is a waiting room and you know it."

The waiter returned. Demetrius ordered the duck. Damian's schnitzel came with a scatter of microgreens that looked embarrassed to be there.

"I'll go to Lang's meeting on Thursday," Damian said.

"Go on Thursday. Go after Thursday. Stop making people ask if you're solid."

Damian set down his fork. "Kessler asked that?"

"Kessler asked whether your position on national defense was real. I said yes."

"So that's it. I sit in Lang's little war room and smile while Kessler counts his percentages—"

"Don't." Demetrius's knife stopped halfway through the duck. "If you want to moralize, do it somewhere without waiters."

Damian looked past him, out at the gardens. "I wasn't moralizing."

"Then what were you doing?"

"Thinking out loud."

"Try not to." Demetrius cut a piece of duck and chewed. "The defense portfolio is where this family still has weight. That means contractors, committee chairs, people in uniform, people adjacent to people in uniform. You don't have to like that. You do have to understand it."

"I understand it."

"No. You understand how it's supposed to work." He set down the fork. "I'm talking about the actual — which deputy minister returns whose call. Which briefing paper gets read because a Desmond handed it over. Those are errands, Damian. Father did them. I do them. You keep acting like there's a version of this where you get the results without doing the errands."

"Maybe I'm being careful."

"Then be careful where nobody can see it." Demetrius took a sip of water. "Nobody in that room needs to know what you think, Damian. They need to see you in the chair when it matters."

"And if I haven't decided?"

Demetrius gave him a flat look. "Then decide. Before somebody decides for you."

The duck was lacquered with a reduction Damian couldn't identify. His schnitzel was fine. The acid in his throat annoyed him.

"You sound like Father," he said.

"For God's sake." Demetrius exhaled through his nose. "Not everything is Father. Though yes, he'd agree with me." He leaned back a fraction. "He asked about you."

"What did you tell him?"

"That you're doing well. That the party's pleased enough. That the hesitation is temporary."

Damian set his water glass down. "You told him I was hesitating."

"I told him you were thinking too hard. He considers that a form of hesitation." Demetrius picked up his fork again. "I'm trying to keep people from deciding you're unreliable before you've even — look, there's a difference."

"You always did like speaking for me."

"Yeah. Someone had to."

Demetrius did not look away. Neither did Damian.

"I'm not — look, I'm not going to embarrass the family," Damian said.

"No," Demetrius said after a beat. "I don't think you will."

They spoke about smaller things after that. A cousin's wedding. The ministry gardens, on which Demetrius had opinions. Their father's view that modern Ostanian literature had mistaken confusion for depth. Damian listened to his brother's clipped certainty and tried, not for the first time, to find the point where the work had stopped being a means and become the thing Demetrius actually cared about: the calls returned, the names remembered, the right man seated at the right table. He couldn't find it.

They split the bill because Demetrius insisted on splitting bills, a gesture toward equality that convinced no one. Outside the Kaiserhof, with the autumn air sharp on his face, Demetrius shook his hand as if they had just concluded business.

"Thursday," Demetrius said. "Lang's working group. I'll tell her you're coming."

"Fine."

"And call Father. He listens to your voice longer than mine."

To Damian, that was as close to vulnerability as Demetrius got: the sideways admission that Donovan preferred the son who questioned to the son who complied. Damian had never known whether Demetrius resented that or tucked it into the same mental drawer as vote counts, donor names, and every other fact that might matter later.

"I'll call him tonight," Damian said.

Demetrius nodded and walked toward the car already waiting for him, its engine running, its driver opening the door before he reached it. He moved with the grim efficiency that always made Damian feel as if the day had been arranged in advance. Damian stood on the pavement in his cheaper suit and watched him go, thinking of Kessler, Thursday, and how every attempt at independence seemed to end in another waiting room where somebody already knew whose son he was.

---

Grosz lived in a third-floor apartment in the Schiller district, east of the government quarter and far enough out that a retired civil servant could afford it without explaining himself. The stairwell smelled like boiled vegetables and floor wax. A cat watched Damian from the second-floor landing as if he were already late.

He knocked twice, then once, then twice again, the old pattern from his father's network, unchanged in thirty years because the people who used it kept old habits long after they stopped being sensible. The door opened on a chain. One eye, watery and sharp, examined him through the gap.

"Herr Grosz. It's Damian."

"I can see that." The chain rattled. "You look like your mother. Come in."

The apartment was small and meticulously kept. Damian took in the folded newspaper, magnifying glass, toast crumbs, and the bookshelves lined with Ostanian political history, military memoirs, and more novels than he would have expected. On the mantel stood three framed photographs: Grosz in a ministerial corridor, younger and straighter; Grosz shaking hands with a man Damian did not recognize; Grosz behind Donovan Desmond at what looked like a state function, both of them wearing the expression Damian had seen in old ministry photographs when men still believed they were building a permanent order.

Grosz himself was seventy-three, thin in the way of men who had once been vigorous and were now just wiry, with liver-spotted hands and a collar buttoned to the throat despite the warmth of the room. He moved carefully but not slowly. A cane leaned beside the armchair, though he did not touch it while Damian was looking.

"Sit. I'll make coffee. Or I have schnapps, if you want to disappoint yourself."

"Coffee."

"Your father would have taken the schnapps."

"My father was better at pretending not to be drunk."

That got the corner of Grosz's mouth to move. "Better at pretending, yes."

He disappeared into the kitchen. Water ran. A kettle clicked. Damian sat in the armchair that did not have the cane beside it and waited. The room had the stale quiet of a place used to only one voice. Traffic filtered up from the street, a bus, a horn, metal dragged over concrete. Inside the apartment, each sound seemed to hang around too long.

Grosz returned with two cups on saucers, the porcelain heavy and old-fashioned, blue flowers worn thin at the rim. He lowered himself into the other armchair.

"You said you had questions about the current situation." He set his cup down. "I assumed you didn't mean tariffs."

"Not tariffs. The Renewal Front. And Vanguard Defense."

Grosz went still so fast Damian saw the fear before the old man had time to arrange his face.

"Who said Vanguard?"

"Demetrius. At lunch. Kessler's name came with it."

Grosz looked toward the hall, then toward the door, listening with his whole head. "Keep your voice down."

"You know them?"

"I knew paperwork that led in their direction. That's not the same thing." He picked up his cup, put it back down untouched. "What else do you have?"

Damian held his gaze. "Holtz wants me drafting trade language as a security issue. Lang wants me in the defense working group on Thursday. Kessler is leaning on Demetrius about my reliability. Somewhere in that I started hearing the words Renewal Front from people who used to know better than to say things in front of me." He paused. "That's what I have."

Grosz studied him for a long moment. "All right," he said. "That's enough to talk."

He stood with a suppressed wince, crossed to a narrow sideboard, and opened the bottom drawer. From the back he took a thin file jacket that looked older than Damian. Inside was a single onion-skin sheet, yellowed, the carbon copy faint but legible.

"Don't touch the middle. It smears."

Damian leaned forward. Across the top of the page, in faded typed capitals, was MAINTENANCE CONTINUITY ANNEX. Below that: a ministry seal, a contract number, and the name VANGUARD DEFENSE in pale, stubborn ink. Four line items sat under the header. The third and fourth were for the same northern depot, the same month, and almost the same wording. The amounts matched exactly: 31,400 dinars each.

"Read the bottom two lines," Grosz said.

Damian did. The third billed for relay-seal replacement on a border communications site. The fourth billed a continuity retention reserve for the same site, routed through Klee Civic Advisory.

He looked up. "They charged for the same work twice."

"Close." Grosz lowered himself back into the chair. "They charged for the parts once and the men twice. Klee had a brass plaque, one rented office, no mechanics, no tools. It existed so the second payment could leave the ministry books wearing a different hat."

Damian looked back down. Same depot. Same month. Same amount. The fraud was lazy enough to feel practiced. "So the annex proves a skim."

"It proves money was being moved off the clean ledger." Grosz tapped the page with one finger. "What that money bought is where I start inferring instead of knowing. I want you clear on that."

"Fine. What do you know?"

"I know more annexes like that kept showing up. And I know officers, contractors, old party men who had no business being in the same building started — I'd see them at the same side tables. Same receptions. People who shouldn't have been in the same room." He rubbed his mouth. "What I think — and I'm calling it what I think, not what I can prove — is that somebody needed those people available to each other. No minutes, no audits."

"The Renewal Front."

Grosz made a face. "That's what people call it. Sure. But some of them wanted a war. Some of them wanted the budget that comes with promising a war. And some of them were just skimming and calling it service." His shoulders shifted under his shirt. "It isn't one thing. I need you to understand that."

"How many annexes did you see?"

"I stopped — enough. More than a few." He picked at the saucer rim. "Enough that it wasn't one lazy accountant."

"That's not a number."

"No." A pause. "No, it isn't."

The apartment buzzer rasped hard enough to make both cups jump in their saucers.

Grosz muttered, "Damn it," folded the onion-skin page in half, and shoved it back into the jacket. "Quiet."

He crossed to the door without the cane, which was how Damian knew he was frightened. He put one eye to the peephole and held up two fingers without looking back. Wait.

Someone's shoes stopped outside the apartment. The shadow under the door thickened, then shifted. A woman downstairs started complaining about post that had been left in the wrong box. Another apartment buzzed in answer. The shoes moved on.

Grosz stayed at the door long enough to count as habit, not paranoia, then returned and slid the file jacket under a cushion instead of back onto the table.

"This is why you don't come here twice in one week," he said.

Damian kept his own voice low. "You think that was for you?"

"I think old men who keep papers should assume every hallway is for them." Grosz sat again, slower this time. "Now ask one useful question and then leave."

"All right. Thursday. Lang's room. What am I looking for besides duplicate charges?"

"Supplement numbers, if they come up. Emergency retention codes — those are the budget lines where the money moves." He thought for a moment. "Watch who talks to the contractors directly. Who skips the staff. And who leaves the room when numbers get specific." He exhaled through his nose. "And don't — look, not everyone in that room is part of it. Lang might just like being important. Half the damage in this country comes from people who aren't even conspiring, they're just comfortable."

"And Voss?"

Grosz's mouth tightened. "Voss is not on that page. I want to be clear about that."

Damian waited.

"His name kept — I kept hearing it. Around the same people, in the same rooms. Enough times that I stopped telling myself it was coincidence." He shifted in the chair. "I'm not saying he ran anything. I'm saying that when a conversation turned from budgets to grievance, when somebody started talking about how peace had made the country weak — Voss was there, or he'd just left, or somebody had just come from talking to him."

"The phrase peace is theft."

"Yes." Grosz's eyes flicked toward the door again. "His, maybe. Or maybe somebody said it and it stuck to him. I never heard it from his mouth directly."

"This is —" Damian stopped. Started over. "Why tell me this now?"

Grosz was quiet for a moment. "Because you came here with Kessler and Vanguard in the same sentence. That's different from the other times." His voice roughened. "And because Kessler's name being in your brother's mouth the same day it's in mine — I don't like that. I've probably said too much already."

"You think this conversation puts you at risk?"

"I think I'm seventy-three and I live alone in a building with one stairwell." He looked at Damian without blinking. "So, yes."

Damian nodded.

"Don't come back here to tell me what you find," Grosz said. "Don't come back at all. Not next week, not the week after. Not unless you have proof in your hand that changes everything." He leaned forward. "Thursday you go to Lang's meeting. You sit, you listen. If annex numbers come up, write them down afterward, not in the room. Watch who talks to whom when procurement gets specific. Then you leave looking like a Desmond son who showed up to do what Desmond sons do."

"And if they notice what I'm doing?"

Grosz looked at him for a moment. "Then you'd better hope they noticed too late." His eyes lifted to the photograph of Donovan on the mantel. "Your father never did any of this alone. He had people in hallways, cars downstairs, staff who knew which file to lose if somebody came asking. You don't have any of that. Keep that in mind."

Damian let the paper sit where it was. "You were loyal to him."

"I was loyal to your father, yes." Grosz leaned back carefully. "I'm still — I suppose I'm still loyal to what I thought we were building. You give your career to a ministry and you stay loyal because otherwise what were the years for?" He rubbed his knee. "But that's not the same as wanting them to use you up the way they used me."

The steps in the stairwell were gone. The apartment had gone quiet again. Damian stood.

"I'll take the contract number," he said.

"You'll memorize it." Grosz slid the page back into the file jacket. "Nothing leaves this apartment."

Damian read the number twice, then a third time.

He was at the door before he turned back. "One more thing."

Grosz closed his eyes for a moment. "You people always say that like it won't be the costly part."

"Does this stay inside Ostania?" Damian asked. "The money. The buyers. Any of it."

For the first time since opening the file, Grosz looked genuinely tired.

"I saw one routing sheet before I retired," he said. "One. Threat assessments moving through a broker to a Westalian-owned procurement firm. Not stolen. Sold."

Damian stared at him. "By whom?"

"If I knew that, I wouldn't be sitting in this apartment with a bad radiator." Grosz's voice had gone flat. "I don't know if that channel still exists. I don't know if Vanguard was part of it. I don't know if the same people handled both sides. What I know is one sheet crossed my desk and I never found a clean reason for it."

Westalian money. Ostanian grievance. The same old appetite on both sides of the border.

"Good," Grosz said. "Now go before I decide I've lost my mind."

---

The walk home took twelve minutes, east along Friedrichstrasse and then north toward his building. The streetlights were coming on. Leaves had pasted themselves into the gutters. The cold got through the cloth of his coat faster than it had last week and stayed there.

He kept hearing four things. Kessler. Voss. Westalian money. Maintenance continuity annex.

He would have to write the tariff paper before evening and then attend Lang's working group on Thursday pretending Demetrius had won. He would have to sit through defense men talking budget and posture and readiness while trying to catch contract numbers without looking like he was catching contract numbers. If he did it badly, the suspicion would not stop with him. It would travel backward to an old man in a third-floor apartment with a cane by the chair and no one else in the room.

Damian's apartment was on the fourth floor of a building that had once been elegant and was now merely respectable, which suited the story he told about himself. Modest enough to signal independence. Well-located enough to remind anyone paying attention that the independence was elective.

He let himself in, hung up his coat, and turned on the kitchen light. He should have drafted the counter-position paper. He should have written down the contract number while it was still clean in his head, then burned the page. Instead he stood at the counter for a full ten seconds, looking at the telephone, and picked it up.

Not calling would make it back to Demetrius by breakfast anyway.

The house line rang twice. A secretary answered on the third ring and put him through after a pause just long enough to remind him that even in his father's home there were gates inside gates.

"Damian."

His father's voice had not softened with age. It was still dry and level, and Damian still found himself straightening under it before he could stop.

"Father."

"Demetrius said you were calling."

"Then at least one branch of the family communicates efficiently."

A breath crossed the line, close enough to amusement that Damian resented it. "How did Holtz take the room?"

"He left believing I think tariffs are a security emergency."

"Good."

Damian stared at the black window over the sink. His own reflection looked thin in the glass. "Lang wants me in her working group on Thursday."

"She should have wanted you months ago."

"Trade kept me occupied."

"Trade keeps young men occupied. Security decides who matters." Paper shifted on the other end, another conversation maybe waiting just beyond the receiver. "Will you be there?"

"Yes."

"Then be there properly. Don't stand in a corner looking civilized while other men do the ugly part."

Kessler's name rose in Damian's throat and stopped there. Grosz had said keep your voice down, and somehow the warning had followed him into a different building. "And if the ugly part is the point?"

His father was quiet long enough for Damian to picture him weighing whether the question deserved an answer.

"Then learn who is paying for it," Donovan said. "Don't confuse disgust with strategy."

There it was, the familiar weight of the family method, interested in usefulness before anything else. He could hear how much of it had shaped him before he'd been old enough to refuse it.

"I'll keep that in mind."

"Do. And write the tariff paper before midnight. Holtz likes quick sons." Another beat. Softer, brief enough to doubt. "You sound tired."

"It's been a long day."

"They all are."

The line went dead.

Damian stood with the receiver in his hand until the dial tone flattened out. Then he set it down, opened the hall closet, and pulled the cardboard box from the top shelf.

It was the only box he had brought from the family house. Everything else in the apartment had been bought new, a deliberate break from the curated weight of the Desmond household. This box he had never unpacked and never thrown away.

Inside were school things. An Eden Academy report card with marks that were good but not exceptional, which had been a particular kind of failure in his father's house. A certificate for the inter-class spelling competition, second place. A ribbon from athletics day. He had run the four-hundred-meter badly and finished fourth, and the ribbon said PARTICIPANT in silver letters, the kind of consolation Eden preferred to dress up as dignity. A folded program from the school festival, his name listed as part of the gymnasium cleanup crew. A pen that had run dry years ago. At the bottom, under tissue paper that his mother had probably put there before she got sick, a photograph.

Class photograph. Eden Academy, third year. Four rows of students on the gymnasium steps, faces arranged into the tense stillness children gave a camera after one warning too many. Damian found himself in the third row, second from the right, looking furious.

In the second row, between Becky Blackbell and a boy he couldn't place, a girl with bright pink hair and a crooked tie grinned at someone just outside the frame.

He looked at her longer than he meant to, then turned the photograph over. On the back, in his mother's tidy blue hand, the rows had been labeled years ago. A damp brown ring had eaten through the middle of the second row. Becky Blackbell's name was still clear at the edge of the stain. The next name had washed into one capital A and a tail of blue.


# Chapter 8

The noise started in the elevator.

She had spent the morning at WISE with the International Conference Center file open under her hand, matching Bond's burning terrace to public summit schedules and renovation notices until the place in his vision turned into a real line on paper. That was all she'd managed: the ICC was real, the upper terrace was real, and the summit still sat on its calendar without giving her a date for the fire. Strauss had dropped the Concordia invitation on her desk after lunch and called the reception useful overlap. It hadn't replaced the ICC lead. It had cut across it. The copied schedules were still folded inside the satchel at her hip.

It did not come from the machinery. The Concordia's elevators glided without a sound, part of what people were paying for. The man beside Anya said nothing. He had a leather portfolio, a neutral face, and an expensive tie. His thoughts, unfortunately, were having a full panic spiral.

*Left the gas on. No. Did I? I turned the knob.*
*I'm almost sure I turned the knob.*
*If the building burns they'll find the Ackermann notes on my counter and think I wrote a suicide note. Christ.*

Anya adjusted her earring and watched the floor numbers climb.

Tonight was going to be loud.

The elevator opened onto the third-floor lobby. Beyond the double doors, the Westalian trade delegation's autumn reception was already underway. She could hear the quartet through the wood, then glassware, then shoes on polished floor. Under all of that ran the other layer.

*If he says grain tariff one more time I'm leaving.*
*Nice shoes. Who is she?*
*Where is the shrimp. They said seven.*
*Maria's going to assume the worst. Again.*

Two hundred minds, maybe more, all stacked on top of each other. The gas man was right behind her, still thinking about the stove. Before she could step into the room, a side door opened off the service corridor and Damian came through it with a paper folder under one arm and Thursday still all over his face.

He had changed ties between Petra Lang's working group and the reception. The rest of him had not recovered. His jaw was set too hard. The diplomatic smile was present in outline only.

"You look terrible," Anya said.

"That's generous." He came to a stop beside her without quite standing beside her, close enough to talk and far enough to deny later. "Lang's meeting happened."

"And?"

"And I'd like to know why a correction to the Vanguard annexes reached Ackermann's customs people before Petra Lang admitted she'd made one."

That cut through the room noise cleanly. "You got the annex numbers?"

"Most of them. Not the first version."

"What was missing from the correction?"

"Two shipping lines and one staffing note." His fingers tightened once on the folder, then eased. "Lang called it routine cleanup. Nobody at that table believed her enough to look relaxed."

"Voss?"

"Didn't attend. Brandt did. Ackermann did. Dietrich drifted through after and kept nudging everything back to timing as if he'd taken a wrong door and stayed for the port policy."

"That's not suspicious at all."

"No, it's charming." Damian glanced at the ballroom doors. Somebody laughed inside. Somebody else thought about cholesterol with total despair. "One more thing. The word review kept coming up."

"What kind of review?"

"That's the problem. At least three kinds." His voice stayed mild, but the irritation underneath had edges. "Lang means committee review. Ackermann means the checkpoint review that decides whether his people can move the corrected freight line. Brandt hears that and thinks November signatures slip and Voss starts asking where they went. One of the Westalian trade people is using review for oversight on his own budget tables. Same word, three different knives."

Anya let that settle. Inside the room, a server hurried by with a tray of sparkling wine and thought only about not dropping it in front of the ambassador again. A woman near the entrance was trying not to think about her heel blister and therefore thinking about nothing else.

"What do you want from me?" she asked.

"If Ackermann, Dietrich, or Brandt turn up in your range, listen for anything that links the annex corrections to the reception. If you hear review attached to schedules or staffing, remember exactly who said it."

She looked at the folder. "You wrote it down."

"I wrote down what people said out loud. That's not the same thing." His gaze flicked to hers for a beat too long, enough to register the ask underneath. He needed what she did in rooms: observation, intuition, impossible pattern recognition, whatever careful lie both of them preferred on weekdays. "I have the south end and Lang. You take the east and bar side. We compare later."

"You're giving me the side with Ackermann, security, and alcohol."

"I know your talents."

"That sounded like an insult."

"You hear insult because you assume the worst of me."

"I assume the accurate amount."

Something almost softened in his mouth. Then his thoughts cut in around the edges of his calm face.

*If she can read this room the way she reads every room, she'll hear what I missed.*
*If Ackermann moved the paper before Lang spoke, somebody wanted timing more than policy.*
*And if this turns out to be nothing I spent an afternoon writing tariff language for men I can't stand.*

"Go be respectable," Anya said.

"You too."

They went in by different doors.

The Concordia announced itself through logistics before decoration: terrace doors at the west end, a service corridor at the east, hotel staff doubled into security near both lanes, and Yuri Briar holding the cleanest sightline in the room. The quartet existed somewhere behind the crowd. Westalians had already claimed the bar.

Anya took a glass of white wine from a passing tray because it gave her hands a job and made her look less like she was counting the room. She started moving.

A square little man with customs cuff links and a wet upper lip stood near the piano pretending to care about museum lending rates. *If Ackermann asks for annex four again, tell him Lang moved it to review. If he asks where review, say committee. If he asks which committee, laugh.*

Anya drifted another step and caught a different voice, this one from a woman with a trade badge and a fixed social smile.

*Checkpoint schedule holds if the review doesn't interfere. That's all. That's the line. Hold the line.*

At the buffet, an Ostanian general with enough medals to plate a second jacket was thinking about shrimp with the focus of a man inspecting artillery. *Tail came off clean. Good kitchen. Sauce is wrong. Too much lemon. Not enough bite.*

Strauss from WISE stood by a pillar with a gin and tonic and the face of a man who resented both halves of it. *Halverson cut the ugly columns again. Not cut. Shaped. That's the polite word. Oversight gets the shaped set. Everybody at the desk knows it. Nobody says it where minutes get kept.*

Across the room Petra Lang had Damian pinned beside a floral arrangement so expensive it had to be state-sponsored.

Lang was silver-haired, broad through the shoulders, and built like a woman who had spent twenty years making men confuse bluntness with permission. Out loud she was talking pragmatic tariff realism. Inwardly she was nowhere near tariffs.

*Smile at the boy. Keep him warm. Ackermann leaves in twenty. After that it doesn't matter whether Desmond sulks.*
*Who gave Brandt the first copy. Idiots. If she says annex in this room I will bury her.*

Damian's face gave nothing. Damian's thoughts were cleaner.

*You changed the sheet after the room sat down.*
*And now you're quoting my paper back to me like I should thank you.*
*Go on. Mention national stability one more time.*

Yuri Briar stood watch at the east side of the ballroom.

He wore a dark suit with an earpiece almost invisible against his hair. Anybody who'd never worked security would have called his posture relaxed. Anybody who had would have seen the readiness in it. His weight stayed balanced, his hands loose, and his eyes made the same sweep every eight seconds. Anya counted once to be sure.

His thoughts cut through the room with familiar sharpness.

*West entrance clear. Kraus has kitchen corridor.*
*Three extra Westalian staff not on the advance sheet. Logged it. Don't like it.*
*Three is planning, not sloppiness.*
*There. Cultural attaché. Third government room this month.*
*That isn't culture. That's circuit work.*
*Sis would tell me to stop being suspicious. Sis is not on security detail.*

Cold moved through her stomach. His eyes were on her now, not warmly, not as family by marriage, but as pattern recognition.

Anya slipped into the nearest conversation knot, all bilateral arts funding, ceramics residencies, and exchange fellows. She kept her face interested and her shoulders loose. Nobody in that circle cared whether the Ostenheim program was oversubscribed, which helped.

Near the dais, the ambassador's wife tapped a spoon against her glass for a toast. The room leaned toward her in polite waves. Chairs stayed put, but bodies moved. Clusters compressed, and the thought-noise went from crowded to layered.

*Smile now.*
*Please don't let him mention wheat figures.*
*If I miss the tram this ends in a taxi and my wife is going to kill me.*
*Ackermann is leaving too early.*

The toast itself was nothing but friendship, exchange, stability, autumn light across two nations, and other expensive lies. The useful part lived underneath it.

*Brandt can wait until Monday if the checkpoint review slips.*
*No. Monday's too late.*
*Keep him away from Dietrich until after the revised tables go through.*

Anya stood in the middle of polite applause and felt the shape of the evening change under her feet. The word Damian had handed her downstairs kept surfacing, but it would not hold still. Committee review, checkpoint review, oversight review, maybe more. The syllables stayed the same, but the rooms inside them kept changing.

Quadrants helped: upper left, upper right, lower left, lower right. A room this big could drown her if she let it turn into one blur. She read, sorted, moved, and kept moving. Cultural attachés were allowed to mingle. They were not allowed to haunt.

She worked east first because Damian had asked and because Yuri already thought she was a problem there.

Ackermann stood near a marble pillar talking to a red-faced shipping man and Dietrich, who looked exactly like the sort of person who would use the word synergy as a threat. Out loud they were discussing cross-border warehousing. Inwardly they were doing something else entirely.

Ackermann: *Do not say checkpoint. Not here.*

Dietrich: *If Brandt wanders over I will break both his hands and call it diplomacy.*

Shipping man: *Smile. Nodding means I understand. I do not understand.*

Anya took a canapé she didn't want and stepped into the edge of their orbit just long enough to matter.

"My office keeps hearing from museum couriers that customs delays are worse on the north route," she said to nobody in particular. "I was wondering if that's seasonal or just bad luck."

The shipping man brightened because he thought she'd offered him an exit. "Transit always worsens in autumn."

Ackermann smiled at her with the particular blank warmth senior officials reserved for harmless young women. "Paperwork worsens in every season, I'm afraid."

*Who invited her.*
*Westalian. Fine. Harmless.*
*If Dietrich says one stupid thing I will have to smile through it.*

"Arts shipping has its own little catastrophes," Anya said.

Dietrich gave her a look like he'd ranked her at once and found the result uninteresting. "Everything has its catastrophes."

His thoughts were less composed.

*Everything has its schedule too. Friday, if review closes. Monday if it doesn't. Not here. Not here.*

She almost stilled. Friday what. Monday what. Before she could edge closer, someone else joined the cluster and dragged the conversation sideways into port fees. The useful thought line vanished under irritation, shoes, hunger, boredom.

Anya stayed another thirty seconds to avoid looking like she had come for anything particular, then peeled away toward the windows with the sense of having seen a thread disappear back into cloth.

A military aide near the curtains was consumed by the question of whether he'd parked in a tow zone.

One junior diplomat within range was thinking with total spiritual commitment about whether his fly was down.

It was.

There was no professional way to solve that. Anya left him to history and kept walking.

Brandt was, unfortunately, real enough. He stood half-hidden by a potted palm with two fisheries men and the face of someone being forced to discuss weather with enemies. Out loud he was dry and competent. Inwardly he was clogged with scheduling.

*If Ackermann says the review holds, the November signature moves.*
*If the November signature moves, Voss asks questions.*
*I do not want Voss asking questions.*

There it was. Not proof, but a shape.

Anya cut behind a server and nearly walked straight into Yuri.

He caught the tray she clipped before the glasses could fall, efficient and without fuss. The server muttered thanks and fled. Yuri set the tray back into the man's hands and turned to her with a professional expression so neutral it took effort.

"Ms. Kessler."

Anya swallowed a pulse spike. "Officer Briar."

"You remember me."

"You're hard to miss."

"That's generally the point." His tone stayed courteous. His thoughts did not. *She moved toward Ackermann. Then Brandt. Not random. Not social.* "Enjoying the reception?"

"Enjoying is a strong word."

"Work, then."

"Culture is work."

"This many times a month?"

There it was: not an accusation, but a pattern laid on the table like silverware.

Anya sipped her water because it bought half a second. "Trade season gets everybody dragged into rooms they'd rather avoid."

"Mm."

*Bad answer. Too ready.*
*She knew my name before I offered it.*
*We've met enough for that. Doesn't help.*

He let the silence hold long enough to be intentional, not rude and not friendly either.

"Stay clear of the east service corridor after eight-thirty," he said. "We're rotating staff and I don't want anybody wandering into the wrong lane."

"Good to know."

"And if anyone asks, you heard it from hotel management."

"That's very generous of hotel management."

One corner of his mouth moved, not quite amusement, more like professional acknowledgment that she had seen the cover and understood it.

"Enjoy the shrimp," he said.

Then he turned away.

Anya stood where he left her and did not look after him. Her skin had gone too tight across her shoulders. Yuri's suspicion had crossed from ambient thought to spoken behavior now. It was still deniable, still fixable, and still real.

Across the room Damian looked up from the defense liaison and caught Yuri walking away from her. His thought flashed clean through the crowd.

*What did he want.*
*Not now. Keep smiling.*

Anya gave the smallest shrug she could manage. Damian's attention snapped back into diplomatic mode before anybody else noticed it had moved.

She worked the west side for ten minutes because east had become expensive. An academic spent five solid minutes thinking about a dead ficus in her office. A banking adviser wanted desperately to leave his wife and even more desperately not to lose her apartment in the divorce. Someone near the bar was planning to steal monogrammed napkins for reasons too bleak to examine.

Then one thought made her stop by a lacquered side table.

*Do not discuss procurement numbers at functions, Dietrich, for God's sake.*

It came from a narrow woman in a navy dress speaking to nobody she liked. Her mouth was still smiling at the ambassador's cousin. Her mind was on the east cluster and the exact kind of stupid that ruined men.

Anya turned her head just enough to see Dietrich again, now with Ackermann and a customs man and one more face she didn't know. The quartet cut into something brighter. Laughter rose near the terrace. The room kept pretending it was a party.

It had only been forty minutes since Damian came out of Lang's meeting downstairs. Already she had annex corrections, checkpoint timing, shaped WISE numbers, procurement nerves, and Yuri asking why culture kept putting her next to the wrong people. The evening had stopped feeling broad and started feeling arranged.

* * *

Damian was near the terrace doors again, listening to a woman from the Foreign Affairs Ministry explain port logistics with real interest. That should not have been attractive in anybody. On him it was still weirdly effective.

*If they fix the northern bottleneck the border looks calmer. If the border looks calmer the ministry loses its excuse for the defense allocation.*

Even his thoughts sounded like briefing notes that had gone to school.

Anya waited until the woman excused herself.

"If port logistics ever seduce you away from me, I'd like advance warning," she said.

"I'll try to be humane."

"That'd be new."

"You were talking to Yuri."

He went straight to it. His face stayed pleasant. His eyes did not. Anya read the question sitting behind them before he said it.

*Did he make her.*
*No. If he'd made her he'd still be here.*
*Don't overreact in public, idiot.*

"He warned me away from a service corridor," she said.

"That's almost sweet."

"His internal review was less sweet."

That got a tiny tightening at Damian's jaw. "He's clocking patterns?"

"Third room this month."

"Annoying."

"For me, yes."

For a second they stood shoulder to shoulder like two mildly bored reception professionals discussing traffic. Underneath, the pressure between them was doing something else.

"Anything else?" Damian asked.

"Brandt cares about November signatures and whether Voss starts asking questions if something slips. Dietrich is waiting on Friday or Monday depending on a review he didn't define. Ackermann's customs man has rehearsed a lie about annex four."

"And Strauss is still shaping numbers for oversight."

"Good." Then, quieter: "Too good."

"That's what I said about Yuri."

He ignored that on purpose. *Friday or Monday. So the schedule isn't stable. Good. No. Bad. Useful. Bad.* "Lang will be at the west side after the next toast. Ackermann keeps orbiting east. If there's a handoff tonight, it uses the crowd between."

"You're optimistic."

"I prefer irritated."

They both looked at the same movement at once: Dietrich cutting across the floor with the half-speed patience of a man who believed people would always open around him. Behind him, Hesse was talking educational exchange with an Ostanian academic near the west pillar.
