Lentz, the ceramics program officer, hovered one cluster over with the fixed hopeful look of someone who still believed kiln temperatures could interest strangers.

Anya drifted toward Hesse and Lentz because boring clusters made better cover than useful ones. A server squeezed past on his way to the west bar. She tucked her elbow tight against her ribs and tilted her head to save the glass without wearing half the tray.

Damian looked up from her like he'd been struck.

It was not the polite sweep he gave everybody. This was different: old memory, not present calculation.

*There. Elbow in. Head tilt.*
*I've seen that before. Where.*
*Eden. Festival cleanup. Henderson's shoes full of soap. Pink hair everywhere.*

*No. Wait. She used to tilt her head before saying the worst possible thing.*
*This woman just did it.*
*How many people in Berlint have that exact shade. No. Stop.*

Her name hovered at the edge of his mind, not in the thought yet, close enough to burn.

A hand landed on his arm.

The defense liaison had stopped avoiding him. Damian's focus snapped away hard enough that Anya felt the memory sink under the professional layer in real time: name, file, border posture, handshake, tone. The childhood image dropped straight through the floorboards.

She set the glass down and flattened her palm on the polished wood. The cool surface helped a little. Her pulse was hitting hard enough to make the stem rattle against her fingers.

It was too close. Three more heartbeats and school-Anya and attaché-Anya would have clicked together in his head. After that came the questions. Why was an Eden girl wearing Westalian credentials. Why did she keep appearing anywhere his covert life got dangerous. Why did she keep landing on the right answer half a beat before the rest of the room even saw the question.

Hesse was talking educational exchange with an Ostanian academic near the west pillar. Anya slid into the gap and waited through a long, stupid twenty-count for an opening.

"The preparatory courses help with that," she said when the academic mentioned networking. "I did the embassy exchange three years ago and I still keep almost recognizing people from it. Whole week of name tags, forced smiles, and stale conference-room air. Faces blur after that."

Hesse agreed. The academic agreed. Nobody cared, which was perfect.

Then Damian appeared at her shoulder with a fresh drink and the bland smile he used when he was absolutely running a probe.

"You did the preparatory exchange?" he asked.

Anya looked at him. "Spring intake."

"At the embassy annex?"

There it was. It was not casual. It was a probe.

Hesse was still nearby, thinking about the annex's leaky roof and the classroom urn that could strip paint. Good. Enough to grab.

"The annex for the seminars, the old ministry rooms for language drills," she said. "Hesse kept pretending the urn wasn't poison."

Damian's face did not change. His thoughts did.

*Fine. The annex. The stale room. Then why did I go chasing the annex off one stupid school memory. Why Henderson. Why that head tilt.*

"I was trying to place you," he said aloud.

"Everybody tries at these things," Anya said. "Do enough receptions and every face starts stealing pieces from the others."

"Fair."

He should have left it there. For one second Anya thought he would.

Instead he turned his head toward Hesse with the same mild expression.

"You were with that intake?" Damian asked. "Before the roof finally gave up?"

Hesse blinked.

It was a tiny thing, barely anything, but it was enough.

His mouth shaped a social smile half a beat late. His thoughts were sharper.

*What intake.*
*Roof? I was still in trade that year.*
*Do not look confused. You are too old to look confused at a party.*

Anya felt the air go thin.

Hesse let out a soft diplomatic sound that could have meant yes, no, or pass the salt. "The annex leaked for years. That hardly narrows it."

It was a bad answer, not because it was false but because it was protective.

Damian heard the wobble in it too. Anya saw him hear it.

She cut in before the silence could gather weight.

"He guest lectured once," she said, too fast and then making herself slow down. "Not my intake, I guess. That's why the room stuck harder than the actual teaching. Sorry. I merged two miserable memories together."

Lentz laughed politely because politeness was what she had. Hesse grabbed the offered bridge and walked across it.

"A professional hazard," he said. "Government buildings do blur."

His thoughts were a mess of gratitude and irritation.

*Bless the girl for fixing it.*
*Did I ever lecture there. Maybe once. No. Trade, then transfer. Whatever. Smile and keep it moving.*

Damian looked at her for one beat more than he needed to.

*He didn't know her.*
*Or he didn't know enough to say so.*
*Either way that story is checkable.*

There it was. It was not recognition. In its own way, it was worse.

Anya had not just dodged a memory. She had given him something concrete to keep.

"My apologies," Damian said to Hesse in the same public tone. "I've become one of those men who thinks every old room in Berlint is the same old room."

"A common disease," Hesse said, relieved to have a name for the awkwardness even if it wasn't the real one.

Damian raised his glass slightly to Anya. "I'll let you get back to ceramics."

"Thrilling subject," she said.

"I can tell."

He moved on.

Lentz resumed explaining kiln temperatures with patient enthusiasm. Anya nodded in the right places and heard almost none of it. Damian's thought stayed stuck under her skin:

*That story is checkable.*

Maybe not tonight, and not this minute. But the leverage had changed. Before, he had a feeling and an old photograph and the bad luck of seeing her everywhere. Now he had a witness reaction, a cover anecdote, and the knowledge that when pressed she could build an answer out of almost nothing and make it stand up in public.

This wasn't the same old almost. This one left residue.

From the east side of the room, Yuri tracked her again.

*Bar. Hesse. Ceramics officer. South window cluster.*
*She's not mingling. She's mapping.*
*And Desmond keeps crossing to her. Too much.*

No good answer existed for that. Anya did the only thing she had. She crossed back toward the bar and stayed put long enough to look boring.

That lasted ninety seconds.

Ackermann drifted into range again with Dietrich and the shipping man. Their spoken conversation had become lighter, which only made the thoughts underneath more obvious.

Ackermann: *Friday, then. If review closes Friday.*

Dietrich: *Unless Brandt loses his nerve.*

Shipping man: *I hate these people. I hate these people. I hate these people.*

Anya kept her face on her water glass and listened harder.

Ackermann: *No move until coverage shifts.*

Then somebody bumped her elbow and the sentence shattered into taxi anxiety, dry-cleaning, and a memory of bad oysters. She turned and caught only a woman apologizing with sincere horror.

"So sorry."

"You're fine," Anya said automatically.

It was not fine. By then the room had resolved into one dirty chain: Lang's correction, Ackermann and Dietrich waiting on some review clock, Brandt trying to keep Voss from smelling a delay, Strauss and Halverson shaping the WISE side of the paper, Yuri deciding she belonged in his notes. She still did not know what moved on Friday or Monday, only that it would move around people already watching her.

Then Yuri came back a second time, not to her but to a post two meters beyond her shoulder, and spoke quietly into his sleeve. Out loud it was logistics. In his head it was not.

*If Desmond keeps crossing to the west bar, log it.*
*And log the attaché. If she's in the room when the staff rotation changes, I want it noted.*

Anya set her glass down before she could crush it.

She was now in two separate men's working notes for two separate reasons and neither of them knew the real one.

* * *

By nine o'clock the ballroom had gone thick.

Three hundred people, maybe, enough that the thought-noise stopped sounding like separate minds and turned into pressure. The ache behind her right eye had deepened, her jaw tight, her shoulders tight, her fingers flexing around the water glass without permission. A waiter went past thinking in furious detail about three days of unwashed dishes and a pan of burned rice his roommate claimed was soaking. She told herself to keep moving and keep sorting.

A woman near the ambassador was dying inside because she'd realized too late that her dress matched the ambassador's wife's.

Brandt was still stalling the November deployment signature.

A man by the far curtain was thinking about his sick kid's fever and pretending to follow an energy conversation.

Lentz, poor Lentz, had circled back to whether hotel water would ruin her glaze test.

The room kept handing Anya proof that most private panics were private for a reason and useless to anyone but the person having them.

Then the clean one hit.

*Asset inside WISE is still holding. Move the handoff before review closes the window.*

Anya stopped.

She did not stop dramatically, just enough that the server behind her had to swerve.

The voice came again, sharp as a knife on tile.

*No delay. Coverage shifts after the review. Pull it forward if you have to.*

It was close, very close. Somebody inside this cluster of Westalian staffers, Ostanian counterparts, servers, security, and trailing spouses was within range and thinking about a Westalian asset and a handoff window like it was a timetable problem.

Anya turned once, slow. Twelve faces. Fourteen if the passing server counted. Nobody lit up with WISE. Another pass got her hotel booking, knee pain, one exhausted thought about the late tram, then only *handoff* breaking apart. Then nothing.

The phrase under her ribs stayed ugly anyway.

*Inside WISE.*

No name. No desk. No certainty about which review the thinker meant, or whether inside WISE meant inside the service or only inside the thinker's version of it. Surface thoughts were weather, not proof. She knew that better than most. But somebody close to handoffs and coverage shifts believed there was a usable asset in WISE. That was enough.

She tried the cluster once more. Hospital transfer, not betrayal. Torn stocking, not conspiracy. Broken-flute wages, not a mole. By the time Damian touched her elbow, the knot had already started to dissolve.

"You look pale," he said.

"Occupational hazard."

"From Yuri?"

"No."

He looked at her properly then.

*Something moved.*
*Not here. Don't ask here.*

"Did you get something?"

"Maybe."

That was too honest. "Or maybe the room finally turned everybody into the same person. Hard to tell."

His eyes narrowed. "That's not an answer."

"It's what I have."

He let that sit, saw the lie in it, and left it alone.

*Later, then.*
*No. Can't. Lang's still here. Damn it.*

"Don't do anything stupid," he said.

"That's rude."

"It's accurate."

He moved off toward Lang again. Annex numbers, checkpoint timing, delayed signatures, WISE. Same web.

She could not take *somebody near the bar thought there was an asset inside WISE* to Sylvia, Loid, or Damian and survive the first question. So the fragment stayed hers.

Tomorrow: review calendars, coverage shifts, everyone who could see both WISE paper flow and Damian's annex lane. Timing before identity.

She set the empty glass on a passing tray, took her coat from the attendant, and left with *still holding* scraping the inside of her ribs.


# Chapter 9

Across the street, movement caught her before thought did. A man at the tobacconist window looked into the glass instead of through it, using the reflection to keep Damian in frame. Another farther down the block matched Damian's pace without ever seeming to hurry.

For twenty minutes, Anya had kept Damian well ahead after he left the Foreign Affairs Ministry. Far enough not to trip his instincts. Far enough to tell herself the tail was protocol, just an operational partner keeping eyes on an ally in an unsecured environment. Partly true. The rest was simpler and worse: three days after catching *the asset inside WISE* at the reception, she couldn't leave his movements alone.

Then the route changed.

A traffic protest had clogged Lindenplatz, and ministry security had peeled off earlier than usual to reroute the cars. Pedestrians bunched along the west-side blocks where the street narrowed. Damian, still dragging the meeting around inside his head, got compressed into the same slow channel as everybody else. So did the two men shadowing him. So did she.

At the newsstand on the corner, she stopped to thumb open an evening paper and let the distance close. Fifteen meters, maybe a little less, close enough for the commuter smear to thin and the first clean thought to break through.

*Left side. Low approach. Maintain visual on package.*

A second mind clicked in from the curb opposite Damian. *He'll take the next turn. Intercept at the dark stretch.*

Package. That was military language, and wrong for both SSS and police. They weren't tracking a target or a subject. They meant to move him.

Only when Damian stepped into range did the edges of his thoughts brush hers: budget numbers still turning over, a line he wished he'd pushed harder on an undersecretary, the blunt headache of three hours spent with committee procedure. He had no idea.

The vendor was still grumbling about exact change when she dropped coins on the counter and left the paper open.

The first attacker was behind Damian by maybe five meters now. The second had started across the street, angling toward Schultzgasse, a narrow side street with bad lighting and locked residential doors. If Damian turned left the way he usually did, they'd take him in the dead stretch between the lamps.

She couldn't shout a name. Not her cover name for him, not the real one. Anything she put in the air within earshot of two professionals became theirs.

She ran.

Wet leaves made the cobblestones slick. Her flats skidded once and caught. Between a parked delivery truck and a stalled taxi, she cut into the street and kept them inside range by moving. One hand was already inside the first man's jacket. The second man had a metal tube half out of his pocket. Baton, most likely. Maybe a blade.

Damian turned left onto Schultzgasse.

*Now,* the first attacker thought. *Go.*

They came in from both ends. Anya hit the corner in time to see the shape of it: Damian in the middle, one man accelerating behind him, the other stepping out of a recessed doorway ahead. Coordinated. Professional. Rehearsed.

"Damian!"

He turned. The grab caught his shoulder instead of his neck, and that difference probably kept him alive. Damian stumbled into the wall. The second man snapped a telescoping baton open and came in fast.

Her elbow drove into his kidney, exactly where Yor had taught her to aim. Find the floating rib. Commit. Follow through. Yor would've taken the arm out of the socket after that. Anya wasn't Yor. He wrenched free, swung backhanded, and the baton cracked into her left side. White pain flashed across her vision and emptied her lungs in one violent rush.

His next thought came bright as a flare. *Step back. Reset. Take the throat.* She ducked the follow-up swing, drove her shoulder into his diaphragm, and took him down onto the wet stones with her. Forty kilos on her, maybe more. In half a second he was rolling, pinning her leg with his hip, hand reaching for her neck.

Damian smashed the other attacker across the head with his briefcase. It was a wild swing, no technique at all, but it landed. The man staggered. Damian hit him again. Leather split. Papers burst into the street and lifted in the cold air like startled birds before rain slapped them flat. Then the attacker caught Damian's arm and buried an elbow above his left eye. Skin opened. Blood sheeted down immediately.

*Knee.* Not a plan. Just the part of her brain that wanted him off. Her heel came down hard on the second attacker's kneecap. Cartilage or ligament gave with a wet crunch she felt through her whole leg. He screamed, rolled clear, and Anya scrambled up with one arm locked over her ribs. Standing all the way straight was out of the question.

"Get up," she said. "Move. Now."

The first man was already recalculating, circling back with that steady professional gait that meant the briefcase hit had only delayed him. His intent came through in one cold strip: *woman first. trained. man second.* Six seconds and he'd tagged her as the threat. That said enough about who they were dealing with.

Damian knelt in his own blood, one hand at his brow, the broken briefcase handle still clenched in the other. Anya hauled him upright. Behind them, one attacker was shaking his head clear. The other kept trying to stand on a leg that wouldn't take weight. Then the first thought flashed again. *Abort. Regroup. Secondary extraction.* She didn't wait to parse the rest. She dragged Damian toward the main street and ran.

He kept up. Blood ran into his left eye, and he wiped it away with his sleeve without breaking stride. Speech didn't arrive until two blocks later, when bright residential lamps replaced the bad dark of Schultzgasse and late pedestrians were still out with dogs and grocery bags.

A building wall caught Anya between the shoulder blades. She leaned on it and tried to pull air past the grinding pain under her ribs. A woman with a dachshund crossed the street rather than pass them, her thoughts spinning on repeat. *Blood. He's bleeding. Call someone. No, keep walking. Not your business.* Every inhale hurt in the same stripe. Not broken, probably. Badly bruised, definitely.

"What the hell," Damian said, then broke off, fighting for air. Blood kept tracking down from the split above his eye and onto his collar. "What the hell just happened?"

An ambush. That part was obvious. The uglier part was the precision. They had known which meeting, what time he would leave, which route he'd take. The session had been closed, ministry staff only. No public calendar. No open schedule.

*The asset inside WISE.*

"Not here," Anya said.

"Ministry security can trace the route change."

"If the route leak came through ministry security, that just tells them where you are now." She pushed off the wall before her side could stiffen any more. "I need to get you somewhere safe."

---

They kept moving west for fifteen minutes, long enough for the diplomatic quarter to fall away behind them.

A handkerchief appeared from Damian's coat pocket with the kind of automatic propriety that came from being raised in a house where pressed linen existed for every possible contingency, including ambush on a Tuesday. Anya kept one arm locked over her left side and her range open. Every mind coming toward them got checked. A woman thinking about her cat. A man drafting an angry text to a supervisor and deleting each version before he reached the next corner. A teenager listening to music loud enough that the bass leaked from his headphones and into his thoughts at the same time. Normal Berlint. Normal evening. Except for the two of them, moving too fast and leaving blood behind them in small, invisible increments.

The fallback apartment sat in a residential block in the west district, far from the diplomatic quarter and farther from government buildings. Anya had memorized the address during her first week in Berlint and had never used it. The site belonged to an old emergency tier that field agents learned on paper and analysts never saw unless someone activated it. Until that happened, it stayed dark.

The front door opened with a six-digit code she had to enter twice because her fingers wouldn't stop shaking. Third floor. No elevator. By the time they reached the apartment, each step sent a hot pulse from her ribs down into her hip.

The apartment offered the bare minimum: bare walls, a couch frame with a thin mattress instead of cushions, a kitchen counter, a sink, a bathroom, a closet. Dust had gathered in the corners. Inside the closet sat a first-aid kit, two changes of anonymous clothes, a prepaid phone, and a handgun she left where it was. There was no heat, and the place smelled only of stale air and old plaster. Through the uncurtained window, another building's television flickered blue-grey across the back courtyard.

Damian sat on the edge of the mattress and pressed the heel of his hand to his eyebrow. Blood seeped between his fingers. Combat-speed thoughts still ran under his skin. *Who were they. How did they know. Ministry calendar was internal. Was I watched earlier. Is she hurt worse than she's showing. Her hands are shaking. She's trying to hold them still and they are shaking.*

They were. She noticed when he did.

The first-aid kit had what it always had: gauze, saline, antiseptic wipes, butterfly strips, ibuprofen in foil. At the sink, her hands went under dish-soap-thin water that ran rust for two seconds and then cleared. The kit came back to the mattress with her.

"Let me see."

He moved his hand.

Two centimeters, maybe a little less. Deep enough to keep bleeding, not deep enough for stitches if she could get the edges to hold. Clean split. Precise hit.

Saline first. Gauze. He flinched at the antiseptic and stayed still anyway. By the time the second butterfly strip went on, the cut had stopped bleeding hard and settled into an angry seep at the margins. Warm skin under her fingers. Warmth in the cold apartment. His breath catching when it stung. None of that needed further analysis and none of it stayed out of her head.

"Your turn," he said.

"I'm fine."

"No, you're not."

"It's bruised."

"You're holding yourself like breathing is a bad idea."

That almost made her laugh. "Breathing is a bad idea."

There was no clean way to inspect bruised ribs without lifting her shirt, so she lifted it. A dark bar had already started to bloom across the left side of her torso, red at the edges, going purple by morning. Fingertips moved along the line of it. Nothing shifted. No grinding. No sharp instability. Just pain.

Damian looked once and then looked away too late. His thought got there before his face did. *That hit was meant for me.* Hair pushed back from his forehead with a blood-smeared hand. The rest of the thought broke itself off before it turned into apology.

"Ibuprofen," Anya said.

He passed the foil packet over. Three pills went down with tap water, rust first, then cold. By the time she sat again, the room had found its after-midnight noises. The pipes knocked. The building settled. Next door, a sportscaster called a football match with too much feeling. Under that, the neighbor's thoughts kept circling table money on the second half and the regret already forming.

"How did you know?" Damian asked.

Not quite a question. More like a problem set down between them. Blood dried on his fingers while he stared at them. *She was there before it started. Already in position. How does anyone do that unless--*

"I'm good at reading a bad street."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the one you're getting."

He looked up. The calculation ran in real time behind his face: press and risk the alliance, or let the lie stand and keep tonight intact. She watched the decision happen. He let it go.

"Okay," he said. A beat later he added, quieter, "Thank you. For all of it."

"Yeah."

Silence settled again, but not empty this time. Adrenaline was leaving in stages, like drainwater taking forever to clear a clogged sink. Cold came in behind it. So did exhaustion. So did the sudden vicious desire for home, for Yor's catastrophic cooking, for Bond on the rug, for Loid asking the wrong question in exactly the right tone.

"They knew your schedule," Anya said.

"I know."

"Walk me through it."

His mouth tightened. "The route wasn't even settled until the end of the meeting. There was a traffic protest on Lindenplatz, so security pushed us out the west side instead. Revised note went out at eighteen twelve."

"Who got it?"

"My office. Ministry security. Driver pool." He pressed the clean side of the handkerchief to his brow and thought past the pain. "And the Westalian liaison mailbox we use for movement alerts."

Anya sat up straighter despite what it did to her ribs. "The standard one?"

"WS-seven. Why?"

So it wasn't all of WISE, and it wasn't every analyst with database access either. Whoever had put men on Schultzgasse needed the ministry revision or the liaison traffic that mirrored it. That gave the leak a corridor, still too wide to feel useful but narrower than the whole institution. Her false report had already taught WISE to read Damian through the wrong lens. If the ambush had moved through a mailbox tied to his movements, then her lie was somewhere in that chain whether she wanted it there or not.

"Pen," she said.

He blinked. "What."

"Do you have one?"

A diplomat who left home without a pen probably got disowned. Damian reached into his inside coat pocket and handed one over without comment. Anya pulled the folded instruction sheet out of the first-aid kit and wrote on the blank side: `18:12 / west exit revision / WS-7`. The letters looked jagged. Good enough.

"What is that?" he asked.

"The first useful thing we've got."

A crease formed between his brows. "You think it came through that mailbox?"

"I think somebody with access to your revision moved fast enough to put two trained men in the dark before you got there. That narrows the field."

"To what?"

"Not enough." The paper got folded twice and pushed into her coat pocket. "But it's not nothing."

His eyes tracked to the closet. To the prepaid phone sitting on the shelf inside. "Can't you call this in?"

Anya got up, crossed to the closet, and picked the phone up anyway. Cheap plastic. Still wrapped in the kind of institutional dust that settled on objects nobody used. The battery was clipped in but not seated.

"One call on this activates the apartment," she said. "Duty officer logs the address, dispatch logs the time, somebody opens a file, and everybody with emergency access can see that this location is hot."

"And if your leak is inside emergency access."

"Then I hand them you alive and tell them where I hid you."

That landed. Damian sat very still for a second, handkerchief pressed to his brow, blood drying brown at the edge of it.

"Then don't call," he said.

The battery stayed in her hand. Practical. Immediate. Infuriating, because he was right and because she had known it before he said it.

"If I keep this place dark," Anya said, "it stays outside the active grid. That means no medic, no extraction team, and no report until I can move you somewhere else and send it from a line I trust."

"I said don't call."

"You don't get to order me around in my own safe house."

"Fine," he said, with the politeness he used when he was too tired to hide irritation. "I'm bleeding on your mattress and respectfully suggesting you don't notify the mole."

That almost did make her laugh.

The phone went back on the shelf. Battery beside it, not in it.

"First light," she said. "I move you first. Then I report."

He nodded once. Choice made. Cost attached.

She didn't trust the choice just because they'd made it. Anya crossed to the window and turned herself sideways, keeping out of the thin rectangle of light. The courtyard below held laundry lines, bicycle frames, and a woman on a back stoop smoking through a fight with her landlord in her head. No operational cadence. No one watching the building. At the door Anya listened to the stairwell. One floor up, a baby woke angry enough to rattle the walls. One floor down, two students argued about whether a goalkeeper could count as world-class with distribution that bad. Normal building noise. Normal minds. The absence of pursuit only made the line of her shoulders loosen by degrees.

"You do that every time?" Damian asked.

"Every time it matters."

His gaze followed the route of the sweep, then the cold room around them, as if recalculating what kind of life required habits like that.

For a while neither of them said much. The room felt different after the decision, tighter but clearer, as if refusing the call had given the danger edges instead of letting it spread everywhere at once. In the closet, the dead phone sat useless. In her pocket, the note pressed against her thigh.

Damian looked around the apartment again. Bare bulb. Rust-edged faucet. Mattress without sheets. A whole emergency life reduced to the minimum number of objects. *This is what safe means for her.* No pity in it. No disgust. Just the plain, startled recognition that the cold room was not an abstraction.

"Eden Academy had this thing," he said.

The turn was abrupt enough that Anya almost asked him to repeat it.

"After the dodgeball tournament. Losing team had to clean the gym. Floors, chairs, all of it." One finger touched the butterfly strips as if checking whether the wound was still there. "My team lost because a girl punched me."

Anya stared at him. "During dodgeball."

"During dodgeball."

"With her fist."

"With her actual fist. I was eight. It was humiliating."

A laugh threatened. She held it back. Barely.

"I'd said something about her hair," Damian went on. "I still can't remember the line, which is probably good for me, but I remember the punch exactly. Shoulder, not face. Very considerate."

Bubblegum. He had called it bubblegum. Full confidence, zero survival instinct. Her knuckles had hurt for an hour afterward and the detention had been worth every second.

"And then there was the school festival cleanup." His eyes stayed half-closed, voice drifting with exhaustion. "Poster paints everywhere. Blue all over the floor. There was this girl on the cleanup crew laughing so hard she kept sliding when she tried to mop." The thought underneath arrived less neatly. *Pink hair. Blue shoes. Same laugh. Same tilt of the head when--*

He opened his eyes fully and looked at her.

"You ever go to Eden?" he asked.

It came out quiet, almost casual. The thought behind it pressed harder. *Say no if you want. Just tell me why I know that laugh.*

Anya's stomach dropped. "No."

Too fast. He heard it. So did she.

*That's not right. Or I've got a concussion and I'm building a whole childhood ghost out of a laugh and a bad light.*

"You remind me of someone," he said.

"You've mentioned that."

"I know." Pain flared behind his eyes and broke the memory apart again before it could settle. He rubbed at his brow with the heel of his hand and exhaled through his nose. "Sorry. Getting hit in the head has apparently made me nostalgic."

That did it. The laugh got out.

Not the diplomatic one. Not the polished, cover-friendly version she used at receptions. The real one came up too loud for the room, sharp with exhaustion and relief and the simple fact that she was sitting in a freezing safe house beside a bleeding diplomat who had just confessed to losing a dodgeball match because he insulted a girl's hair.

Damian blinked at her, startled for a beat, and then the laugh took him too. He pressed gauze back to his eyebrow mid-laugh because the strips pulled when his face moved, which only made it worse. Next door, the sportscaster went into a goal call at exactly the wrong time and their laughter cracked open again. Pain stabbed through Anya's ribs every time the laugh hit and she didn't care enough to stop.

Laughter wound down the way it actually did, in smaller and smaller aftershocks until all that was left was the cold apartment around them and the ache lodged under her left side.

"I haven't done that in a while," Damian said.

"Same."

The room had gotten colder while they were busy not noticing it. Anya stood to raid the closet for anything softer than the mattress and found one wool blanket folded on the top shelf, stiff with dust and old storage smell. When she shook it out, the apartment briefly smelled like soap and cardboard.

"Luxury accommodations," Damian said.

"Don't get spoiled."

He took one edge because of course he did. There wasn't enough blanket for diplomacy. By the time they settled it over their knees and shoulders, they were sitting shoulder to shoulder again, the wool scratching through her shirt and his thigh touching hers because the mattress was narrow and neither of them had the energy left for a more graceful arrangement.

The blanket didn't reach their feet. Damian noticed first and pulled it sideways without comment until it covered the strip of bruised skin still exposed above her waistband. His knuckles brushed her there and stopped as if he'd realized where his hand was only after it got there. Not strategy. Not flirtation polished into technique. Just an instinctive adjustment that landed much harder than it had any right to. Anya looked down because looking at him seemed worse. He did the same. The wool settled. Neither of them moved away.

The tactical churn in his thoughts had finally burned itself out. What came through now was quieter and much more dangerous. The thin mattress. The cold room. The handkerchief in his lap. The woman sitting close enough that if he moved one hand-width, he could touch her. Not the operative. Not the ally. Her. The way she'd gone between him and the baton without waiting to think.

*I don't want to go back there.*

---

Nobody was coming.

She had chosen that with the battery in her palm and the phone still dark in the closet. There would be no medic, no handler, no second pair of hands. The apartment held only the bare bulb, the distant sportscaster, their blood, the wool scratching at their shoulders, and the note in her pocket that meant the mole now had a shape even if it didn't have a face.

The silence between them changed anyway.

It wasn't the silence of people who had run out of language. It was the one that arrived when both people knew exactly what was about to happen and were still giving each other time to stop it.

Damian's surface was wide open. Exhaustion and pain had stripped him down to almost nothing. At a reception he could keep half a dozen thoughts moving in parallel and never let one show. Here the current ran clear. *She's right there. If I touch her and she moves away, fine. If she doesn't--*

Anya should have shut her range down. Same as she should have made the call. Same as she should have told him the truth when he asked about Eden. That entire list of correct decisions stood off to one side and watched her ignore it.

"Damian."

"Yeah."

"I'm sorry about your eyebrow."

"It's fine." His fingertips found the strips. "I'll look rugged."

"You'll look like you got elbowed in the face."

A crooked mouth, almost a smile. "Ruggedly elbowed."

That was enough.

The safe house light did nobody any favors. It showed the split in his brow, the blood dried at his jaw, the hair he'd shoved back too many times because he needed his hands to do anything except shake. It probably showed the same thing in reverse: bruised ribs, shirt tugged down in a hurry, a mouth still swollen from laughing too hard. They were twenty years old, nearly killed, alone together in a room too cold to mistake for comfort.

When he leaned in, Anya didn't retreat.

He kissed her carefully at first, more question than claim. His fingers touched the side of her face, cold from the room. Copper lingered where blood had gotten to his lip earlier. Under that there was only him, no diplomatic polish, no operational language, no cover story strong enough to stand between them and what was happening.

She kissed him back.

Everything professional went vague at once: alliance terms, risk assessments, the negotiated fence around whatever this had been until now. The blanket slipped to their waists. His other hand came to her hip and stayed light for half a second, giving her room to pull away. She didn't. Her hand found the back of his neck. His pulse jumped under her fingers. A single startled *oh* from him, and then the hesitation was gone.

Trying not to read him worked exactly as well as trying not to hear the sportscaster through the wall.

Fragments hit in bursts. *Real. Her mouth. Don't stop.* A key turning in an apartment too quiet when he came home. Cardboard boxes still unpacked beside a chair with one jacket over it. His own voice at a meeting, saying things he didn't believe in the tone required to make everyone else believe he did. *Don't do the polished version now. Don't make this another performance.* Then, smaller and sharper because it was happening now instead of earlier: *Please. Let this be real.*

Those weren't confessions. Strong feeling had ripped them loose before he could stop it, turning private spill into broadcast.

Her whole body reacted before she had a usable thought. Her hand jerked in his hair. Her mouth went slack. The kiss lost its rhythm so abruptly that Damian felt it.

He drew back at once. Not far. Just enough to look at her. His grip loosened on her hip. *Too fast. I hurt her. Stupid.*

The room changed in a heartbeat. A moment earlier it had been hunger, relief, the shock of still being alive. Now information contaminated it too. She could still taste his want. She knew about the empty apartment, the lonely key, the plea he hadn't meant to say anywhere except inside his own head.

She should've let him go.

Instead she caught the front of his shirt and kissed him again.

Not careful this time. Not clean. She went back in with her eyes open for a second and one hand braced hard on his shoulder, as if force could turn the act back into something simple. It couldn't. Her ribs complained when he pulled her closer. He made a soft sound against her mouth and then another when pain tugged at the split in his brow. Nothing about it felt like the first kiss anymore. This one kept breaking on breath, on pain, on the extra current she couldn't shut off.

Then memory flashed through him, bright because the same nerves were doing too much at once. Gym lights. Soap water skidding across varnished floor. Poster paint ground into the rubber edge of a sneaker. Pink hair shoved back with impatient fingers. Laughter. The class photo waiting on a folding table by the wall. The thought that chased it came closer than the others had.

*Anya. Anya F--*

His mouth stalled against hers. A hiss broke out of him as pain spiked behind the wounded brow and shattered the rest.

She heard even that much. Heard the name start. Heard how near it had gotten. And still, after one suspended second that could've become stopping, she kissed the corner of his mouth and kept going.

He drew back just far enough to breathe. His forehead rested against hers. One hand stayed spread between her shoulder blades, warm even through the fabric of her shirt. The other still held her at the hip.

"I don't know what this does to the operation," he said.

"I don't either."

A thread of laughter touched her mouth. "Probably nothing good."

"Probably not."

He didn't move away. Neither did she. For a few seconds the only thing that existed was the fact of his hand between her shoulders and the way both of them were still dragging air like they'd run to get here.

Eventually he leaned back against the wall. Contact thinned but didn't break. Their knees still touched. Her shoulder stayed against his. His fingers, without seeming to decide it, caught in the fabric at her sleeve and remained there.

Exhaustion hit him fast after that. Adrenaline had been doing impossible work and now it was done. The eyelids got heavy. The line of his mouth softened. Thought slowed, then drifted.

What came back as he slid toward sleep wasn't just the gym floor this time. It was syllables.

*Anya.*

A pause long enough to make her own pulse kick.

*For--*

His mouth moved once against the wool at her shoulder and stopped there. The name didn't stay in him. Pain broke it every time it got close to locking. But it kept coming back closer.

Anya stayed very still.

The bare bulb buzzed. His blood had dried brown at the edges of her nails from the butterfly strips. In her pocket, the folded note dug into her thigh: `18:12 / west exit revision / WS-7`. The prepaid phone stayed dark in the closet with its battery beside it.

He slept in short, uneven drifts with his shoulder against hers and his fingers loose in her sleeve. Her jaw hurt. The bruise under her ribs flashed hot whenever he shifted. Once his mouth moved against the wool again and that unfinished name scraped through her hard enough to turn her stomach. She kept her eyes on the black window until they burned and didn't pull free.


# Chapter 10

Under the kitchen light, the milk was going to be a problem.

The next evening, Yor knew the milk was going to be a problem and that Anya had texted at dawn, *moved him. he's okay. report later from somewhere cleaner.* At noon an embassy number she didn't recognize sent another: *still north on `WS-7` don't wait up love you.* Yor didn't know who *him* was or what `WS-7` meant. She only knew Anya had been up before sunrise, had not come home, and was using the stripped-down work language that usually meant she'd gone too long without food.

She held the carton at an angle and read the date stamp. Tomorrow. Back into the refrigerator it went, behind the leftover stew, where Loid was less likely to discover it. He had opinions about expiration dates that she considered disproportionate to the actual science of dairy spoilage, but she'd never found a way to say that without sounding like she was defending spoiled milk, which she wasn't. What she was defending was the principle that milk didn't become poison at midnight on the printed date. She was fairly sure about this.

Bond had taken over the middle of the living room rug again, chin pressed into the worn border, sleeping with the calm certainty that everyone else would step around him. His breathing came deep and even, with a faint whistle at the end of each exhale, and one back leg twitched with whatever passed through a precognitive dog's sleeping mind. Yor sometimes caught Anya watching Bond with an expression that suggested she was listening to a station Yor couldn't hear, but the habit was so old and so gentle that Yor had filed it years ago among the things about her daughter that she loved without understanding.

Across the hall, Anya's old bedroom door stood half open. The bedspread was smooth.

A bento box sat open on the counter. This was not strictly necessary. Loid was at the university for a consulting session, one of the occasional appointments that maintained a cover identity that had long since stopped feeling like one, and he wouldn't be back until evening. But Yor liked making them. The routine was grounding. Rice balls, three, with pickled plum. Tamagoyaki, two slices, trimmed where the browning had edged into char. A thermos of mushroom miso soup from a recipe she'd clipped from a magazine in the city hall break room.

The soup tasted too salty. Not catastrophically, but enough that she could hear Anya's polite silence if she ever served it, the careful way Anya said "It's good, Mama" when what she meant was *This is mostly salt*.

The bento went into the refrigerator with a note: *The soup is maybe a little salty. Sorry! Please don't feel like you have to drink it. But I think it's okay? Yor*

As she dotted the question mark, her phone buzzed.

Not a ring. Just one hard pulse against the countertop, the pattern reserved for numbers that didn't exist in any directory. She set down the pen and picked up the phone.

An address on the northern outskirts. A name: Karl Dominic. A timeframe: tonight. Beneath it, the standard return instruction blinked in smaller text. Confirm completion within forty-five minutes of execution. And at the bottom, the six-digit code that confirmed it was real.

Twice was enough. The message would erase itself in ninety seconds. She set the phone face-down on the counter and went back to the bento box, adjusting the thermos so it wouldn't leak during refrigeration. Once the lid was closed, the knife she'd used for the tamagoyaki got washed, dried, and returned to the block.

Bond opened one eye from the rug, then closed it.

"I have an errand tonight," Yor told him. "Papa's dinner is in the fridge, so leave it alone."

She considered sending Anya another message about coats and late trams, decided she was already hovering in a way twenty-year-olds hated, and didn't. Instead she packed the leftover stew into a smaller container in case Anya came by after midnight hungry enough to stop pretending she wasn't.

After a shower, she dressed in a fitted dark jacket, pants that didn't restrict movement at the hip, and flat shoes with grip soles she'd ordered from a work-supply catalogue under the reasoning that icy sidewalks were dangerous and practical footwear was just common sense. Which was true. The shoes were also excellent for moving quietly across concrete and gravel, which was the actual reason she'd bought them.

In the closet, behind the shoe rack and a box of seasonal clothes she rotated every April and October with a conscientiousness that Loid found endearing and Anya found baffling, sat a locked case. The key lived on a chain around her neck alongside a small enamel peanut charm, a prize from an Eden Academy festival booth, won by a six-year-old Anya and presented with the solemnity of a military decoration. The charm's paint was chipping at the edges, and she'd never replaced it.

Inside the case: two blades, a garrote wire, a compact medical kit, and lockpicks that had been Garden standard issue since before Anya was born. From it she selected the longer blade and the picks. The garrote she left. It was a single target in a compound setting. The blade was cleaner, faster, easier to sheathe when the job was done. The garrote required sustained proximity she didn't enjoy. That wasn't out of squeamishness. It was because the garrote was slow, and slow meant time spent close enough to hear the sound the body made, and that was a sound she preferred to keep brief.

With the blade strapped to the inside of her left forearm, under the jacket sleeve, Yor checked the draw. The motion was smooth. The lockpicks went into her inner pocket. The phone went into the other one, screen down over the return code she was not going to think about yet.

In the hallway mirror, she looked like a woman heading out for the evening. It was a bit severe. She tried the small reception-desk smile she used at city hall when she arrived for her shift. It softened things.

On her way out, she stopped at the hall table where Loid had left a shopping list in his careful handwriting: rice, laundry soap, Bond's joint medication, milk. That reminder had been on her list all day. He would buy a new carton, find the old one hiding behind the stew, pour it out, and give her the expression. Not disapproval exactly. More the gentle bewilderment of a man who had spent decades in intelligence operations and could not fathom his wife's relationship with dairy.

At the bottom she added *milk, please check fridge first!* in her rounder, larger handwriting, and beneath it: *Back late. There's stew if you're hungry.*

The door locked behind her. The landing smelled of the neighbor's floor wax and the building's old radiators kicking against the October chill.

Then she went to kill Karl Dominic.

---

The compound was forty minutes north of Berlint proper, where the city's residential blocks thinned into industrial lots and gated estates belonging to people who valued privacy over proximity. Yor took the tram to the northern terminal and walked the remaining two kilometers along a service road paralleling a drainage canal. The air smelled of wet concrete, diesel from a freight depot she'd passed a kilometer back, and the faintly vegetal rot of standing water in the ditch.

The tram had been half full. Shift workers folded into coats. A student asleep against the window with ink on two fingers. A woman with grocery sacks and a face so tired it looked arranged that way. Yor stood the whole ride, one hand on the rail, and read the briefing file once before it wiped itself. Karl Dominic. Fifty-three. Arms broker. His supply chain had been linked to the recent military buildup along the Ostanian border: weapons transfers routed through shell companies, warehouse leases, and stamped invoices that made rifles travel dressed as civilian freight. The Garden's assessment classified him as a destabilizing agent. Eliminate. Standard contract.

Standard. The word belonged to the paper trail around every job: assignment number, return code, the deposit arriving on the fifteenth. Take the name. Do the work. Wipe the blade clean before it dried. Be home in time to pack leftovers and answer Loid when he asked how city hall had been. If she stopped in the middle and asked who had checked the file, who had signed off, whose daughter was waiting in some apartment with the light on, the whole mechanism would seize. So she had not asked. She had called that trust.

So had the shipping clerk. Three months ago. A man in the port district whose briefing profile listed arms connections that turned out, when she'd checked afterward, to be invoices for industrial machine parts. Legitimate equipment for a legitimate factory. The man had begged, not for himself, which she could manage, but for his daughter, who was waiting at home. They sometimes begged. She'd trained herself to close that channel years ago. But the invoices. The gap between the profile and the evidence. She'd reported the discrepancy to her handler. The handler had said: *Thank you, Yor. Don't worry about it. These things are reviewed.* And she'd stopped worrying.

Mostly, she'd stopped worrying.

The landscape changed as she walked. Berlint's streetlights gave way to intermittent industrial security lamps, and the sounds of the city, tram bells, traffic, a bar's music leaking from somewhere, faded into the low drone of freight machinery and then, past the last depot, into nothing much at all. The service road was unpaved beyond the terminal's reach: packed dirt and gravel that crunched under her soles. Past a shuttered machine shop came a fenced lot full of construction aggregate, then a drainage pump station where someone had left a folding chair and an empty thermos. The only other living thing she'd encountered in the last kilometer was a cat that crossed the road, evaluated her with the professional indifference of an animal that knew it couldn't be caught, and vanished into a culvert pipe.

Half past ten found her at the compound. The perimeter was a three-meter concrete wall topped with razor wire and motion sensors at the corners. Beyond it: the roofline of the main building, interior lights, the muted geometry of an estate that had been a manufacturing facility before Dominic repurposed it. Sealed loading docks on the east side. The main gate faced south, connected to the highway by a paved access road.

Surveillance settled into place. Three men visible at first glance: one stationed at the gate, shifting his weight foot to foot with the restlessness of a man assigned a post he found boring; one near a cargo van parked in the loading area, its rear doors open; one walking the interior perimeter on a patrol route. She timed the patrol. Four minutes per circuit. Consistent rhythm. The pace of routine rather than alertness. A second look caught more: office lights burning in the southwest corner of the main building, the intermittent shadow of men crossing a conference room farther in, the bright square of paperwork being moved from one hand to another. A live deal, not storage.

The north wall had a vulnerability. A motion sensor housing mounted at an angle that covered the airspace above the wall rather than the wall face itself, a factory installation never corrected. Exactly the mistake that stayed in place when security was maintained by men who filled out forms instead of climbing walls.

When the patrol rounded the south wall, she went up the north wall at the sensor gap and dropped into the compound's northeast corner behind a maintenance shed. The shed smelled of machine oil and dried grass clippings. Thirty still seconds passed while the patrol guard's boots crunched by on the other side, steady and unhurried, the steps of a man completing a task he'd completed a thousand times without incident.

The crunch faded toward the south. She moved.

The man at the cargo van was leaning against the rear bumper, smoking. His sidearm sat on his right hip, holstered, the retention snap undone. It was structural carelessness. He'd never encountered trouble here, and the boredom of guard work had eroded whatever discipline he'd once maintained. Yor came around the van's blind side. The last three meters took less than a second. She put the blade through the base of his skull at the junction where the brainstem lived, and he dropped. She caught his weight against the van's bumper and lowered him to the pavement without noise. His cigarette rolled under the tire.

Inside the van: long wooden crates, unmarked, secured with military-grade packaging straps. The chemical smell of preservative oil seeped through the wood grain. Rifles. She counted twelve crates. Enough for a company.

From there she moved toward the main building.

The loading dock's sealed entrance had a secondary door, service access, a standard pin-tumbler lock that the picks handled in nine seconds. A corridor inside, fluorescent tubes overhead, half of them dead and the survivors flickering with a buzz that set her teeth on edge. An office door on the left, closed. Voices from deeper in the building, male, three or four of them, the measured rhythm of negotiation. The private sale. Buyers in the building. A steel fire door marked MAINTENANCE STAIR sat at the corridor branch, paint bubbled near the bottom from old flooding.

That changed the approach. Buyers meant witnesses and potentially more armed men. She needed the principal and the exit, nothing else.

Two guards in the corridor, stationed outside a conference room. The first noticed her at six meters and reached for his radio. Wrong decision. Yor closed the gap in a stride and drove the heel of her palm into his trachea. He folded with a wet, shocked gasp. The second guard was faster. He'd drawn his sidearm by the time she reached him. But his partner was between them, and the half-second he spent calculating a clear firing line was a half-second he didn't have. Her blade opened the inside of his forearm from wrist to elbow, deep enough to ruin the arm, and the gun clattered to the floor tiles. An elbow below his ear dropped him after that.

The first guard's radio came with her. The channel stayed quiet long enough for a fragment from the conference room to carry through the door.

"Not until Karl says," one man said. "He wants the papers in order."

Dominic liked control. No one would burst into his office uninvited while buyers were waiting to be told they could touch what they'd paid for. That would buy her minutes. Not many.

The corridor branched. Left toward the offices. Right toward the voices. She went left. The buyers were not her contract.

Karl Dominic was alone in a corner office at the corridor's end, behind a desk buried in working stacks of paperwork, speaking into a phone. He was shorter than his file photo suggested, stocky, balding, in a dress shirt with the sleeves pushed past his elbows. The office smelled of cigar smoke layered over cheap instant coffee from the tins stacked in freight depot commissaries. A framed photograph on the desk showed Dominic with a woman on a boat, somewhere sunny, both of them squinting at the camera with the particular resignation of people who'd been told to smile.

He looked up when the door opened.

"No," he said into the phone, already irritated, already mid-argument. "I want the confirmations in front of me before they touch the second shipment. If Voss wants deniability, he can pay for it twice."

Then his face processed confusion, recognition, fear.

He dropped the phone and lunged for the desk drawer.

Yor was faster. She had been faster than every person she'd ever been sent to kill, and twenty years had not changed that. The speed lived below thought, below decision, in the architecture of muscle and nerve that the Garden had trained and she had maintained through thousands of hours of practice she told Loid was yoga. Her blade caught his reaching wrist before his hand found the drawer, and the cut was deep enough to part tendons. He screamed, short and bitten off, with no room left in it for dignity.

He tried to stand. She put the blade through the left side of his neck, angled for the carotid, and held it for the two seconds it took. His legs buckled. He went down against the desk, scattering papers, and slid to the floor trailing a red line down the laminate front.

The phone was still connected. A tinny voice on the other end asked, "Karl? Karl, what happened?"

Yor disconnected the call.

The desk intercom buzzed almost at once. Once. Then again. She looked down. Beside the blotter lay a cream index card in Dominic's thick handwriting.

22:35 buyers seated  
22:40 signatures  
22:45 yard release

The wall clock above the filing cabinet read 22:38.

Wiping the blade on the hem of Dominic's shirt, she sheathed it. Her hands were steady. They were always steady during the work. They didn't shake or hesitate or feel the weight of what they'd done until afterward, when the adrenaline burned off and she was just Yor again in a kitchen with a knife and a cutting board, pausing over carrots long enough to blame the tremor on the cold.

The papers on the desk were organized in working stacks, not abandoned clutter. Tonight's crates. Tonight's buyers. Tonight's payments.

Garden training had been explicit on this point. The Garden provided the briefing; the operative executed the contract. Investigation was a different role, different people, a different level of the organization. For twenty years, that division had been enough.

But the shipping clerk. The invoices that hadn't matched. The handler's voice: *Don't worry about it.* And the daughter, waiting at home.

Yor looked at the papers scattered across Dominic's desk and the ones that had fallen to the floor, some of them soaking up the blood pooling from his neck, and she didn't leave.

The top stack held shipping manifests and transfer orders for military hardware: rifles, communications equipment, explosive ordnance, all routed through holding companies whose names meant nothing but whose addresses landed in Berlint's government district, near the defense ministry. The shipment codes repeated. So did a name. Werner Voss.

The intercom buzzed a third time. In the conference room, chairs moved. Yor set her phone to silent, braced it over the desk, and took the fastest images first: cover sheet, buyer list, Voss transfer page, the order for encrypted field communications equipment that had moved six weeks earlier to an anonymous warehouse near the ministry. The quantities suggested equipping a field unit, not filling a depot. An active operation, being assembled through Karl Dominic's desk.

Under the manifests sat a second packet, clipped to the first on heavier cream stock. Those were Dominic's confirmations.

The radio in her hand hissed. "Gate to corridor," a man said. "We need Karl in two."

These were not freight receipts or customs clearances. They were completion records, the back-office proof Dominic would use to show a liability had been handled and demand payment for it. One look told her why they felt familiar. The forms used the same sequence as the Garden's own closeout notices: assignment number, compensation release, executing party.

The contracting entity was coded. She didn't recognize the designator. The executing entity, on the summary sheet clipped to the front, appeared nine times across four years under a numerical code Yor knew as well as her own address. She'd seen it on her payment records for twenty years, on the deposits that arrived on the fifteenth of each month, on the brief confirmations after each completed assignment, on the secure documents she signed when she accepted new contracts.

The Garden.

The code sat in the same place she usually looked for her own fee. Her fingertips slipped on the cream paper. For the first time that night the room gave a small, ugly tilt under her shoes.

Faster now, she read. There were nine assignments: liability removal and witness management. They were not border threats or coup planners. They were cleanup for bad shipments and men who had seen too much. She photographed the summary page, then the payment table behind it, the release dates landing on the fifteenth like the deposits in her own account. A door latch clicked in the corridor. Someone spoke outside, annoyed and too close to make out.

One entry carried a date from three months ago and a district marker from the port. She found the corresponding page halfway down the packet and snapped it before the words stopped blurring. There were the machine-parts invoices again. There was the clerk's name. She saw the cramped office, the stack of stamped forms, the moment he started begging only when he understood she wasn't there to threaten him. He had said his daughter was waiting at home.

The shipping clerk had not been an exception. Or if he had been, this dead man had eight others filed beside him.

There were still pages under her thumb. She did not get them. The hall outside had changed shape. A buyer's voice carried clear now, no longer muffled by conference-room walls. "Where is he?"

Yor took one last image of the page headers still buried in the packet, enough to keep the assignment numbers and dates, then laid the cream stock back where she'd found it. Taking the whole packet would announce the search before she reached the yard. Leaving it meant Dominic's people might not know for minutes which pages had been looked at and which hadn't.

Karl Dominic had not hidden a confession in a lucky drawer. He had been working through tonight's sale with the paperwork laid out exactly as he needed it, shipment on top and cleanup underneath, because men like him believed paper was insurance.

Twenty years. The number landed like a deposit date. For twenty years the Garden had handed her a name, a district, a return code, and she had sharpened blades, wiped sinks, hung up her coat, and told herself Loid's shoes by the door and Anya's bed across the hall stayed safe because she did what was necessary.

Now an arms broker had kept proof that the Garden did cleanup for his supply chain in the same neat stacks as freight paperwork.

What if the shipping clerk had not been a mistake. What if the mistake was every time she'd folded a closeout slip and put it away without looking harder.

No clear next step followed that thought. The blank space frightened her. She had always known what came next.

The first shout went up as she reached the corridor. Not a full alarm, not yet. Just the shocked bark of a man finding the guard on the floor outside the conference room. The clean route was gone. She hit the maintenance stair instead, took the steps in silence, and came out through a lower service bay that stank of solvent and wet cardboard. By the time she crossed the yard, the perimeter patrol was already turning north twenty seconds ahead of schedule.

Back over the wall she went, using the same sensor gap and not enough time. Her left hand caught the razor wire on the descent. She felt the resistance and the tug but not the pain. The pain arrived thirty seconds later on the service road, a line of hot sting across her palm that resolved into a shallow cut running along the old scar from when Anya was eight and learning to cut carrots and the knife had slipped from her small grip and caught Yor's hand instead. Anya had cried for twenty minutes. Yor hadn't minded the cut. She'd held her bleeding palm behind her back so Anya wouldn't feel bad and said, "See? It's nothing. Let's try again."

With the cut wrapped in her jacket sleeve, she kept walking.

Then she saw the ditch.

At the base of the wall, where the service road curved toward the canal, the weeds beside the industrial waste containers had been pressed flat into a human hollow. A cheap thermos lay on its side with the cap screwed back on tight. Beside it sat compact field glasses wrapped in dark tape to kill reflection, a pencil worn down to the metal, and a folded municipal survey map pinned under a broken brick. The compound perimeter had been sketched on the back in quick pencil strokes: south gate, loading dock, patrol route, the north sensor gap she'd used. In the upper corner someone had written `WS-7`.

Somebody had been here long enough to count.

Movement broke beside the containers.

A figure rose. One shoulder lower under a light pack. Weight on the back foot. Small enough to be underestimated.

Yor's mind measured the distance, the angle, the quickest path to the throat.

Then the figure stepped into the spill of security light and it was Anya.

Yor stopped. Her daughter was in dark clothes, not the diplomatic wardrobe, not the outfits of a cultural attaché at Westalian embassy events, but cut from the same logic as Yor's own: cloth that wouldn't shine, snag, or hold the light. Anya's hair was pulled back under a dark knit cap. Mud darkened the knees of her trousers. A compact camera strap crossed her ribs under the jacket. The field glasses on the ground were hers. So was the map.

Anya looked too thin. She always looked thin. Milk and soup were small problems with practical answers. This belonged to the other pile, the ache that stayed lodged behind Yor's ribs because Anya was twenty and an adult and there was no decent way to make an adult eat.

Neither of them spoke for a moment. The canal water moved in the ditch. The compound's lights threw a pale halo against the low October clouds.

"You should eat more," Yor said. "You look thin."

Anya's mouth opened. Closed. Her eyes dropped to Yor's left hand, the jacket sleeve dark and stiff with blood, the makeshift wrapping already soaking through.

"Your hands are bleeding, Mama."

"Oh." Yor looked at her hand as if the blood were a social inconvenience, like noticing a stain on her shirt at a work dinner. "It's, um. It's not bad. I scraped it on a fence. It's really not deep. I just didn't have a, um. I'll clean it when I get home."

Her own voice sounded absurd in the canal dark. Fence. As if Anya were twelve and had found her after grocery shopping instead of outside a weapons broker's wall with surveillance notes pinned under a brick.

Yor looked at the map again. "What are you doing here?"

Anya followed her glance and, for a second, seemed to consider lying. Yor watched the thought pass through her face and go.

"I caught a fragment at a reception," Anya said. "Asset inside WISE. Then a west-side mailbox gave me `18:12 / west exit revision / WS-7`. I spent three days lining review windows up against movement traffic and who could touch it. It kept narrowing. Dominic's gate was the first place the corridor turned into an actual person, so I came to watch it."

She swallowed. "I was supposed to see who came out after Dominic. Get a plate number, maybe a face. Anything I could use."
"I didn't think it was going to be you."

Yor's eyes went to the taped field glasses, the notes, the careful little pencil marks counting patrol time. Not an accident, then. Not curiosity. Her daughter had come north from some other piece of trouble and laid herself flat in canal weeds to watch a killer's house breathe.

"You were going to follow them?" Yor asked.

"If I had to."

The answer came too fast. Anya heard it too and made a face at herself, small and irritated.

"I mean," she said, "I was going to decide that when I knew who it was. I hadn't really finished the plan."

That was so recognizably Anya that Yor almost smiled. It vanished before it could settle.

Behind the wall, someone shouted again. This time the word *body* carried clearly.

Anya's gaze flicked to the compound, then to the phone-shaped weight in Yor's pocket, then back to her face. A change passed over her there, quick and impossible to name. Not surprise exactly. Not fear. Recognition, maybe, but pointed in a direction Yor could not place and could not safely ignore.

"They were waiting on signatures," Anya said. "If anything's missing, they'll know fast."

Yor said nothing.

Anya held her eyes. "And if you make whatever call comes next tonight, they work backward. The wall. The ditch. Me. So don't. Just go home."

The night seemed to thin around that sentence.

Yor didn't ask how Anya knew there was a call. She didn't ask what reception, what mailbox, or why her daughter now sounded like someone who understood reporting windows and search patterns well enough to price the next fifteen minutes. Anya didn't ask why Yor had Dominic's blood on her sleeve, or what was in the pocket she'd glanced at twice, or whether the look on Yor's face belonged to pain or knowledge Yor didn't want named.

Those questions stayed where they always stayed between them, large and untouched. But the space around them had changed shape. It was no longer ignorance. It was arrangement.

"I need to get back," Yor said, gesturing toward the road to the tram station. The old line came to her out of habit and sounded childish now. "It's late."

"Yeah." Anya nodded once. "I'm not coming with you."

"Right. Of course."

Another silence sat down between them. The canal gurgled. A light snapped on above the compound's loading dock and threw a harder glare across the road.

"Were you here alone?" Yor asked.

"Yes. It was just watching. I wasn't going in."

That answer she believed. It did not reassure her.

"Be careful getting home," Yor said. "It's cold tonight. You have a proper coat? Not just that one?"

"I know the route. I've got layers."

"There's stew in the fridge. From yesterday. If you want to stop by. You don't have to. But it's there."

"I'll come by. Tomorrow, maybe."

"And the soup. The mushroom one. It's new. It might be..." Yor glanced helplessly at her bleeding hand as if it had personally interrupted her sentence. "I think I used too much miso. But it's fine. Probably. It's an experiment."

"It's probably not that bad. I'll try it."

Yor wanted to cross the six paces between them. She wanted to put a hand on the back of Anya's head and say one ordinary sentence that would get them safely through the night. Instead she kept both hands to herself. One was bleeding. The other had touched Dominic's paperwork and carried photographs of the Garden's code in an arms broker's office.

"Goodnight, Anya."

"Night, Mama."

Yor turned toward the tram road. Looking back felt impossible. Even so, before the bend took the canal out of sight, she heard the small sounds of Anya repacking the observation post: thermos cap, brick against gravel, paper folding once and then again.

At the dead streetlamp near the service-road junction, her phone vibrated: the confirmation window closing. Eleven minutes.

If she entered the code, Garden would start with the breach. North wall. Ditch. Flattened weeds, field glasses, the map marked `WS-7`. Then Dominic's desk, the missing pages, the images on the phone in her hand.

For twenty years completion had meant code, silence, home.

Tonight Yor slid the battery free.


# Chapter 11

After the canal, Anya spent three days inside WISE headquarters, and by now the fluorescent buzz felt lodged behind her eyes.

Not from the reception itself. Crowds gave her the bright-light headache and then let go. This was three days of precision work: engineered elevator rides, timed coffee runs, standing beside colleagues with a dead phone in her hand while their thoughts scraped past hers in fragments, taking the long way to the archive because two analysts from signals usually argued there at 10:15 and one of them had clearance she needed to hear from up close. The most invasive thing she had ever done on purpose.

Yor kept breaking through it anyway. Dominic's records. Garden's code in Dominic's paperwork. The crack that had opened in her mother's head on the service road. But Anya had nothing she could carry into a briefing folder, no way to ask Yor what she'd found without admitting she'd stolen it out of her mind, and no reason to think Yor would tell anyone before she tried to think her way back into obedience. That was the bet Anya had made when she turned away instead of following Yor home. Maybe Yor would go quiet for a few days. Maybe, in those same few days, Garden would clean the trail and Yor would let them. Damian's warning about the WISE leak felt shorter than that. So Anya came to headquarters and stayed.

In her fourth-floor cubicle, coffee went cold beside an access-log spreadsheet. The fluorescents buzzed. Reheated fish from the break room was making the whole floor smell like punishment. Outside, two analysts argued about sourcing methodology while one of them worried about his dead dog and the other about an overdue credit-card bill.

Reading WISE all day got her the same junk everybody else carried around: errands, resentment, money, lunch, whether anyone had replaced the toilet paper on the third floor, whether a mole on somebody's shoulder looked worse than last week, whether the vending machine was still eating coins. Intelligence came in bursts. Between bursts, life kept being stupid and ordinary.

It had started with a single fragment from the diplomatic reception: *the asset inside WISE*. A mole, somewhere in a crowd too dense to pin to one skull. She had tried replaying the room afterward, flat on her bed with a pillow over her face to block the morning light, but telepathy wasn't a recording. It happened once and then it was memory like any other, full of holes and false certainty.

So she'd started the other way. Paper first, the way Loid had taught her since she was sixteen: before you use the cheat, do the work. Access logs. Intercepts. Who sat in which briefing, then somehow the Renewal Front knew the same thing a day later. Grosz had helped by cutting the whole giant bureaucratic mess down to the parts that actually mattered: army planning, contract lanes, the places where decisions turned into parts and shipments. If somebody inside WISE was feeding that machine, the access pattern should touch those lanes.

By Monday noon she had a private grid open across three windows: names down the left, compartments across the top, then print releases, late badge swipes, leave blocks, briefing attendance, routing authority, and a last column she hadn't labeled because the label would have been *things I only know because I stood next to people and stole them.* She let herself write in it anyway. Berger: hates Vienna posting. Dreher: hiding medical thing? Falk: gives nothing. Then she stared at the words until the shame stopped being dramatic and turned useful.

Eleven analysts had the clearance and the opportunity. Two weeks of paperwork got that down to seven. Two had been out of Berlint during key leak windows. Two more never touched the right compartments. One had the right access but spent every suspicious evening in a gambling room loud enough that even telepathy would have had to fight the slot machines. Another was having an affair so obvious in his thoughts that Anya wanted to apologize to every person who had ever accidentally stood near her. Brief reads of colleagues whose surface minds were banal, frightened, horny, or furious in ways that had nothing to do with procurement channels dropped the list again. By Monday evening she had three names.

Anna-Lise Berger had been one of them. Berger's file looked bad in the abstract: access to the right summaries, two after-hours printer releases, a stretch of bitterness sharp enough to read from across a conference table. An elevator ride killed that. Berger's head was full of Vienna resentment, her mother's slipping memory, a dentist appointment she'd been avoiding for three months, the dull ache of being forty-three and professionally overlooked, and a panicked internal reminder to buy incontinence pads before visiting her mother again because the last time she'd forgotten and spent two hours pretending not to notice the wet chair. When the doors opened, Berger smiled at Anya and stepped out still innocent, and Anya stepped out carrying things Berger had never offered her.

She ducked into the stairwell afterward and stood with one hand on the rail until the elevator started moving again. Protect your own secret by prying under everybody else's. Good work. Real principled stuff.

That left two. Martin Dreher and Gerald Falk.

Dreher worked signals analysis on the third floor. He was fifty-one. He drank black coffee with two sugars in the cheap styrofoam cups everybody hated. A desk photo showed his wife, two sons, and a Golden Retriever who looked disappointed to be there. He had been in the building fifteen years and brought Friday pastries from a bakery near his apartment. He was the kind of colleague people described by habit because the habits were what you noticed first.

His file looked worse than Berger's had. Dreher's desk touched the Baltic readiness brief, border-positioning summaries, and the procurement annexes Grosz had effectively put a red circle around. Over the previous month he'd pulled the same bands of material the Renewal Front kept staying a step ahead of. Three times he had released hard-copy packets from the third-floor secure printer and then vanished from the building for somewhere between ninety minutes and two hours. Three times the Renewal Front had later anticipated deployments or inspections that should have stayed inside WISE. On paper it looked clean in the ugliest way.

Anya spent most of Tuesday morning making herself prove it twice.

She checked the access times again. 13:08 on the procurement annexes. 13:14 on the border summaries. Secure print release at 13:23, user tag unresolved. Dreher badged out at 13:41. Leak window opened that evening.

Then another. 14:02, 14:05, 14:11. Print release. Blank calendar block. Dreher gone for an hour and forty-eight minutes. By the next morning the Renewal Front had already shifted material off a route WISE had planned to watch.

Then the third. Same lane. Same disappearing act.

It was enough to make her go downstairs instead of forwarding the pattern immediately. If she was going to light a fire under internal security, she wanted his face in the room with the numbers.

Tuesday morning put him alone in the break room with an empty mug in his hand, staring at the motivational poster above the microwave as if the eagle might tell him what to do next. Anya filled her own cup and stopped within range.

His thoughts hit like cold water.

*Tuesday. He said Tuesday. If the second panel is worse than the first one, if Kessler was wrong, if I've got three weeks, maybe four before it shows, before Eva looks at me and knows. Nine months with her mother and I can't do that to her. I can't.*

The panic broke, re-formed, and tried to pass as work.

*Budget meeting at two. Baltic assessment. Focus.*

Then it slipped again.

*Second referral first. Referral means paperwork. Paperwork means the WISE medical plan. HR sees the claim and I'm done. Cash for one more visit. Kessler at four. Tell Eva it's Wilkinson. Tell her I'm staying late. Please just believe that one more time.*

By the time the thought-stream collapsed into raw fear, Anya's hand had tightened hard enough on the cup to make the styrofoam creak.

"Hey, Martin."

He startled. "Anya. Hi. Morning."
