"You okay? You look a little..."

"Fine." Too fast. "Just a long week. You know how it is."

*Don't ask. Please don't ask. Not here.*

"Sure," she said. "Get some rest."

Back upstairs she tried, for twelve whole minutes, to make the read mean anything else.

Maybe Kessler was a cutout. Maybe referral meant the first step in some contact protocol. Maybe cash-only plus no paper trail plus timed absences plus the exact right document pulls was enough even if the fear underneath it wasn't about ideology. People did dangerous things for reasons smaller than belief all the time. Debt. Shame. Family. Disease. A private crisis didn't clear him. If anything, private crises made people vulnerable.

That line held until she opened his work lane and saw how neatly the bad facts stacked.

She pulled Dreher's access history, printer releases, and leave calendar into the same window and stared until the pattern stopped looking accidental. Three leak windows matched three unexplained afternoon absences, each between ninety minutes and two hours. Each absence landed after he'd pulled procurement annexes or border-positioning summaries that the Renewal Front had later anticipated almost exactly. Nothing on his calendar explained those gaps. Nothing in the user-facing system accounted for the hard-copy releases. Today's schedule had another blank block beginning at 3:40.

She checked the routing authority on the packets. Wilkinson had signed the broader Baltic tasking, but not the individual print pulls. She checked the printer room logs. Same unresolved section-code release all three days. She checked Dreher's desk assignment notes from the shared project folder. Half the filenames were routine. Half touched exactly the lanes Damian had warned her were still hot. She checked the archive request tray and found his name again in the handwriting box, request submitted for hard-copy review because "screen comparison unreliable." That might have meant aging eyes. It might have meant he didn't want a digital trail.

At 11:40 she went back downstairs under the excuse of a copier jam and walked past his cubicle. Dreher wasn't there. His desk was too neat for somebody in panic except for one open drawer crammed with antacid tablets, a crushed receipts envelope, and a bakery box folded flat for reuse. The family photo sat angled toward his chair. Next to it was a yellow legal pad with budget numbers in one column and three dates circled hard enough to bruise the paper.

Clinic dates, maybe.

Handoff dates, maybe.

The problem was that both stories used the same evidence.

At 12:06 she came back up and opened Falk's personnel file, willing the answer to be there instead because Falk made more sense as a traitor and less sense as a man with a hidden oncologist. Twenty years older than her, senior enough to move paperwork without being noticed, close enough to Loid that betrayal would actually count. But Falk's recent access looked broad in the ordinary way senior analysts looked broad, and the telepathic passes she had managed on him had turned up nothing except weather, budgets, and the kind of tidy administrative annoyance that clung to men who alphabetized their own tea tins.

By 1:30 the choice had narrowed to something simpler and worse. If she waited for cleaner proof on Falk, Dreher's 3:40 blank block would come and go. If Dreher was the leak and she sat on it because cancer was an uglier secret than treason, then the next warning would reach the Renewal Front before anyone inside WISE moved.

The pattern said four o'clock with Kessler, cash only, no paper trail.

If that was a handoff and she spent the afternoon trying to get a cleaner read on Falk, she'd be waiting through another leak to avoid making herself uncomfortable. Falk was still there. Dreher had a clock on him.

By 3:12 she had written the memo.

The wording was careful. Too careful. Nothing in it exposed how she knew. She listed the paper pieces one by one: overlapping access, weird hours, missing afternoons sitting right on top of the old leak windows, hard-copy packets that seemed to vanish, a man looking like he was about to come apart. Then she cut that last one back to "observable strain" because she couldn't write *I heard him thinking about dying in the break room,* deleted "sudden" from the line above, and printed the page.

Kurtz took it in internal security without reaction. Mid-forties, flat voice, office that smelled like stale paper and orange-peel cleaner.

She read the first paragraph while Anya stood there and said, "Why now?"

"Because there's another gap this afternoon."

"And not yesterday?"

Anya kept her face still. "Yesterday it was a pattern. Today it's a clock."

Kurtz looked back down at the paper. Not suspicious. Not reassuring either. Just evaluating. "If you're wrong, this burns a senior analyst for having a private life."

Too late to pretend that wasn't exactly what she was doing. "If I'm right, he gets the rest of the week."

Kurtz set the memo on the in-tray with one finger. "We'll look."

Counterintelligence yanked two analysts off the Ostanian desk the next morning. IT went rooting through Dreher's terminal traffic and comms metadata. A watcher took the desk opposite his in the bullpen with a legal pad and the posture of a man pretending not to watch the exact person he was watching. By lunchtime the whole third floor was walking funny, compensating around holes nobody was allowed to name while Dreher moved through the middle of it under surveillance he hadn't agreed to and couldn't yet feel.

Anya felt it for him anyway.

She felt it in the way the floor had gone fractionally wrong. Conversations stopped when he approached and restarted too fast after he passed. Printer queues backed up because the extra reviewer assigned to cover him didn't know the third-floor machine needed to be kicked low on the left side to catch the second tray. Somebody in logistics kept thinking *which one is it* every time he saw a security officer walk by. Dreher himself seemed to notice only that people were busier than usual. His surface thoughts stayed trapped inside appointments, numbers, and the repeated impossible task of deciding whether to tell Eva before or after the next scan.

At lunch on Wednesday she passed him in the corridor with a paper cup of soup in one hand and a folder tucked under his arm. He nodded like always.

"Afternoon."

"Hey."

*Don't spill. Don't spill on the report. Tell Eva Thursday. No, tonight. No. Tonight and then she looks at you differently forever.*

Anya made it all the way to the stairwell before the guilt hit hard enough to bend her over the railing.

The investigation ate two days.

Late on the second evening, Kurtz's report hit Anya's secure inbox. Three lines in, she got up from her desk and took it into the bathroom.

Martin Dreher was not selling intelligence to the Renewal Front.

Three weeks earlier, Martin Dreher had been diagnosed with Stage III pancreatic cancer. Dr. Kessler was an oncologist at a private clinic across the city. The referrals were medical. The cash was medical. The blank blocks in his afternoons were appointments, imaging, treatment planning, and time parked two streets over because he couldn't yet drive straight home and tell his wife it was happening to him now, not someday. He'd kept it off the WISE plan because a flagged claim would pull him from active work before he was ready to be looked at like a man leaving.

But Kurtz's report didn't stop there. Counterintelligence had tailed him on Tuesday and Wednesday. Both times Dreher left the building alone, took the tram to Zeidler Private Oncology, signed in under his own name, met with Kessler, then sat in his car on a side street with both hands locked on the steering wheel before driving home. No third-party contact. No alternate route. No communication with any known Renewal Front intermediary before, during, or after the appointments.

The document pulls were real and they were legitimate. Wilkinson had assigned Dreher to the Baltic contingency packet after Geller's intercept note, which meant the procurement annexes and border-positioning summaries Anya had circled were exactly what he was supposed to be reading for the weekly readiness brief. The "missing" printer releases had not disappeared into a handoff. They had gone through a third-floor secure batch queue mapped to the section code instead of individual user badges; security recovered the binders from Wilkinson's locked project cabinet with Dreher's tabs and margin notes still in them. Terminal audit clean. Communications audit clean. Personal finance clean except for the clinic withdrawals.

The leak-window alignment had been the cruelest part. Those documents passed through half the desk in the twenty-four hours before every readiness brief, which was exactly when the real mole needed them most. Dreher's absences had overlapped the leaks because his appointments happened to fall on the same afternoons Falk, Wilkinson, Geller, and two duty officers were all moving through the same circulation chain. Anya had mistaken shared timing for a private channel.

The last page listed the remaining names with access to those packets under the same project code. Gerald Falk was on it.

In the stall, the folder sat open on her lap while warm air pushed through the vent overhead. Somebody came in, used the sink, left again. Another person followed, thinking in loud irritated fragments about a train delay and a tampon she'd forgotten to replace. The thoughts crashed against Anya's skull and kept going.

Every piece she'd read had been true: Tuesday, cash, the second referral, the terror. None of it had been about treason. A man trying to keep chemo off an HR system had sounded, at the surface, exactly like a man preparing a covert handoff, and she had handed that mistake to counterintelligence in a neat memo.

Two analysts lost two days on him. IT burned hours on the terminal of a man who had been reading survival rates on his lunch break. Physical surveillance watched him come out of an oncology clinic with his face wrecked from trying not to cry in public. Somewhere in the building, the actual mole had all that room.

His desk photo came back to her, along with the Friday pastries and the low tuneless humming from three floors down. The flattened bakery box. The antacids. The legal pad with those three dates stabbed into it. She had turned the worst private fear of his life into a security referral and then made half the building rearrange itself around the damage.

Cold metal bit into the scar in her left palm while she held the stall latch and waited for the nausea to level out. At the sink, the face in the mirror looked the same. Trusting it felt harder.

She closed the report, then opened it again because hating herself was not the same thing as fixing the hole.

Falk on the last page. Falk still inside the chain.

She stood at the bathroom sink until the fluorescent hum flattened back into one sound she could stand.

---

Gerald Falk was in his usual corner of the fourth-floor bullpen the next morning with tea and a cable digest. Seeing him there felt less like spotting a colleague than realizing a familiar fixture in the room had shifted half an inch overnight. He had once shown up at her Eden graduation with a silver fountain pen that still lived in her desk drawer. Not a sentimental gift, exactly. Useful. He'd said a person should own at least one pen that made ugly paperwork feel expensive.

The shared printer gave her an excuse to stand near him. Pages fed out. She straightened them one by one and stayed inside his radius.

"You look tired," Falk said without lifting his eyes from the digest. "Eat breakfast."

His mind moved under the words in tidy little rows.

*Budget allocation for Q3 is twelve percent high again. Same trick as last year. Sylvia won't fight the escalation numbers because they're useful. Pension committee at noon. Actuarial adjustment still wrong. Might rain.*

"I'm fine," Anya said. "Long week."

"Isn't there always."

*Wilkinson routing note is sloppy. Geller used one intercept and called it a pattern. Move the extra eyes. Fix it later.*

Forty seconds passed. Then a minute. His thoughts kept moving through budget irritation, pension math, weather, soup rotation. Then, warm and unguarded in the middle of it:

*She doesn't eat enough. Loid used to do the same thing.*

That was all. There was no spike, no concealed channel, no buried panic broadcasting through the walls. After Dreher, the quiet was almost worse. Noise had made her wrong. Silence gave her nothing to be wrong about.

Back at her desk she opened Kurtz's attachment again, this time not for the shame. She pulled the circulation-chain names into a separate note and started working through them one by one. Wilkinson had authority but not enough unsupervised time on the live desks. Geller's access was narrow and loud; if he moved a channel, three people complained in writing. The duty officers rotated too often. Falk sat at the crossing point: senior enough to alter coverage, ordinary enough to vanish into procedure, trusted enough that nobody read his tidy annotations as ambition.

She opened the Wilkinson file that Falk's stray thought had tagged. Falk had annotated it at 8:12 that morning and signed off on a further coverage shift starting the next day at 0600. Two more analysts off live review of the Ostanian diplomatic procurement traffic. Temporary on paper. Forty-eight hours. Bureaucratically clean. The exact desk Damian had spent the last week warning her the Renewal Front still used to move material and instructions.

It wasn't the only clean cut.

She dug back through the previous ten days and found three smaller reallocations with Falk's initials on them. A one-shift analyst loan from procurement review to embassy media traffic. Harmless if you believed Geller's note. A delayed archive pull on border customs chatter because the desk was "overcovered." Sensible if you believed Anya's false assessment had bought them room. A request to widen a watchlist on Westalian student groups while narrowing one on Ostanian freight brokers. Perfectly defensible in isolation. Together they formed a pattern of tiny absences around exactly the lanes Grosz and Damian kept naming.

The first of the edits had landed the morning after Sylvia accepted Anya's report on Damian.

She sat back in her chair so fast it squealed.

There it was. Not proof. Worse than proof, in a way. A shape built out of things she'd made easier for somebody else to do.

Her false report had not created Falk. It had created air. He'd been breathing it ever since.

From the cubicle across the aisle, somebody was thinking furiously about a yeast infection and whether business-casual allowed boots with a heel. From the far hall came a burst of laughter, then the stray thought *if he brings tuna again I'm transferring departments.* Headquarters kept being headquarters while the page on Anya's screen quietly accused her.

She opened her desk drawer and found the fountain pen where it always lay under spare batteries and a pack of headache tablets. Silver barrel, black grip, weighty in the hand. Falk had given it to her outside Eden's assembly hall while Loid was trapped in traffic and Yor was trying not to cry onto Anya's shoulder.

"For forms worth signing," he'd said, and then, because Anya had been eighteen and suspicious of all adults on principle, "It isn't a bribe. You're not important enough yet."

She had laughed. He had, too.

Now she rolled the pen between her fingers and thought about all the things administrative men could kill without ever touching a gun. A desk didn't go blind in one dramatic flash. A desk went blind because somebody wrote TEMPORARY RESOURCE REALIGNMENT at the top of a form and signed where trust expected them to sign.

At 11:20 Falk stopped by her cubicle with his mug.

"You staying buried in coverage tables all day?"

Anya locked the Wilkinson file before turning. "Looks like it."

"Take a lunch break before you start seeing patterns in the carpet."

"Maybe the carpet's suspicious."

One corner of his mouth moved.

*Ask if she's going home this weekend. No, don't crowd her. She already looks strung thin. Wilkinson still can't format a note to save his life. Move the six o'clock handoff. No, not handoff. Coverage shift. Christ, get the wording right.*

The stumble was so small she almost missed it. Not a revelation. Not some obvious crack. Just one word appearing where it shouldn't and getting shoved aside fast enough that somebody without telepathy would never have known it had been there.

Her pulse kicked once under her ribs.

Falk tipped his mug toward the break room. "There's still some decent coffee if you move fast."

"That sounds fake, Gerald."

"It is, but it's less fake than the tea."

He kept walking.

Anya sat there with the locked screen and the pen in her hand and tried to decide whether that one slip counted as anything or whether this was how another innocent man got burned. *Handoff* could have meant the shift paperwork itself. People said the wrong operational word all the time. She knew that. She also knew he had corrected it inside his own head before the thought finished forming.

Not proof.

But no longer nothing.

She spent the next hour building the smallest case she could trust. Not betrayal. Not motive. Just motion. Falk's initials on the coverage changes. Falk on the circulation chain for the Dreher packets. Falk catching and using the gap her false report had opened. Falk's brief internal slip over the six o'clock change. By 1:00 the list fit on half a page and still looked thin enough to hate.

She did not take it to Kurtz.

Not yet.

If she handed internal security another elegant wrong answer, she would deserve whatever came after it. Damian was right about the time pressure, but Damian didn't work inside a building full of people whose private disasters could mimic treason down to the minute. This one she needed to watch happen.

At 16:40 she checked the next day's floor assignment board. The procurement desk coverage shift was still there. 0600. Falk's initials in the approval field. No one had challenged it.

Tomorrow's hunt stopped being abstract. If somebody inside WISE wanted that lane dark before the weekend, 0600 was the hour to watch.

The encrypted call came at nine that night, one-time cipher and frequency rotation. Anya sat on her kitchen floor with her back against the cabinet and had to try the key sequence twice before the line cleared through static.

Damian's voice came on stripped of its diplomatic polish.

"The weather turned."

"How bad?"

"Our friend at the garden center moved up the planting schedule. Public event. Meant to look like the people across the street started it."

It was a diplomatic incident, something visible and meant to read as Westalian provocation.

"When?"

"This weekend. Midweek at the latest. Days, not weeks."

The kitchen faucet dripped once into the sink. Then it dripped again.

"I lost two days on the personnel issue," she said.

"What happened?"

"I was sure about one of them. I was wrong. He was hiding that he's sick. Bad sick."

Silence held on the line. Then, quieter, "I'm sorry."

The words hit harder than they should have.

"I flagged him because the paperwork fit," she said before she could stop herself. "Access history, printer pulls, missing afternoons, all lined up with the leaks. It was all real. It just meant oncology instead of treason."

Damian took a breath. Paper shifted on his end, then stopped. "And the real one?"

"Still inside the same circulation chain. I think it's Falk."

"Think, or know?"

"Think. More than I did yesterday. Less than I want."

"What changed?"

She looked at the note she'd already started on the back of the electricity bill, though there was almost nothing on it yet.

"He signed a forty-eight-hour coverage cut on one of the diplomatic procurement desks. Starts at six tomorrow. Then I went backward and found three smaller reroutes in the same lanes, all after my Damian report went into the system. He keeps making the hole look administrative."

The static hissed softly between them.

"Which desk?"

She gave him the channel designation.

Another pause followed. "All right," he said. "Don't watch speeches, then. Watch deliveries. If the event is this weekend, they'll move parts first. Your person is clearing air for that or somebody is using his paperwork to do it."

"That's more useful."

"I know."

Her shoulder eased a fraction against the cabinet. It wasn't enough to matter.

"There's something else," she said. "He almost thought the wrong word."

"Meaning?"

"Meaning I don't know if I caught a tell or if I'm doing the same thing again and building a whole case out of one second of somebody's bad brain."

"Then don't build the case out of that second."

She shut her eyes.

"Right."

"Use what the paper can survive," Damian said. "Use what you can watch happen in real time. If you're right about him, he's going to need that desk dark for a reason. If you're wrong again, you burn another innocent person. So don't guess. Sit on the lane and see who uses it."

That was the first clean instruction anybody had given her all week.

"Okay."

"Can you get eyes on it alone?"

"I can get there before the shift change. After that I'll decide if I need help."

"From someone you trust?"

She let out one dry breath.

"That list is not having a good week."

He was quiet just long enough for her to picture the look he would be making if they were in the same room. Not diplomatic. Not amused. The other one.

"Anya."

"Yeah?"

"Be careful."

"You too."

"If the schedule moves again, call me."

"I will."

The line clicked dead.

For a moment she stayed on the floor listening to the pipes and the neighbor upstairs moving around in shoes that should have been illegal after nine. Then she pulled the electricity bill fully onto her lap, turned it over, and wrote:

0600  
diplomatic procurement desk  
Falk

Underneath that she added, after a second:

watch, don't report

Then, lower:

Yor one more night.

It was ugly and it was still the calculation. Her mother was somewhere in the city with Dominic's photographs and whatever was left of her faith in Garden. Anya was betting Yor would stay quiet until tomorrow because the other clock had reached six in the morning and a desk inside WISE was about to go blind.

The apartment went dark when she hit the switch. Her head still hurt, but now it was the ordinary version, the screen-stare, skipped-meal kind. She set the note beside the phone, got into bed, and stayed awake long enough to count the hours until six between the faucet drips.


# Chapter 12

At 05:58 the Communications Room sounded like three clocks losing patience at different speeds.

Fluorescent buzz. Receiver hiss. Someone two desks over turning a page too hard. Anya sat with the headset pressed to one ear and Falk's diplomatic procurement lane open in front of her, a thin green trace that had spent the last hour pretending to be ordinary. The note she'd written on the back of the electricity bill lay folded beside the keyboard, softened at the corners from how many times she'd touched it.

*watch, don't report*

Damian had said it like an operational correction, not advice. If Falk needed the desk blind for a reason, the useful thing was not to warn WISE first and let the reason disappear. Sit on the lane. See who used it.

At 06:00 the trace vanished.

Not degraded. Not noisy. Gone.

Anya straightened. "Run that back."

The duty analyst at the next terminal frowned at his board. "There's nothing to run back. Feed's dead."

"How dead?"

"Switched off, not dropped. That's clean."

Clean. Deliberate. Exactly on the minute. She kept the headset on as if the channel might decide to grow a voice out of static. Nothing crossed. No courier. No pickup. No useful mistake. Just the blind lane sitting there like somebody had lifted a floorboard and shown her the hole underneath.

The analyst glanced over. "Do you want me to flag it upstairs?"

Every instinct she'd been trained into said yes. Damian's voice, six hours old and still annoyingly solid, said no.

"Not yet," she said. "Keep the room on it."

He hesitated, then nodded.

By 06:11 the null had hardened into fact. Whatever was supposed to move through Falk's desk had not used the window because Falk had already done whatever he needed to do. Waiting longer would not give her proof. It would just make the salvage smaller.

She picked up the secure line. Sylvia answered on the second ring.

"Report."

"The lane went blind on schedule and stayed blind," Anya said. "Nobody touched it while we had eyes. If there's still anything clean in the packet, we need copied papers moving north before the search pattern tightens."

"Route."

"Kreuzfeld. Forged customs sleeve. West-side check-in by 2300 if the border's still lazy."

"If it is not lazy?"

"Then we find out tonight instead of tomorrow."

Paper rustled on Sylvia's end. "I can release the copy set and put Crane on it. You will return to your cover engagement this evening."

Of course she would. "Understood."

When she put the phone down, the dead trace was still dead. The room smelled like burnt coffee and hot wiring. Outside the narrow basement window the sky had turned the dull color of old tin.

She stayed long enough to watch the rest of the room wake up around the blind spot. Printers coughed to life. A junior analyst swore at a paper jam. Someone unwrapped a cheese sandwich that made the whole basement smell like onion. At 07:03 border confirmed Crane had the copy set and was moving north on forged customs papers. At 07:17 Sylvia sent a three-word follow-up over secure internal: HOLD COVER TONIGHT. By the time Anya finally stood up, the channel was still dead and the ordinary noise around it somehow made the silence worse.

* * *

The Blackbell estate glowed against the October dark. White lanterns lined the drive in a row so straight Anya could picture Becky stopping the car to fix one if it leaned. The topiary garden was lit from every flattering angle. Becky had confirmed photographers from three publications, which meant the effortless look on display had been built out of seating charts, florist threats, and at least one hang-up.

Anya stepped out of the car and the thoughts hit before the cold air did.

*If the valet scratches my paint, I'm going to lose it. I just had it detailed. I'm literally watching him.*

*She's wearing the Moreau. She's wearing the Moreau. I was told I was the only one with the Moreau.*

*Seven more handshakes. I can do seven. Maybe eight if I count the wife.*

Two hundred minds. She let them blur to a murmur and sorted for signals: security detail, two private guards, one off-duty SSS officer she recognized from the diplomatic reception, exit routes, and the running estimate of how long she could stay before Becky noticed if she slipped out early.

The answer, from a thought she'd caught three weeks ago during a phone call: *If she cancels on me one more time, I'm killing her. I'm not joking. I will actually kill her.* Becky had probably meant socially. The sentiment had been clear.

Damian came around the car. Dark suit, precise posture. The thin scar through his eyebrow was the only thing that didn't match the packaging. He hadn't said much on the drive over. The only useful thing he'd offered was a complaint about road checks north of the city, and the thought under it had translated cleanly: the SSS had pushed one Kreuzfeld inspection roster earlier than expected. If their courier was already on the road, earlier mattered. The mole was still unidentified. The Renewal Front's clock had shrunk to days. Attending a charity gala while all of that pressed at their backs felt obscene, but covers needed maintenance. Anya's social calendar had to look like a real person's social calendar.

"Ready?" she said.

"No."

"Still nothing from six?" he asked quietly.

"Nothing that helps."

His mouth tightened. "That's a terrible answer to get before a party."

*The things I do for East-West cultural exchange. Kirchner is going to corner me and talk about tariffs for forty minutes, and I'm going to smile until my face detaches.*

"It'll be fine," Anya said. "Becky throws a good party."

"You keep saying that."

"The food'll be amazing."

"That's what you said about the reception."

"And was I wrong?"

He paused. "You weren't entirely wrong."

*She wore that color before. The state dinner. Stop it. Trade policy. Think about trade policy.*

Anya walked toward the entrance and tried not to listen to what he was trying not to think.

Becky got to them in the foyer before the door had fully shut.

"You came!" She grabbed Anya by the shoulders, held her at arm's length, and frowned. *Mascara under one eye. Left earring fastened crooked. And she's doing the hair thing again. Something happened.* Becky swiped a thumb gently beneath Anya's eye, then straightened the earring with two quick fingers. "You actually came. I was going to be so mad if you bailed."

"I said I'd come," Anya said.

"You did your face in a moving car," Becky said under her breath. "That's never a good sign."

"Traffic."

"Same category."

"You said that about the gallery opening in August and then you texted me from a parking lot at eleven saying work blew up." Becky released her and turned to Damian with sudden, predatory interest. "And you brought a guest."

"Damian Desmond." She extended her hand. "Eden Academy. You cried at the eighth-grade science fair because your volcano didn't erupt."

*Oh no. She remembers the volcano. Of course she remembers the volcano.*

"There was a structural issue with the display," Damian started.

"It didn't erupt."

"The sodium bicarbonate ratio was off."

"Damian. It didn't erupt. I was there. You cried."

His public smile held, barely. "It was a frustrating afternoon."

*Kill me. Just kill me now.*

Anya bit the inside of her cheek.

Becky smiled, the real one, not the camera version. Her dark curls had been pinned into something that had probably required a second pair of hands and a prayer, and she was wearing a dress that probably cost more than Anya's monthly cover salary, but underneath all that she was still the girl who'd traded her lunch for Anya's dessert every day for a year at Eden and thought she was getting the better deal.

"I'm glad you're here," Becky said. The thought under it matched the words: *I'm glad. I'm glad she came.* "Both of you. Go eat, talk to people, enjoy yourselves. I have to deal with a crisis. They brought Riesling instead of Gruner Veltliner and I'm going to start a war."

She was already moving, heels sharp on the marble, thoughts skipping ahead of her.

*Check the wine table. Find Lila. She's here. Okay. She's actually here. Maybe tonight. Maybe tonight will feel real.*

Anya watched her go and felt the first pull of the evening settle behind her ribs, right where the bruise from the ambush had mostly healed and not quite vanished.

Inside, Becky had managed the impossible trick of making a donor gala feel like an Eden reunion with better tailoring. Old classmates kept spotting each other across the room and reacting with the same faces they'd worn at thirteen. Board members hovered near the logo wall pretending not to monitor who had been photographed with whom. Even the waitstaff moved like they'd been briefed that disappointing Becky Blackbell personally was a career-ending event. She'd even found a way to make nostalgia obey a seating chart. Place cards carried old house colors. The photo wall had been angled so nobody looked tired. Every part of the room said the same thing: Becky had built this on purpose, and everybody in it was cooperating whether they meant to or not.

Anya worked the room the way she'd been trained: peripheral scanning, threat assessment, social mapping. Tonight was supposed to be maintenance, not operations. She needed to be seen doing ordinary things: making small talk, circling the buffet, existing in public beside a friend who threw galas. Normal. Unremarkable. A cultural attache with a social life.

The problem was that Eden Academy alumni were everywhere.

Becky had been mining the old school network for years. Lawyers, diplomats, media producers, one person Anya was fairly sure had become a professional horse trainer. Every few feet another face clicked into place with a surface thought attached to it. *She still looks like that?* *Don't bring up the debate trophy.* *If Kessler says merger one more time, I'm leaving.* Childhood hadn't loosened its grip on anybody.

A woman in green intercepted Anya near the buffet. "Anya. From Miss Henderson's class. Do you remember me?"

*Please say yes. Please say yes. I rehearsed this in the car.*

"Of course. You sat next to the window. Third row."

"Yes. Oh my God." The relief in her head was immediate. *She remembers.* "I'm Petra. Petra Vogt. I'm in publishing now."

"That's great."

"What about you? Someone said you're with the diplomatic service?"

"Cultural attache. Mostly paperwork and events."

"That sounds amazing."

Petra's thoughts corrected the sentence for her. *Sounds boring, honestly, but she looks incredible. Is that silk?* Anya smiled, said they should catch up properly sometime, and escaped before either of them had to pretend they meant it.

Before she could make it to the terrace doors, Becky reappeared with the expression she wore when an event had slipped half an inch off center and everybody around her had become useless.

"No disappearing yet," Becky said. "I need one respectable photograph with the youth foundation board, because apparently the room only believes in charity if enough attractive alumni stand near the logo."

"You have photographers from three publications," Anya said.

"And not one of them can manufacture legitimacy out of thin air. Move."

She herded Anya and Damian toward a step-and-repeat set up by the musicians, where a photographer and three board members were arranging themselves into something that would read as spontaneous generosity in print. Otto Kirchner, silver-haired and pleased with himself, shook Damian's hand and launched directly into complaint.

"If anyone in Foreign Affairs asks," Kirchner said, "my logistics people lost half a day to customs because Kreuzfeld decided every truck needed to be turned inside out. Children's medicines do not improve in storage."

Damian's expression didn't change. "I'm sure the relevant offices have their reasons."

*Or somebody upstream decided the road mattered tonight.*

The photographer lifted his camera. Becky adjusted Anya by the elbow, adjusted Damian by the sleeve, then stood back and frowned the way she did when people were making her work harder than necessary.

"Closer," she said. "You look like you were assigned alphabetically."

They stepped half an inch toward each other. Flash. Another flash. Kirchner kept talking about manifests, delays, a refrigerated shipment stalled north of the city. Half of it was donor theater. Half wasn't. Kreuzfeld kept coming up, in Kirchner's complaint, in Damian's earlier thought, in the officer by the bar. The word had started following her around the room. WISE's courier was supposed to cross there before the tighter search rotation fully bit, papers hidden inside a customs sleeve that would look ordinary under a lazy inspection. If the inspection was no longer lazy, eleven o'clock would not be a routine signal. It would be the moment she found out whether the whole salvage plan had just died in line.

Becky examined the preview monitor, satisfied enough to stop tormenting them. "Great. You both look expensive and vaguely public-minded. Go circulate."

Near the bar, the off-duty SSS officer from the drive accepted a refill and laughed at a joke he wasn't listening to. Under the laugh his mind ran on a separate track. *Kreuzfeld moved to before dawn. Good. Let them hit wire instead of fog for once.*

The name cut through the room. Kreuzfeld was the northbound salvage, the copy set Sylvia had pushed through when the desk went blind. A courier should have cleared on forged customs papers, crossed to the west side, and signaled by eleven. Moving a roster on a Thursday night meant somebody expected movement, not paperwork, and "before dawn" meant whoever had planned that route might already be walking into brighter light than promised.

She found the buffet. Becky's money had bought the panic menu: gorgeous, expensive, too much of it. Anya loaded a small plate and drifted toward a table near the terrace doors. Damian had been absorbed into a group near the bar, dark suits and policy talk and a fog of young-diplomat thoughts trying to sound older than their owners. *Regional framework. Don't say tariff twice. If he asks about my posting, smile.* His thoughts surfaced through the crowd noise at intervals. *Bilateral trade framework, that's safe. Say that. Kirchner still parts his hair the same way. And if Kreuzfeld is tightening tonight, the courier with the forged papers is walking into a search line.*

That last line caught like a hook. She didn't look at him. She took a bite of goat cheese on toast and chewed slowly while the operational clock, which had been background pressure all evening, moved up a notch.

10:17. Forty-three minutes until the check-in that was supposed to tell her the salvage route had worked. Forty-three minutes of donor smiles, alumni chatter, and pretending she wasn't listening for the first sign the night had gone bad.

A waiter offered champagne. She took a glass and didn't drink from it. The glass was a prop, an excuse for her hands. Across the room, a retired diplomat was telling the same customs story he told at every event. Two tables over, an elderly woman was planning the argument she intended to have with her husband when they got home. *If he says one word about my mother, I swear to God...* Anya tuned away before she got the rest.

They were careful with each other in public. They had been careful since the safe house, since the bruised ribs and split eyebrow and the quiet after, since the line they'd crossed and then, by mutual unstated agreement, backed away from without deciding which side they meant to live on. Here, in a room full of people who knew one or both of them, careful was the only option. It also weighed a ton.

Once, near the buffet, their paths crossed. Damian reached for a napkin. Anya reached for a second goat cheese toast.

"The cheese is good," she said.

"I noticed you're eating all of it."

"It's strategically important cheese."

He almost smiled. *Don't smile. You're smiling.* "I'll leave you to your strategy."

He moved away. The almost-smile stayed in her chest longer than it should have, and she hated that, because her brain should have been on the mole, on Kreuzfeld, on the clock, on anything except the exact way his mouth moved when he was trying not to be amused.

Then somebody found Becky's nostalgia trap.

She'd set a side table near the bar with stacked Eden yearbooks, a leather binder of archival photo reprints, and a rented carousel projector clicking school pictures onto the cream wall by the terrace doors. Lila, one of Becky's media contacts and an Eden alumna, had apparently decided this counted as public service.

The wall filled with a yearbook class photo first: thirty children in Eden blazers, squinting at the photographer with expressions ranging from dutiful to mutinous.

"Oh my God," Lila said. "Look at us."

Nostalgia spread fast and got less accurate by the minute. People abandoned drinks to crowd around the table and wall. Somebody flipped through yearbooks. Somebody else dug through the binder. The projector clicked from sports day to the end-of-year ceremony to a class picnic where half the boys looked seasick. Adults laughed at the evidence that they had once been tiny and ridiculous.

Anya watched from the edge of the cluster and felt her stomach tighten.

Erich Kessler materialized at her table with his third beer and the comfortable posture of a man who had found his equilibrium several drinks ago. Erich ran logistics for his family's distribution company. His surface thoughts gave her the rest: resentment over his older brother's recent promotion, and a homesick gratitude for the one place where people had talked to him before they learned his last name mattered.

*Good to be here. Good party. I miss this. I miss when we were all in the same building and nobody'd figured out who was going to matter and who wasn't.*

"Anya." He dropped into the chair across from her, grinning. "From Eden, right? Becky's friend?"

"That's me."

"You look exactly the same." He pointed at his own head. "The hair. I'd recognize it anywhere. Nobody else had pink hair. You were like the one bright flag in the whole school."

*Pink hair. Class photo. Second row. She sat behind me in Mr. Kraft's class.*

"I've been told it's distinctive."

"Distinctive? It's a landmark. I used to navigate the hallways by your head."

She laughed. The laugh was real enough. Erich was harmless enough. For half a minute it was just a party.

Then he leaned over the nostalgia table, pawed through the clear sleeves in the binder, and let out a delighted noise. "No. Becky is evil for this. Lila, put this one up."

The projector clacked. The wall filled with an Eden charity bazaar planning committee from the year Becky had decided twelve-year-olds should save a pediatric ward through sheer force of personality. A folding table vanished under ribbon spools, raffle books, paper flowers, and a dented cash box. Becky stood in the middle pointing at somebody outside the frame like a tiny dictator. Damian was at one end with a clipboard. Anya was at the other with scissors in one hand and a face that clearly objected to the concept of volunteer labor.

She remembered glue drying on her fingers. Becky assigning jobs in a voice even the teachers had obeyed. Damian getting handed the tally sheet because Becky said his handwriting looked trustworthy enough for money.

"Oh, this is incredible," Erich said. He slapped a yearbook open beside the projector and pointed his beer at the wall. "Desmond!"

He called it loudly enough to turn heads. Damian looked over from his conversation.

"Erich." Smile. Measured. "Good to see you."

"Come look at this. Charity committee. She had us doing unpaid labor before puberty."

Damian crossed the room. His thoughts shifted as he moved. *Of course Becky kept this. Of course Erich found it now.*

Erich kept talking, beer-warm and easy, one hand on the yearbook, one on the beer. He looked at the projection. He looked at Anya. He looked down at the caption block under his thumb.

"Wait." His whole face lit with the loose clarity alcohol stripped him down to. "This is the funniest possible thing. Becky has seriously been throwing the same event her whole life." He jabbed the page at Damian. "Look. That's you, that's me, that's Becky, and that's Anya Forger pretending not to murder us over raffle tickets. Same face and everything."

The name landed in the room like a glass hitting tile.

Anya Forger.

A man on his third beer had said it out loud at a party while grieving his own vanished importance and having no idea what he'd just done.

And then something in Damian's mind locked hard enough she felt it in her teeth.

*Forger.*

The yearbook page under Erich's hand. The girl in the photo box in his apartment. The woman six feet away in silk with a champagne glass she hadn't touched.

*Anya Forger.*

Not resemblance. Not the same laugh, the same tilt of the head, the same almost-memory. Her.

Anya's stomach dropped hard enough to feel like a missed step. The photo in Damian's apartment, the one he'd kept tucked away in that childhood box, flashed through her head with humiliating clarity. Becky had done this without knowing she'd done it, built a whole room out of the past and invited them both to stand in it.

His expression didn't change. Twenty years of political upbringing and Desmond discipline held the line. His face produced a half-smile. His hand stayed steady around his glass.

"That was a long time ago," he said, voice perfectly even.

*Don't do this here. Not in Becky's house. Not with cameras.*

Erich was still going. "And Becky still has the same staffing model, apparently. Child labor and threats."

"I think you're reading a yearbook with beer in your eyes," Anya said.

She heard her own voice from a distance, bright and easy and built for exactly this kind of deflection. "This is why archivists shouldn't serve alcohol."

"Maybe," Erich said, squinting down at the caption. *Tiny print. Why is the print this small?* He took a drink. "Or maybe Becky is committing typographic crimes. Same difference."

He moved on. The cluster shifted back toward the wall, the binder, the yearbooks, the harmless pleasure of finding out the tallest boy in class had become the shortest man in the room.

Damian did not move on.

He stood where he'd been, glass untouched.

*Not here. Think first.*

His eyes found hers across the room. Half a second. Less.

Then somebody beside him asked a question about wine. He answered without missing a beat.

*Later.*

Anya set her champagne glass down before she dropped it. The music slid into a waltz. Erich turned another page in the binder. Around them the party kept moving.

She pressed her fingertips into the tablecloth until the weave marked her skin, then headed for the terrace doors. Not fast. Fast would've looked like panic.

Crossing the ballroom took forever. Kirchner had Damian by the cuff again, still talking about customs exemptions with the damp intensity of a man who mistook access for friendship. Becky was laughing at a joke she clearly hated because a camera was pointed at her. Erich had already found another audience for his nostalgia. Everybody else was fully occupied with the ordinary business of a rich party, which made the single locked thought in Damian's head feel even louder.

He looked up once as she slipped toward the doors. His face stayed perfectly diplomatic. The thought under it did not.

*Later. Not here. Think first.*

She kept walking because looking back would count as an answer.

* * *

The terrace was colder than she'd expected. The party burned warm behind the glass doors, but out here the October air had finally given up pretending to be kind. Wide flagstones, wrought-iron railing, a view of the grounds that disappeared into black after about twenty feet. In daylight the garden probably looked expensive. Tonight it looked like a place you could vanish into.

She gripped the railing. The cold metal helped. It was an object, a boundary, a fact.

Damian was still inside, at the edge of her range. His mind kept striking the same points and sliding off them. *Forger.* *Not here.* *If Kreuzfeld tightened before dawn, the courier is already late.*

If border traffic hit now, she'd need a secure line, a reason to vanish, and more than thirty seconds before somebody came looking for her. The bag at her wrist felt heavier than it was.

The pager had not moved yet, but she kept feeling the outline of it against her skin anyway, as if her body already knew the night was about to get worse.

"You look like you're about to climb over."

Becky appeared in the doorway, shoes in one hand, wine in the other. At some point during the evening she'd taken her hair down. The engineered updo had surrendered to gravity and humidity, and the loose curls made her look less like a magazine spread and more like the girl Anya had known since kindergarten.

"I'm fine."

"That's not even convincing fake-fine." Becky came out barefoot and leaned on the railing beside her. "You're doing the thing."

"I'm not doing a thing."

"You're doing the thing where you go quiet and stare at the middle distance like it insulted you personally. You've been doing it since we were six. I know the thing."

*She's not fine. Did someone say something in there? Did Damian say something? If he did, I'll throw him into a hedge.*

Anya said nothing.

They stood for a while. The party murmured on the other side of the glass. Below them, somewhere in the garden, an irrigation system clicked on and started spraying arcs of water across the dark lawn.

"Good event," Anya said at last.

"It's fine." Becky sipped her wine. "The Riesling disaster is over. The shellfish disaster is over. The Brahms disaster is not."

"The Brahms disaster?"

"The quartet keeps playing that one miserable piece that makes donors remember mortality. I asked for upbeat."

"Did you give them sheet music?"

"I gave them a mood board."

"A mood board. For a string quartet."

"It was a very clear mood board."

Inside, the quartet finally gave up on the funeral music and found something quicker.

Becky's thoughts shifted. The traffic about caterers and donors and musicians dropped away. Under it was the heavier thing she'd been carrying all evening, tucked under timing and hostess energy, waiting for a moment when she could stop being watched.

*Just say it. Say it wrong if you have to.*

"Can I tell you one real thing?" Becky asked.

"Yeah."

"You're my best friend." Becky winced before Anya could answer. "No, that's not, I mean obviously that's true, but I didn't come out here just to announce friendship like I'm drunk at prom. I broke up with Marc."

Anya turned toward her. "When?"

"Six days ago. Five. I don't know. Tuesday was in there somewhere and now I can't stop thinking about Tuesdays, which is insane." Becky rubbed at one eye with the heel of her hand. "You know what I do on Tuesdays? When nothing's on the calendar? I eat cereal for dinner. I watch cooking shows. I talk to my cat. That's the real humiliation, by the way. The cat knows everything."

Despite herself, Anya almost smiled. "That is pretty bad."

"I told Marc that once and he did this face like I'd said something adorable on purpose." Becky stared out into the garden. "Then I started talking about that documentary I want to do, the eastern-district one, with the artists nobody covers unless there are famous people in the room, and he said, 'That's great, babe,' and asked what we were doing Saturday."

She took a drink, swallowed, then shook her head.

"No, that's not fair. It is fair. I don't know." Her laugh came out thin and wrong. "He wasn't mean. That's the part making me crazy. He sent orchids on my birthday. He said he was proud of everything I'm building. Building what? A guest list?"

She rubbed the stem of the glass between two fingers and looked annoyed at herself for even needing to explain it. "I kept meaning to call you, and every time I picked up the phone I could hear myself about to do the polished version. The funny version. Like, surprise, rich girl problem, man admired the brand and missed the person. I couldn't stand hearing it come out that way."

The sprinkler clicked through another rotation below them.

"We'd been sleeping together for three months and he still talked about me like he'd skimmed a profile." Becky dragged a hand through her loose curls and stopped there, gripping the back of her neck. "He didn't want me. He wanted the version that gets you into rooms."

The sentence sat between them.

Becky looked down at her wine. "And I almost did it to you just now. Came out here and almost gave you the room version because I don't know how to stop doing it."

The truth pushed at Anya's teeth. She had been hearing the private version since kindergarten.

Her pager vibrated against the inside of Anya's bag, hard enough to tap Becky's bare wrist. Two short pulses. One long. Border channel.

Anya didn't move. Becky went on, quieter now. "You're the only person I don't have to do that for."

She slid her hand into the bag and shielded the tiny screen with her palm. `KZF 2300 MISSED. RETURN SECURE ASAP.`

The railing bit into the scar in her left palm. Becky was still leaned into her shoulder.


# Chapter 13

Anya was already awake when the phone rang at 6:14 on the first morning of November. She had slept an hour at best. It was probably less. Most of the night had gone to encrypted border traffic on her WISE terminal and the neighbor in 4B thinking, *please please please just sleep, I'm begging you, just one hour.* The baby on the other side of the wall kept crying. The mother's thoughts were louder.

She picked up on the second ring. Sylvia got straight to it.

"At approximately 0340, border control at Kreuzfeld was breached. Crane was moving on forged papers when a three-man team intercepted him. They had his route, his timing, and the cover packet. He died on site."

Anya sat on the edge of the bed with the phone hard against her ear. The baby cried again. The neighbor thought, *please, please, this one time,* and then nothing but blank tired static.

"His body has been recovered," Sylvia said. "The documents have not. I have informed his wife."

Crane. David Crane. Parking space, three months ago. "Chief," every single time. Forty-one. Wife, no kids. He kept border stamps in an Altoids tin in his glove compartment because supply never had the ones he wanted when he wanted them. He had spent the last six weeks running eastern courier lanes Anya had helped make possible.

Three weeks ago she had filed Damian's network as political vanity with a useful pedigree attached. She had written that the chatter hanging off his eastern contacts was posture, not live logistics. She had cut the note about customs clerks and freight schedules. She had downgraded Renewal Front movement on the eastern corridors from active concern into a duller, safer category. WISE moved the Desmond-adjacent traffic off hot watch and into periodic review. Duplicate eyes came off Kreuzfeld. Courier lanes were rebuilt around the thinner coverage picture. Crane drew one of the replacement runs.

That did not mean her report put rifles in anybody's hands. It meant the hole existed. Somebody with routing access had used it.

"There is another development," Sylvia said. "At approximately 0300, an SSS tactical unit executed a search warrant on a residential address in eastern Berlint. The location matches an address from our analytical review last week. The warrant was specific. They knew what they were looking for."

Damian's cache. The apartment. The shelves arranged by region. The false wall behind the kitchen cabinet. The coffee stain near the baseboard that he had left because replacing the carpet meant letting the landlord inside. She had laughed when he said it. He had not.

"The material was seized intact," Sylvia said. "Analysis is pending. The eastern sector is compromised."

Compromised. Broken. Burned open. Gone.

"I have questions about your reporting timeline," Sylvia said. "We will address them at 0800. Attend in person."

"Yes."

Sylvia went quiet for half a second. Not uncertainty. Just the small stop between the work and the next piece of work.

"Crane was a good officer," she said. "We will debrief in the morning."

The line went dead.

Anya stayed where she was. The neighbor in 4B adjusted the baby and thought, *okay, okay, maybe like this,* and the baby screamed harder. The thought blew apart into exhausted misery.

She called Damian.

He picked up at once. "I'm aware," he said. His voice was controlled, too even, and she could hear how hard he was working at it. "My contact in the SSS reached me twenty minutes ago. They took everything."

"How specific?"

"They went to the false wall first. Then the bedroom floorboards. No wasted motion."

Anya shut her eyes.

"Crane is dead," she said.

A pause. "Who?"

"WISE field officer. He was carrying a courier packet through Kreuzfeld."

"I'm sorry."

He meant it. That was the problem. He did not know what he was apologizing for. He did not know she had handed WISE a cleaner version of him than the truth. He did not know Crane's run had existed inside the coverage picture that lie helped build. He did not know the dead space between what she filed and what she knew had finally filled up with a body.

"There's more," Damian said. "Mertens called an hour ago."

The name hit and brought the rest with it. A ministry official. Careful. Nervous. Two daughters. Saturday football. Photocopies in a gym bag, left in the back seat of his car in the ministry garage because that was the blind spot nobody bothered to search.

"He's out," Damian said. "Told me not to call again. He thinks the SSS got the address through his department's records. He thinks they will come for him next."

"Will they?"

"I don't know. If the files were taken intact, they'll find his name soon enough. I tried to tell him I could arrange an exit. He hung up before I got there."

Anya stood and went to the kitchen window without deciding to. Morning had turned the glass the color of dishwater. She thought of Crane's parking space. Mertens in a ministry garage with a gym bag. The report she had put in Sylvia's hands. She had drawn a line around Damian and called it strategy. Everything outside that line had paid for it.

*Please just sleep,* the neighbor thought. *Please.*

"Do you think the raid and Kreuzfeld were the same operation?" she asked.

"I think they were two precise hits inside forty minutes," Damian said. "I think both were tied to the same work. The rest is guesswork."

"A good guess."

"Yes." He exhaled. "Still guesswork."

That helped and did not help.

"We need to meet," Anya said. "Not the usual place."

"I know somewhere."

She hung up, stripped off her T-shirt, and stepped into the shower. The water never got fully hot. She let it hit the back of her neck anyway. On the tram west, a man in a wool scarf thought about invoice numbers, a woman in nurse's shoes thought about whether she had time to shave her legs before tonight, and somebody near the back kept replaying *did you lock the cabinet, did you lock the cabinet, did you lock the cabinet* until Anya wanted to turn around and shake him. The city had made room for dawn and deliveries and normal resentment without asking anybody's permission. Crane stayed dead inside it just fine.

The printing shop block was still half asleep. Someone inside was already running a press. The sound came through the walls in steady mechanical shudders.

&nbsp;

* * *

&nbsp;

The basement was underneath the shop, reached through a service door beside a dumpster that smelled like wet ink and cardboard. Damian had a key. He did not explain how. The stairs were steep concrete. By the halfway mark, the railing had leached enough heat from her palm that she shoved both hands into her coat pockets.

The room below was about fifteen feet by twenty, all concrete except for a steel folding table, four mismatched chairs, and one fluorescent tube on the ceiling. Damp had browned the far wall in vertical streaks. Damian was already there with his elbows on the table and his hands folded loose, coat still on, as if he had been holding the same position for an hour. The burn mark near his left cuff stood out darker than the rest of the wool.

She pulled out the chair and sat. Neither of them spoke.

Above them, someone in the shop dragged a paper crate across the floor and let it drop. The thud came down through the ceiling, dull and blunt. Damian kept looking at the table.

*Too static. Stupid. Should've moved it. If they took the little ledger, Mertens is finished.*

There was a pale coffee ring on the table, half rubbed away. Anya put two fingers beside it and kept them there.

"They went to the false wall first," Damian said at last. "Straight there. No search pattern. No hesitation."

"So the warrant wasn't just specific."

"No."

"Mertens still thinks the address came through ministry records?"

"He thinks someone pulled an internal property file connected to his department. He thinks the SSS is going to —" Damian's voice went flat. "He thinks they're coming for him. He's probably right. I don't have anything useful to say to that."

Anya nodded.

"Crane was carrying forged driver papers through Kreuzfeld," she said. "The team that hit him knew the exact lane, the exact time, and the cover packet. That did not come off the street."

Damian's gaze dropped to her hand by the coffee ring.

"Then somebody inside your routing handed them the window."

"Yes."

Another thud upstairs. Someone laughed immediately after, bright and stupid and normal.

Damian rubbed his thumb against the side of his index finger. "They had my apartment. They had your courier window. Both before sunrise, and I keep —" He stopped. "I keep trying to make that two separate things and I can't."

"The coordinated part is still a guess," Anya said.

"It doesn't feel like a guess."

"Still a guess."

He looked up at that. The fluorescent light flattened the color out of his face.

"Yeah," he said. "Still a guess."

There it was. Not agreement. Discipline. The difference mattered and she hated that it mattered when Crane was dead.

"Mertens is going to be okay," she said.

He looked at her.

She heard how bad it sounded too late. "No. I don't know that. I just..." She swallowed. "I know he mattered to you."

A drip hit the floor somewhere behind her. Slow. Then another.

"He has two daughters," Damian said. "That's the part I can't stop thinking about."

Anya nodded because anything else would have been a lie.

"Tell me about Crane."

The question landed hard enough that she almost laughed. Not because it was funny. Because a man was dead and Damian still wanted him to be a person, not a line item. That almost broke her.

"He called everybody chief," she said. "Even Sylvia, once. He used too much powdered creamer in bad coffee and then pretended he liked it. He whistled when he was waiting at checkpoints, but only the same three bars because he couldn't remember how the song went after that. He could spot a bad stamp on forged papers faster than anyone I know." She swallowed. "He was forty-one."

Damian said nothing.

"He kept spare seals in his car because he never trusted supply. He had one story about a checkpoint dog that he told every time somebody new got assigned east. He always got the middle wrong and the ending right."

The room held it. Upstairs, someone cursed about ink coverage. Somewhere in the pipes a radiator knocked once and gave up.

"He was on a replacement run," Anya said.

Damian's face did not change, but the silence tightened.

"Replacement for what?"

She rubbed at the heel of her palm with her thumb, hard enough to feel the old scar pull. "For coverage we cut."

He waited.

She could have stopped there. She did not.

"I filed the east-sector picture wrong," she said. "I made it smaller than it was. I said it was ego and old men talking war after dessert. I pushed the customs piece and the freight piece down the stack. WISE thinned the eastern watch after that. They opened substitute lanes through crossings they thought were quieter." Her throat felt scraped raw. "Crane took one."

Damian's hands stayed folded. That restraint felt worse than if he had stood up and shouted.

"That's what I can prove," Anya said. "I can prove my report changed the coverage picture. I can prove somebody inside had the new window. The rest is me looking at a dead man and wanting the line to point somewhere clean."

He inhaled slowly through his nose.

"Then don't make it cleaner than it is."

She looked at him.

"Your report helped build the lane," he said. "That doesn't mean you briefed the men waiting on it."

"You don't know that."

"No." His voice went flatter. "Neither do you."

She looked back down at the coffee ring. He was right. He was annoyingly, relentlessly right about her in all the places that had nothing to do with telepathy.

*She's going to take the whole thing into herself if I let her.*

That one landed with force and no new information. She still hated it.

"At the reception," she said, "I caught a fragment about the mole and I let it slide because I couldn't place it. Then I burned two days on the wrong analyst."

"Which analyst?"

She shook her head too fast. "Doesn't matter now."

It mattered. He knew it mattered. Her evasion hung there between them like a smell.

"It matters if you're still guessing at the wrong people," he said.

"I know."

"Do you."

It was not a challenge in his public voice. It was quieter than that, and more exhausted.

Anya opened her mouth, closed it, tried again. "I know I missed it."

"That isn't the same sentence."

Above them, somebody dropped a stack of paper. The slap of it came through the ceiling in a flat burst. Anya almost laughed again, this time because the universe apparently intended to keep humiliating her until she went numb.

"My network is burned," Damian said after a moment. "Your eastern routing is burned. The mole is still —" He rubbed his face with one hand. "Mertens has two kids. I can't even tell him what's coming."

"Yes."

"So if we're still doing this, the shape changes."

She looked up.

"No more fixed caches," he said. "No more apartments holding paper. No more couriers either of us hasn't looked in the eye."

"That's not much of a network."

"It's what's left."

"What about your people?"

He almost smiled and failed. "I have fewer people than I did at dawn."

The drip behind her slowed. Stopped. Started again.

"If I have anything for you, I bring it," Damian said. "If you have anything for me, you bring it. Nothing sits still long enough to be photographed, copied, or sold."

Anya nodded once. "Okay."

"And if Mertens calls again, I answer once. After that, nothing in writing."

"Okay."

He looked at her for a beat too long, not speaking.

*Don't ask now. Not like this.*

He pulled off his coat and held it out across the table. "Take this."

"I'm fine."

"You're shaking."

"I'm cold."

"Yes," he said. "That's why."

She did not take it. After a second he laid it on the table between them, the sleeve falling over part of the coffee ring.

"I have to be at WISE in half an hour," she said.

"Then go."

It should have sounded like dismissal. It did not. It sounded like the thing you said when there was no useful version of stay.

She stood. So did he.

"What do we actually have left?" she asked.

His gaze flicked to the coat, then to the concrete floor, then back to her face.

"Direct contact," he said. "You and me. That's it."

Anya nodded once and moved past him toward the stairs. When the service door banged shut behind her, his coat was still spread across the table.

She made 0800 with three minutes to spare.

&nbsp;

* * *

&nbsp;

The WISE east annex smelled like wet wool, radiator dust, and coffee that had died sometime before dawn. A security clerk with a razor nick on his chin checked her badge and thought about mustard. Somewhere behind the inner door, two analysts were arguing out loud about a border map while one of them thought, *if you say contour line one more time I will put this pencil through your hand.* Nobody sounded like a building where a man had died an hour ago. The noise of ordinary irritation almost made her sick.

Sylvia was waiting in Briefing Room B with the blinds half closed. A route map had been pinned to the far board. On the table sat a thin gray folder, a stack of copied manifests, and one black-and-white checkpoint photo turned face down. Sylvia did not stand when Anya came in.

"Sit down," she said.

Anya sat.

Sylvia pushed the folder across the table. "Open page three."

It was her assessment from three weeks ago. Clean typescript. Her name at the top. The lie sat there in flat bureaucratic language, smaller than the damage it had done.

Desmond-adjacent freight and customs chatter assessed as aspirational political signaling, not evidence of live operational control over eastern logistics.

Anya did not need to read the rest. She had built the sentence herself.

"Read the next line," Sylvia said.

Anya obeyed.

Associated Renewal Front reporting to remain under periodic review pending independent corroboration. No immediate rerouting recommended on current evidence.

Sylvia waited until Anya looked up again.

"This assessment entered the system as analysis, not source color," she said. "Once it did, eastern desk reclassified the Desmond-adjacent corridor from red watch to amber review. Duplicate surveillance at Kreuzfeld, Seebruck, and Langenau was reduced. Support coverage was reassigned north. Replacement courier windows were generated from the new threat picture." She touched the manifest stack with two fingers. "Crane drew the 0340 Kreuzfeld window."

There it was. Not guilt. Process. Not feeling. Chain.

Anya stared at the paper until the words blurred and came back.

"What did you have when you wrote this?" Sylvia asked.

"Fragments."

"From where."

"I can't give you a clean source line."

"That is not what I asked."

Anya heard the unspoken line under it anyway. *Do not hide behind tradecraft now.*

"Social access," Anya said. "Conversations. Indirect material. Nothing I could cite cleanly."

"Enough to think the chatter was live?"

"Yes."

Sylvia's face did not change. That made it worse.

"Enough to say so at the time?"

Anya looked back at the report.

"Yes."

Silence filled the room. Outside the glass, a junior officer hurried past holding two mugs and thought, *don't drop it, don't drop it,* with the absolute emotional commitment of someone whose whole life briefly was not dropping coffee.

"Why didn't you," Sylvia said.

The question was small. It landed like a punch.

Anya's first answer came out wrong. "I thought if we tagged him active we would blow the only line we had into that circle."

Sylvia kept waiting.

Anya hated that waiting. It left too much room for truth to move.

"I told myself I was preserving access," she said. "I told myself I was buying time."

"By distorting an assessment."

"Yes."

"For how long did you know it was distorted."

There was no version of this that did not sound like rot. "When I filed it."

Sylvia looked at her for a long second.

*You idiot. You absolute, gifted idiot.*

That one did not come through Sylvia's face. It came through clean and bright and vanished so fast Anya almost thought she imagined it.

"Then hear the chain as it currently stands," Sylvia said. Her voice had gone flatter, not colder. "Your report changed the board. Falk's desk signed the downgrade into active routing guidance. Eastern watch thinned the corridor. Replacement windows were created. Someone with access to one of those windows handed Crane's packet to the team waiting at Kreuzfeld. Those are the facts available this morning. I will not let you turn the rest into certainty because certainty is easier to punish."

Anya swallowed.

"And Desmond's apartment."

"Specific warrant. Specific enough to suggest either prior interior knowledge or a records leak on the Ostanian side." Sylvia tapped the face-down checkpoint photo instead of looking at it. "The timing suggests coordination. The channels do not prove it. Ministry records could explain the address. Our reroute explains Kreuzfeld. One operation could have reached both. Two separate leaks could have landed on the same night. Until we know otherwise, treat both possibilities as live."

That was the answer Damian had given in different words. The same ugly shape, twice from two different directions.

"Do you believe Falk signed those changes because he was exploited," Anya asked, "or because he is the mole?"

Sylvia's gaze sharpened.

"I believe Gerald Falk touches half the eastern paperwork because that is his job." The line was clipped enough to count as a warning. "If you are making an accusation, make one with evidence."

"I'm not."

*Not yet,* thought the part of her that Sylvia did not need telepathy to read.

Sylvia leaned back in her chair. "Good. Then be useful instead."

She turned the checkpoint photo over.

Crane stood beside a border kiosk in bad lighting, coat zipped to the throat, one hand lifted halfway to his ear as if whoever took the picture had caught him mid-annoyance. Somebody had circled the truck lane behind him in blue grease pencil. Another photo beneath it showed the same patch of gravel later, darker in one long spill beside the curb.

Anya looked away first.

"By 1700," Sylvia said, "I want every omitted note, every downgraded contact, every crossing affected by this assessment, and every reroute that followed from it. Names if you have them. Desk handoffs if you can trace them. If you touched it, I want the exact shape of the damage."

"Yes."

"Nothing leaves your desk today without me seeing it."

"Yes."

"And Anya."

She looked up.

Sylvia's expression had not softened. That made the next part legible precisely because it did not.

"Do not waste the day performing grief at me. Work."

The route analysis room had twelve terminals and air that never seemed fully used by the people breathing it. Anya took the station nearest the dead radiator because nobody else wanted it. The screen took too long to wake up. While it loaded, the analyst across from her thought about whether a second breakfast counted as a moral failure if the first one had been half a banana eaten standing up. Somebody at the copier worried his wife had stopped loving him because she no longer corrected his tie. The ordinary traffic of private nonsense moved through her skull with the same rights as anything else. She wanted silence badly enough to bite the inside of her cheek.

She opened her own assessment first. Then the routing chain behind it.

One lie turned into forms faster than grief turned into sense.

Her report at 09:14.

Eastern desk note, twenty-three minutes later: Desmond-associated freight chatter reclassified amber pending corroboration.

Coverage review that afternoon: duplicate watch at Kreuzfeld no longer operationally justified under current threat picture.

Support transfer the next morning: shadow-car allocation moved north to Nordhafen.

Courier redistribution approved that evening.

Falk's initials sat on two of the three approvals in neat blue script.

Not proof. Just proximity. Too much proximity.

She opened Crane's packet.

Alias: Erwin Baer.

Cover: sugar-delivery subcontractor.

Crossing: Kreuzfeld, lane three.

Window: 03:36 to 03:44.

Support column: none.

Below that sat a field note from two nights earlier, clipped and irritated in exactly the way Crane had been clipped and irritated in person.

Papers serviceable. Guard on lane three still lazy about axle seals. Whoever typed the manifest needs a new ribbon because this one looks like shit.

He had signed it D.C., because everyone knew it was him.

Anya stared at the initials until they stopped meaning anything.

She pulled up her original rough notes from the Desmond file. The sentence she had removed was still there in an earlier draft: contact cluster suggests possible touchpoints with customs scheduling and rail freight timing across east crossings. She had highlighted it once, then deleted it from the final because saying it aloud inside WISE would have brought too much heat too fast. That had been the argument. That had been the shape of the self-justification. Preserve access. Avoid blowing the line. Stay useful longer.

Crane had died inside longer.

A junior analyst stopped at her desk with a stack of memos and thought, *wrong station, wrong station, wrong station,* before realizing she was not the person he wanted. He mumbled an apology and kept moving. Anya nodded without hearing him.

She kept digging.

The downgraded corridor had not only changed courier assignments. It had changed which anomalies triggered immediate review and which ones got rolled into end-of-day summaries. Two customs names she had shoved into a side note rather than the main threat lane never hit urgent follow-up. A flagged fuel invoice sat unanswered for forty-eight hours because the desk handling it was no longer working under red-watch rules. The whole thing was so boring on paper it almost offended her. Nothing in the chain looked like murder. It looked like staffing. It looked like bureaucracy. It looked like a set of adult decisions made in polite language until somebody bled out on gravel.

By noon her coffee had gone cold untouched. By one she had three pages of reroutes, support cancellations, and downgraded anomaly notices. By two she had a list of names she had minimized, a list of crossings affected, and an ugly little cluster in the middle where Falk's desk touched everything without yet touching enough.

She took the printouts to Sylvia's office.

Sylvia read standing up.

"Falk signed these two," Anya said. "The downgrade and the support transfer."

"Yes," Sylvia said.

"That is too close."

"It is close." Sylvia set the pages down. "It is not yet narrow."

Anya hated that she was right.

"Mertens may explain the apartment," Anya said. "If the ministry side leaked the address, and Falk touched the routing, then it could've been two people hitting the same network from different sides."

"Possible."

"Or Falk handed the routing out and somebody else took advantage of the panic on the Ostanian side."

"Also possible."

"That is not very useful."

"Neither is false certainty." Sylvia picked up the pages again. "Keep separating the known from the guessed."

The rebuke landed because it was deserved. Anya stood there anyway.

"If you had to make the call now," she said, "would you pull me off the desk?"

Sylvia took longer than she needed to answer.

"If I wanted to punish you, yes," she said. "If I want the damage mapped before whoever did this moves again, no." She set the pages down for the second time. "So I am not punishing you today. I am using you. Try to deserve the distinction."

That should have felt merciful. It did not.

Anya went back to the terminal and kept working until the words on the screen turned liquid around the edges. At 16:40 she sent Sylvia the first clean reconstruction: the lie she filed, the downgrade it triggered, the coverage cuts that followed, the specific window Crane inherited, the points where uncertainty still started. She left the building after dark with the route map burned behind her eyes and the smell of dead coffee in her coat.

Damian let himself into the apartment after dark and pulled the box from the closet shelf. The spelling certificate was still curled at the corners. The dry pen that never worked. The athletics ribbon. The festival program. The Eden class photo.

Second row, third from the left. Pink hair. Rumpled collar. Becky's sentence at the gala had been enough. The photo only finished it.

Anya Forger.

He slid the photo back under the program, dropped the basement key into the kitchen drawer with the dead batteries, and shut the box. No more fixed rooms. If anything passed between them now, it passed hand to hand. He'd keep working with her. He just wasn't doing it blind anymore.

Rain tapped once against the shut window. The apartment stayed cold.


# Chapter 14

Three days after David Crane died at the Kreuzfeld border checkpoint, Anya stood outside Loid and Yor's apartment with her key in her hand. Loid was home on a Tuesday, radio low behind the door, while Yor moved in the kitchen and Bond's nails clicked across the floor toward her. Home smelled like rice steam and dish soap. Sylvia's voice cut through it anyway. *We lost Crane at the checkpoint.* The floodlights. The broken lane. The gap in coverage Anya had helped cut open with a lie.

She should've come the first morning. Instead WISE had sealed up everyone who'd touched Kreuzfeld: mirrored phones, movement logs, outside contact requests shoved through desks the mole might already own.
