If she'd walked that scrutiny to the Forger apartment, she would've brought the leak to their door. Tonight the summit scramble had finally cracked the hold long enough for her to get here. If she waited longer, tomorrow happened without them.

She let herself in.

Loid was at the sink in an apron, sleeves pushed to the elbows. Yor had the refrigerator door open. Bond hit Anya at the knees hard enough to make her stumble, his thoughts arriving in three blunt beats. *Home. Her. Good.* For one second nobody had turned and she saw the evening as it had been before she came through the door: dinner half-finished, radio muttering, the kind of ordinary hour Crane would never get back.

"Anya!" Yor shut the refrigerator too fast. "You should've called. Have you eaten? Sit down. No, wait, you're freezing. Sit first."

Loid turned. His face stayed easy. The check behind it did not.

*She came alone. No warning. Her hands. How bad.*

"How's work?" he asked.

It was what a father asked in a kitchen. It was also *tell me what room we're in*.

"Papa." Her voice came out scraped thin. "I need to talk to you."

The reading glasses came off his head. That was the only large movement.

"They killed Crane," she said.

The radio died in the middle of a weather report. Loid had crossed the room and switched it off before she registered him moving. Yor pulled out a chair.

"Sit," Loid said. Then, before she could, "Were you followed?"

"I don't think so." Anya sat because her knees had already started to shake. Bond pressed against her leg. "WISE put everybody from Kreuzfeld on internal hold. Phones copied, exits watched, no outside contact unless it went through review. If I came sooner, I brought a tail here."

Loid didn't nod. "And tonight?"

"Credential rush for the summit. Everybody running in circles. I got a clean hour and took it."

Yor set water in front of her and touched her wrist once. "All right. Tell us."

Anya wrapped both hands around the glass and forced herself not to look at the tremor in the water.

"Crane was on a courier line tied to summit prep. Not official prep. Off-book procurement, access schedules, movement that shouldn't have been riding the same route. Somebody burned the checkpoint before dawn and the line went dark with him." She swallowed. "It wasn't only Crane. The same morning, somebody raided one of Damian Desmond's caches. One of his people went underground. Whoever's building tomorrow wanted both sides blind before lunch."

That got Loid's eyes up in a different way.

"The ministry diplomat."

"Yes."

Yor looked between them. "The summit is tomorrow."

"Yes." Anya made herself keep going. "And it's not a pressure tactic anymore. It's the stage. Opening session. Cameras already live. Delegations trapped in place. If they can make the first footage read Westalian, correction won't matter for the first hour and maybe the day after."

Loid leaned one hand on the table. "What part of that do you know and what part are you building from fragments?"

"Know: opening session, public blame, help from inside the building. Strong read: gunfire first, something camera-clear, with an internal relay feeding the floor." She met his eyes because anything else would look weaker than the truth. "Voss is close enough to matter. He didn't build this to miss the first five minutes."

"How much of this has WISE?"

"Enough to panic. Not enough to see the shape." She took a breath. "And I wasn't sending home contact through a channel I don't trust."

Loid held the line a second longer. *No sleep. Guilt first, then tradecraft. Too much of both.* "That's the whole reason?"

"No." The word hurt on the way out. "The hold was the real blocker. After that..." She looked down at the water. "After that I kept thinking I'd come here with something solid instead of fear. That part was me."

It wasn't absolution. It was only accurate.

Yor had gone very still. Not shocked. Recognizing.

*Dominic's office. The manifests. The watermark.*

"I have something," she said, and she was already in the hall before either of them answered.

Loid looked after her, then back at Anya. "You should've taken the first clean window."

"I know."

Yor came back with a thick manila envelope and set it on the table between the glasses and the soy sauce. Her hands were steady by effort.

"Karl Dominic," she said. "The broker from last month. After I killed him I photographed everything on his desk because the papers felt wrong and I knew if I left with only a feeling I'd talk myself out of it by morning."

She opened the envelope. Grainy photographs slid over the wood: shipping manifests, procurement records, a photographed purchase order with the Garden watermark at the top and three completed eliminations listed below it.

Loid picked up the manifests first. His eyes moved fast.

*Voss. Civilian intermediaries. Defense procurement. Supply chain.*

"Where did he get these?" Loid asked.

"He was holding them, or moving them, or both." Yor touched the page with one finger. "This mark is Garden. Internal. Not city hall paper, not ministry. Ours."

Anya looked at the cleanup sheet again. Three dates. Three names. Neat entries beside rubber-stamped death.

"The Garden took contracts for them," Yor said. "Not officially. Through cutouts. They marked people in the weapons chain as liabilities and sent me after them."

Behind the words sat the one she wasn't saying: *I killed them and never checked who was paying.*

Loid's hand stopped on the Garden sheet. "How many?"

"Three here. More in the photographed records. I don't have the full count."

"And how long have you known?"

"Since Dominic." Yor folded her hands together once, then forced them apart. "Long enough to know I'm not going back."

He didn't rush her.

"If they send a handler?" he asked.

"I don't answer."

"If they leave instructions."

"I don't take them."

That quieted the room more completely than Crane's name had.

Yor looked at Anya first, then at Loid. "I'll help with the summit. But not as Thorn Princess. Not as their asset. As Yor. As family."

She didn't dress it up. She just set it on the table with the photographs.

Loid reached across and covered her hand with his. "All right," he said.

Yor turned her hand under his and held on.

Anya looked down at Bond's head against her knee. His fur smelled like dust and soap and the apartment itself. Safe things. Crane had had safe things too.

Loid let one breath pass, then another, and went back to the table because that was what he did when the wall was already on top of them.

"Your documents corroborate the procurement chain," he said to Yor. Then to Anya: "Your summit read gives it a clock. We still need floor placement, leadership seating, and whatever Voss thinks his deniability looks like tomorrow."

"Damian can get that," Anya said.

The kitchen changed again.

Loid leaned back a fraction. "Why him."

"Because he's already in it." She kept her eyes on the cleanup sheet until that felt cowardly and she had to look up. "His cache got hit, his line's burned, and he can get closer to Voss than I can. If we shut him out, he walks into tomorrow blind and we lose half the floor."

Yor asked quietly, "Do you trust him?"

"Not cleanly." Anya could feel Loid sharpen at that. "Enough that when he tells me Voss will stand somewhere, I believe him."

Loid's face gave away nothing. His thoughts did.

*Useful. Risky. Young. Attached to her in some fashion. Deal with summit first.*

Anya wanted the floorboards to open.

"If he comes here," Loid said, "he comes because we need what he knows."

"That's why I'm asking."

He held her gaze one second longer, then nodded once. "Text him."

Anya sent the message under the table because it made her feel less exposed even though both of them obviously knew what she was doing.

`Need you at my parents'. Tonight. Summit.`

The reply came back fast enough to sting.

`On my way.`

Loid saw the screen light. "Fast."

"He was awake."

"Of course he was," Loid said.

Yor stood because she needed something to do with her hands. "People think worse on an empty stomach," she said. "I know that's not the biggest problem in the room, but it's still true."

Nobody argued.

She put rice and vegetables on the table. Anya got a few bites down because Yor was watching and because Loid said, "Two more," in the exact tone that made it sound medical. No one tried to make the silence friendly. Loid read the manifests while he ate standing up. Anya kept catching pieces anyway.

*Whiteboard. Venue map. Clean paper.*

*Knife case in the hall closet. Flats, not heels.*

*Eat. Keep chewing. Don't think about the checkpoint. Don't think about Crane in the cold.*

She put her chopsticks down before she snapped them.

Loid noticed immediately. "Enough?"

She nodded.

The kitchen changed by stages. Plates stacked. The sauce bottle moved aside. Loid swung the whiteboard off the wall and leaned it against the refrigerator. A venue map went up with two fruit magnets. Yor's photographs lined the table. Bond followed the motion with grave interest, then parked himself beneath the map as if he had seniority.

"Opening session is still the obvious window," Loid said. He marked the south terrace doors, the east service corridor, and the west leadership row. "They need spectacle and attribution before anybody can correct it."

"The south side gives them both," Yor said. She hadn't touched her tea. "Sight lines, panic, glass."

"And a fast exit if the outside team knows the grounds," Loid said.

Anya caught the next piece before he spoke it, the route lines already built in his head.

*If Yor starts on the terrace and they come through the corridor first, she loses the angle.*

"Foyer junction," Anya said. "Mama starts there."

Both of them looked at her.

Too fast. Again.

Loid asked, "Why."

"Because the terrace is too exposed from the start."

"That isn't quite an answer."

She gripped the back of the chair. "Because the junction holds both lines until contact."

Yor measured the distance on the map with two fingers. *Thirty-five if the shoes are wrong. Less in flats.* Anya heard it and blurted before she could stop herself.

"Go in flats first."

Yor's head came up.

Anya wanted very sincerely to stop talking in front of other people forever.

"I mean for running," she said too quickly.

Yor held her eyes for one long beat, then went back to the map. "Yes. Flats first."

Loid's pen hovered. *How is she there before I say it. Pattern. Later.* Then he tapped the south side again. "Even in flats, she loses the terrace if the first burst comes from outside."

The room tightened. They shifted the route once, then again. Loid pushed Yor toward the terrace. Yor pushed herself back inside. The arrows multiplied. Nothing got cleaner.

Bond stood up so abruptly his nails scraped the floor.

Yor half turned. "Bond?"

He crossed the room fast, faster than an old dog with stiff hips had any right to move, and planted himself in front of the refrigerator. He barked once and jammed his nose against the lower edge of the map.

Anya was already reading him.

Low wall. Wet stone. Grass crushed under hard shoes. Dark clothes moving fast along the south edge. Light on glass. Heat. Noise.

Bond barked again, sharper this time.

Loid followed the line of his muzzle. "South perimeter."

The spot Bond had pinned was outside the south terrace where the landscaped grounds narrowed near the vehicle approach.

"That's real," Anya said.

Loid looked at her, then at Bond, then back to the map. A different memory flickered through him. *Government event. Weeks ago. She pushed me off a bad line without saying how.* He didn't ask. He circled the south perimeter in red.

Yor came up beside him and measured again. "Then I don't start on the terrace. I start inside, stay out of sight, and move south on contact. First man at the bend before the terrace line sees me."

Loid nodded once. "Better."

Bond pressed against the refrigerator, vision already fading into ordinary dog distress. Anya crouched and put her arms around his neck.

"Good boy," she said into his fur. "I know."

Loid capped the red marker with a click. "You're not on first contact," he said without looking at her.

Anya stood. "I know that."

"You're not on it at all."

"Papa."

Now he looked at her.

"No," he said. "I need one variable in this apartment that doesn't get shot at."

The sentence landed harder because he said it like a handler and a father at the same time and didn't bother to separate the two.

"You need somebody on the floor who can tell when the room turns," Anya said. "Not after. Before."

"Somebody does not have to be you."

"I know the players better than anybody you're going to put in a corridor."

"That still doesn't make you expendable."

Yor set her mug down very carefully. "She's not saying expendable."

"Then what is she saying."

"That if the room shifts before the shooting starts," Yor said, "she'll catch it faster than I will from a junction and faster than you will from a relay."

Loid's jaw tightened. *No open floor. Not after Crane. Find another angle.*

The bell rang before he answered.

Yor got there first. "Hello. You must be Damian. Come in. It's awful out there."

"Thank you, Mrs. Forger."

"Yor," she said at once. "Mrs. Forger sounds like someone who sends complaint letters."

That pulled a surprised breath of laughter out of him before he could stop it.

He stepped into the kitchen damp from the rain, coat dark at the shoulders, hair flattened at the temples. Bond inspected his shoes, circled once, and sat squarely on his feet with the solemnity of an official seal. Damian froze. The first thought Anya caught came too fast and too scrubbed.

*Tea. Maps. Don't look at the hall. Don't think Eden. Summit first.*

"Hi," she said.

"Hi."

Loid came out of the hall wiping his hands on the apron. "Damian. I'm Loid."

The handshake lasted the exact right length. Loid's smile stayed easy. Behind it the inventory clicked into place.

*Good suit. No aide. No sleep. Looked at her first. Personal.*

Anya considered spontaneous death as a practical option.

"Before we go any further," Loid said, "tell me why you're here."

Damian kept one hand on the back of the chair. "Because the same people who killed Crane hit my side before lunch. My cache is gone. Reinhardt's underground. If I keep pretending tomorrow is still a political event with a security problem, more people die."

Loid said nothing.

Damian set his phone on the table beside the photographs. "And because Anya asked."

There it was. Awful. Direct. Impossible to take back.

Loid's thoughts sharpened. *Too honest on the second point. Good. Or reckless.* "If I think you're protecting your own position at her expense, this conversation gets short."

"Fair," Damian said.

Yor, apparently satisfied that the men had completed whatever strange test counted for them, pushed a reheated plate toward him. "Eat if you can. Operations improve when people chew."

Damian looked down at Bond pinning his shoes to the floor, then at the plate, then at the war map on the refrigerator. A different thought rose up, unguarded enough for Anya to catch.

*Fruit magnets on a kill board. Of course this is her house.*

It hit her wrong and almost made her laugh.

Loid didn't give the room time to settle. "Walk us through the parts we don't have."

Damian stepped to the map. The wet young man in the doorway disappeared under the diplomatic polish and the work underneath it.

"Security tightened after the border hit. Too much, too fast," he said. "Voss stays close, but not on the floor. West Gallery. That's where he can watch the hall and still claim distance if it goes bad."

"Can you get near him?" Loid asked.

"Yes, if I look like I'm there to warn him, not expose him."

"Will he buy that?"

"He wants to."

Yor tapped Bond's red circle. "If south is real, I take the foyer junction and move on contact."

"Agreed," Damian said. "If the first shots come from outside, the panic drives inward. That buys the relay seconds."

Loid uncapped a black marker. "Communications room is still my problem."

"Not the main room," Damian said. "Too watched. Secondary technical station off the east service corridor. If somebody's feeding the shooters live updates, I'd start there."

Anya caught the next piece as he thought it, neat and compressed.

*During seating or I lose him.*

"During seating," she said.

Damian looked at her. Loid looked at both of them.

"Why seating," Loid asked.

"Because once speeches start, everybody locks forward," Anya said. "If Voss is pretending to be informal, he'll only do it while the room is still shifting."

Damian let out one breath. "Yes. That's better."

Loid drew a short black line off the east corridor and didn't comment on how fast she'd gotten there.

"Then Anya stays out of the first break," he said. "Diplomatic annex, back row."

"No," Damian said.

The word came too fast. He corrected a fraction too late. "Not the annex."

The kitchen went still enough that even Bond lifted his head.

Loid's voice stayed mild. "Why not."

"Because annex seating goes through ministry roster review now. They tightened it this afternoon." Damian kept his eyes on the map. "East press is cleaner. Better sight line, separate entrance, cleaner exit if the room breaks."

Anya stared at him. He would not look at her.

Loid folded his arms. "You can get her press."

"Yes."

"At what cost."

Damian gave the smallest lift of one shoulder. "Enough."

"Why spend it on her."

There was father in the question now, not hidden well enough to miss.

Damian held his gaze. "Because she's already on too many lines in this operation. I can move one of them."

That wasn't the whole answer. It was honest enough to leave a mark.

Yor glanced between them. "That sounds expensive."

"It is," Damian said.

"Can you afford it?"

He gave a tired breath that wasn't quite a laugh. "No. But I can spend it anyway."

Loid studied him one beat too long, then nodded once. "Do it."

Damian stepped into the hall and made the call where the rain on his coat could drip onto tile instead of the map.

"Petrov. It's Damian. I need one media credential for tomorrow. East Gallery. Full-room access if you can manage it. Yes, I know what hour it is. I also know whose office covered for your nephew in November, so let's skip the part where you pretend you can't."

Yor blinked once. "Oh."

Anya pressed her mouth shut.

Loid wrote `EAST PRESS` on the edge of the board in block capitals and underlined it once.

*He spends favors for her fast. Noted.*

Damian came back in with the phone still in his hand. "Badge by seven. East entrance, not the diplomatic queue. The ministry line got ugly this afternoon."

Loid pointed the marker at Anya. "If the room breaks, you call it. You don't chase anything."

"I know."

"You don't leave the gallery unless the room is already broken."

"I know."

"If it breaks early," Damian said quietly, "she'll see it."

Loid's jaw moved once. He hated the sentence because he agreed with it.

Yor walked the route from the refrigerator to the hall and back again, counting under her breath, then stopped at the red circle Bond had chosen. "If south goes loud, I cut the first man at the bend and keep the rest off the glass. Somebody else makes sure the floor doesn't read me as part of the attack."

"I can do that," Damian said. "If Voss still has half a conscience, that's where I use it."

"And if he doesn't?" Loid asked.

Damian looked at the west side of the map. "Then I use the other half of the room."

Ugly. Practical. Enough.

No one recited the whole plan after that. Loid marked the east corridor. Yor measured the south line one last time in stocking feet. Damian added two names beside West Gallery and crossed one of them out. Anya spoke only when she had to, and even then everybody in the room seemed to wait through the half second before and after her words, as if they were all noticing the same thing and declining to name it.

By the time Loid capped the marker, the whiteboard held only what they could actually carry into tomorrow: south perimeter circled in red, east corridor cut in black, West Gallery in blue, and `EAST PRESS` underlined once between the fruit magnets. Bond had already gone back to sleep with his nose against Damian's shoe.


# Chapter 15

The International Conference Center smelled of floor polish and burned coffee from the press mezzanine. Anya cleared the east-side screening with Damian's credential clipped high on her lapel and a reporter's notebook under one arm. The screener barely looked at her. Good. It left her free to notice the two details that mattered from the East Gallery: the south wall was all glass, and Deputy Minister Gerhardt would have to stand with it at his back.

Four hundred minds hit her at once.

She sat, braced one hand on the armrest, and let the noise come through.

*if this runs past four my daughter will kill me...*

*translator three is missing paragraph six again...*

*coffee's burned, of course it's burned...*

*south service stairs at thirteen fifty, don't look at the doors again...*

Anya opened her eyes.

That could have been security. It could have been catering. In a room this crowded every guilty thought arrived looking important until the next one shoved it aside.

She kept listening.

*Voss is here. Great. So the old men brought their knives after all...*

*if the Westalians say inspections we slow-walk it to committee...*

*first report has to sound Westalian or the whole thing turns stupid...*

That one came from below and to the right, not the press row beside her. It was a male voice, flat with concentration, not panic or fantasy.

On the platform the moderator shuffled his remarks. Gerhardt sat with one hand on the note card beside his water glass. The printed program inside Anya's notebook put his response at fourteen hundred exactly, second speaker after the Westalian deputy minister. He would have to stand to reach the microphone.

*when he stands, center mass. Glass won't matter if the angle stays clean...*

Anya's fingers bit into the notebook.

She looked down as if checking her notes and found a man in a charcoal suit under the gallery with a press lanyard he wore too still. Everyone else in that row did normal bored summit things. He kept mapping doors, railings, glass, podium.

Her headache started there, a blunt pulse behind her left eye.

She listened harder.

*thirteen fifty they're in place, fourteen hundred the deputy stands, if the first noise comes from Westalian side security writes the rest for us...*

It was not one clean thought. Three different ones, caught in pieces and stitched together around the same geometry: south service stairs, south glass, Gerhardt standing at fourteen hundred, Westalian blame. Bond's red-smeared building vision overlaid the terrace for one sick second. Anya swallowed against it and kept breathing.

It was a staged assassination attempt. Gerhardt dead on camera. Westalis blamed before the blood dried. The summit over with him and peace going with it.

The headache sharpened when she opened herself wider, trying to hold onto the source.

*don't look at the camera, don't look at the...*

*if the line's compromised, south first, comms after...*

*why is the shrimp cold in the middle...*

The last one punched a laugh into her throat. It also wrecked the signal. By the time she cut through the absurdity and reached for the operative mind again, it had sunk back into four hundred other thoughts, some frightened, some petty, most useless.

She scanned the hall anyway. Gerhardt's water glass flashed at the podium. The south doors sat under a discreet security camera. A clean shot from the terrace would not need much time. It would only need him upright.

She stood.

She had no earpiece and no radio. The summit's countermeasures would flag any unauthorized transmission before she got half a sentence out. Their plan had dead drops for exactly this reason. She tucked the notebook under her arm and took the side stairs down to the service corridor, forcing herself not to hurry until the gallery doors shut behind her.

The corridor smelled of bleach, old wiring, and government coffee. Staff and security moved through it in practiced silence. Anya walked fast and read what she could as each mind slid past.

*get the interpreter backup to room five...*

*my God, if this blazer button goes now I'm resigning...*

*Falk's in east comms. If the main board drops the cold line on rack three buys twelve...*

She stopped so hard the heel of one shoe skidded.

It was Falk.

Gerald Falk in east comms, knotted up with venue traffic he should never have touched. And the rest of the thought was worse: main board, rack three, buys twelve.

It was not enough yet. It could be building contingency chatter. It could be the one foggy thread that stole minutes from the live shooter on the terrace if she chased it now.

She hated decisions that only worked if she was right the first time.

At the east-side fire exit she crouched behind a red extinguisher cabinet, tore a page from her notebook, and wrote fast enough to make the letters lean.

*South terrace 1400. Gerhardt target. Falk in comms room east corridor.*

She added *rack 3? cold line?* and stared at it.

Question marks killed field notes. So did bad facts.

She struck the line out hard enough to tear the paper, folded the sheet twice, and slid it behind the extinguisher bracket where Loid would check. Fifteen minutes was their agreement. If everything held, he would find it before 13:45. If it did not, paper was already the smallest broken part of the plan.

On the way back she kept listening.

*if the deputy from Westalis cuts the second paragraph the old bastard will still stand, fine...*

*don't scratch your neck, only guilty people scratch their necks...*

*east rack stays dark unless the board dies, remember that, remember...*

That one vanished before she could sort whether it came from Falk, a technician, or someone who only knew one narrow job and nothing bigger. She carried it with her anyway.

By the time she retook her seat the hall had thickened with the specific silence that came right before microphones went live. Her temples pulsed in time with it. She put both feet flat on the floor and reopened herself to the room.

The moderator gave introductions. The Westalian deputy minister rose. Gerhardt sat with one hand on his note card, waiting for his turn.

Below her, the man in the charcoal suit still did not look at the south doors.

*keep the line clean, wait for the stand...*

Anya counted breaths. She tracked Gerhardt's hand, the south glass, the press row's boredom, and the flickering security thoughts already beginning to bend west because Damian had started moving before anyone had invited him to.

The first stir came from the West Gallery while the deputy minister was still on his opening paragraph.

Damian crossed toward Werner Voss with one hand in his pocket and his face arranged too flat to pass for casual. Half the security detail thought *Desmond* before they even turned their heads. His own surface thoughts hit her sharper than the rest because she knew the shape of them by now.

*Railing. Papers. Make him look at the second signature.*

Voss turned before Damian reached him.

*Not here, you stupid boy.*

Anya could not hear the first exchange through the translation feed and the room's collective throat-clearing, but she did not need every word. Damian set the papers on the gallery rail instead of handing them over. Voss touched the top page. His thumb tightened when he hit the second sheet.

*He knows it,* Damian thought, hot and immediate.

Voss's mind moved faster and meaner.

*Who else saw this. How many copies. Desmond's child, Desmond's posture, same disease.*

The microphones caught a pocket of quiet. This time the words carried.

"You say my name out loud," Voss said, voice low but distinct across the hush, "and your own side calls you unstable before they call me guilty."

Damian did not move. "That sounds like an admission."

"That sounds like advice." Voss's gaze flicked to the floor, the cameras, the delegates already leaning their way. "You think you've cornered one man. Pull my thread and see how fast both sides start burning paper. They'll bury you before they let me stand here alone."

That was enough. Not a lecture. A threat. And the threat told her what she needed anyway: the rot crossed the room and both flags were already standing on it.

*Terrace team hears the floor break and moves. Good,* Voss thought.

Anya lurched half out of her seat.

Damian caught it in Voss's face or his own nerve ran out at the exact right time. He gathered the papers before Voss could decide to take them and turned toward the floor.

The moderator looked annoyed when Damian requested a procedural point. The Ostanian delegation chair looked alarmed before the microphone even went live. Alarm, Anya thought, not surprise.

"State your point," the moderator said.

Anya did not hear Damian's first words so much as watch the room receive them.

Heads turned before he finished the sentence. The Westalian deputy minister stalled mid-breath. Two photographers near the side aisle lifted cameras in the same motion, grateful and vicious.

"I am entering documentary evidence," Damian said, his voice steadier now that he had already set fire to the thing, "that Councillor Werner Voss and connected procurement entities inside our own defense apparatus routed funds and materiel to the Renewal Front. I am also stating for the record that those entities overlap with commercial intermediaries tied to Westalian defense firms represented in this building."

Silence took the hall for half a beat.

Then minds burst open everywhere.

*Desmond, sit down.*

*Moderator, cut his mic.*

*Jesus Christ.*

*He's right.*

*He's dead after this.*

*No, he just saved the minister. Unless he didn't. Unless we're already late.*

Damian kept going because stopping would have turned the moment into a plea.

"If you want shell names, I have them. If you want signatures, I have those too. If anyone in this room has been using defense procurement to fund the same people this summit was called to address, the documentation is here. It runs in both directions. It has for years."

The Ostanian chair grabbed for Damian's sleeve and missed. Damian's thoughts came through in hard clipped flashes now, no polish left.

*Too late to be careful. Too late to be anyone's son about it.*

Across the gallery, Voss did not leave. He stayed broad and still and began calculating the wreckage.

*Which desk moves first. Which counsel panics. Which reporter gets the first clean name.*

Anya pushed farther into the room and regretted it instantly. Four hundred minds under pressure did not get louder. They got sharper. Security. Press. Delegates. Terrace team. East comms. Every thread came barbed.

From somewhere east and below, concrete and wiring dragged another channel against the inside of her skull.

*It's Falk.*

It was Loid, not spoken but thought, cold with recognition.

Then Falk, thinner through the building but unmistakable once it hit.

*No abort code. There was never going to be an abort code.*

Anya's hand went to the side of her head.

The moderator hammered for order. Nobody gave it to him. People were already sorting themselves into camps visible only in posture and surface thought: furious that the allegation existed, furious it had been said in public, furious because they had just understood what would happen to them if it stuck.

And under all of it, from the south side, the shooter's mind tightened like a wire.

*wait for the noise. wait for the stand. now if the room breaks first, now...*

The shot cracked from the terrace doors before Gerhardt ever got to his feet.

Glass burst white at the south side of the hall. For one stupid beat the room tried to misname it as a camera pop, an equipment failure, somebody dropping a tray.

Then the shooter thought at her in one clean spike.

*Missed.*

Anya saw Yor through the burst-glass glare a fraction late, dark blazer, cream blouse, already inside the distance where aim stopped mattering.

After that the south side stopped looking like procedure and started looking like Bond's vision with the timing pulled tight.

One man near the doors dropped with both hands at his throat and made a sound the hall could hear even through the alarm. Another turned too slowly and went to his knees with blood punching through his fingers at the side of his neck. A third hit the railing hard enough to ring it, a knife sunk above his collarbone. On the stairs the last body jerked once under a twist that looked wrong even from the gallery.

Four minds flared and went out in ugly fast sequence.

*what is she...*

*knife knife knife...*

*radio, move, move...*

Nothing.

The live minds around them hit harder.

*South side, shots fired.*

*Who engaged?*

*No, don't let press see the blood, Jesus...*

And over the top of all of it, bright enough to cut through everything else for one impossible second:

*Sis.*

Yuri.

The word hit Anya so hard she stood without meaning to. Her chair legs scraped. The photographer beside her cursed and swung toward the glass.

Somewhere beyond the broken south doors, radios erupted. One voice demanded identification. Yuri's thoughts came back rigid with effort.

*Don't say her name. Don't say sister. Don't say anything you can't live inside.*

"Unknown Westalian security asset," he said.

The lie cost enough that she felt it through two floors of concrete and noise.

Gerhardt had vanished under security bodies. The moderator was still yelling into a microphone nobody was listening to. Damian stood half turned between the floor and the south side, exposed in every direction now, and the room had no idea whether to drag him down or point cameras closer.

The thoughts from east comms kept sawing at her.

*Twenty years.*

*You used my child?*

*Your building called it good work.*

*Crane.*

That last one hit her in the knees.

It came tangled with Falk's stubborn conviction and Loid's blank controlled fury. She could not pull the whole exchange through concrete and panic, only pieces, but the pieces were enough to make her stomach pitch. Crane. Her report. Good work. She did not need the full sentence to know the direction it pointed.

The hall disintegrated into managed panic. Press already had handsets up. Security minds flooded the room in blunt urgent bursts.

*camera six locked south side, hold that feed...*

*Desmond just detonated his own career...*

*No, he just saved the minister, don't be stupid...*

*green light. good. lower east is still moving. booster's hiding it...*

Anya froze.

Not because the thought was complete. It was one surface flare from a man three rows behind her already thinking about exits and blame. But it hit the earlier corridor fragment and locked into place: rack three bought twelve if the board dropped, and the lower east channel was still moving with the booster hiding it.

She got up so fast the seat snapped back against her legs.

The headache that followed had teeth. The room tipped left, then right. She grabbed the press rail until the wave passed and shoved toward the side stairs with one hand hard against her temple.

If she stayed, she could keep reading the room and maybe learn what the relay was carrying. If she left, Damian's thread vanished to her and the summit became four hundred muffled radios she could not sort. The decision still was not clean. She made it anyway.

The east corridor lights stabbed at her eyes. Every footfall jarred her teeth. She passed the fire exit where she had left the note. She passed two security officers running the other way and caught *where the hell is east comms support* from one of them before she hit the Communications Room door hard enough to send it banging into the wall.

Loid was inside, shoulder near the doorway. Falk sat on the floor against the far wall with his wrists cinched behind him by Loid's belt. The main screens were dead.

"Lower east rack," Anya said. Her voice came out thin and wrong. "A backup relay's still live. He kept thinking green light and the booster."

Loid did not ask who *he* was. He moved at once.

The room felt smaller with the main board dead, all dark screens, residual heat from the electronics, and the smell of hot dust and cable insulation crowding the air. Falk turned his head to watch them. Bound did not make him harmless. It barely made him quiet.

Loid dropped to one knee in front of the lower rack. Anya went down beside him because standing had become unreliable and because sight alone would not find the thing fast enough.

Metal faceplates and bundled cables crowded the rack. Low on it sat a dull gray signal booster, rectangular and innocent-looking, with more dead space behind it than the casing should have allowed.

*good. green light. just keep it moving.*

Her hand shook when she pointed. "There. Behind it."

Loid tore the mounting screws free with two hard turns and wrenched the booster housing sideways. A smaller device sat recessed in the dark behind it, no larger than a paperback, one green indicator burning steadily on its face.

Falk closed his eyes.

Loid ripped the power lead free.

The green light died.

For one second nobody spoke. The silence after a correct guess never felt like relief. It felt like standing exactly where the next bad fact would land.

Then Loid found the tiny status strip along the relay edge and tipped it toward the ceiling light.

`BUFFER REMAINING 10:48`

Twelve minutes total. Just over a minute spent since the main board died.

The arithmetic held. That made it worse, not better.

"What was it carrying?" Loid asked.

Falk opened his eyes again. "Enough."

Loid set the dead relay on the desk with such care that the restraint became its own violence.

Anya was still on the floor. The relay's green light had burned itself onto the back of her eyes. She kept seeing it every time she blinked.

Falk's thoughts would not stay out, even with her skull trying to close around the noise.

*too neat. should've known she'd hear the rest sooner or later.*

*the girl writes like him.*

That one hit harder than the green light.

Loid must have read the change in her face. He turned from the dead relay to Falk.

"Say it again," he said.

Falk looked from Loid to Anya and back. His breathing was rough from being thrown down, but conviction still sat under it, dry and ugly and upright.

"Her first assessment," he said. "The one that told your desk to stop looking where it should've kept looking. It cut a clean lane through review, and I used it. Traffic the building stopped prioritizing moved through that lane. The packet that put Crane at that checkpoint was one of mine."

Anya did not feel herself breathe.

Loid's face emptied out the way it did when whatever he actually felt had become too expensive to show in front of witnesses.

"You used my child to clean your channel."

"I used work your building stamped excellent." Falk swallowed and kept going because ruining other people had turned into his idea of honesty years ago. "That was the whole sickness. Everyone wanted the numbers filed smooth enough to call surrender peace."

Crane.

She had spent months refusing to think his name all the way through. Border operation. Checkpoint compromise. The memorial Loid said she did not need to attend. Now Falk had shoved the name into the room and there it was, whole and ugly and impossible to misfile again.

Boots pounded in the corridor.

Loid crossed to the door, checked the hall, then came back long enough to haul Falk upright by the arm.

"Not another word in here," he said.

He marched Falk out ahead of the response team and handed him over with only the rank and minimum explanation the corridor required. Security flooded the room around the dead consoles. Nobody asked Anya anything direct yet. That felt temporary in the worst possible way.

When she finally got clear of the room and the building's bright official interior, the cold outside hit her like a second concussion.

She made it to the stone wall beside the service exit and braced one hand against it.

The summit was still emptying by the front steps. Sirens climbed the street. Camera crews crowded the barricades in hard bright knots. Every mind within range was doing the same frantic sorting: shots fired, summit blown open, Desmond, Voss, minister safe, who gets blamed, who gets buried, who gets quoted.

Crane.

She had written the report because Damian was useful and frightened and not what WISE thought he was. She had written it because she wanted room to move. She had written it because the first lie had worked and competence made the second lie easier. After that the reasons had piled up until they looked like structure.

None of them put Crane back anywhere.

Loid came out a few minutes later with his belt still missing and her folded note in one hand. He stopped beside her, not close enough to touch.

"Can you walk," he asked.

She nodded.

He studied her for one long second, the way he studied locked doors before deciding whether they were safe to open.

"Good," he said. "You're with me until we're home."

"Dad, I..."

"Not here." His voice stayed level. "And not in pieces. All of it."

That was worse than shouting would have been.

Out by the barricades, three separate minds were already trying Damian's name on different verdicts.

*traitor.*

*hero.*

*useful.*

Loid turned toward the street. Anya went with him.

He kept her close enough to stop if she drifted and far enough that their sleeves never touched.


# Chapter 16

By the time Loid got her back to the apartment, the summit smoke had dried into her coat and the kitchen clock said 2:11.

He turned on the stove light, not the ceiling one. It left the room in stripes: sink, table, the edge of the whiteboard still scarred with summit routes. His shirt was clean in the places he'd had time to clean. The cuff wasn't.

"Start with the part Desmond already knew," he said.

Anya stood at the table because sitting felt like permission to shake. Crane's name kept surfacing out of the summit noise with the same hard stop at the end. Falk was in cuffs. Yuri was still on the terrace in her head. Her father's hand had failed to land on her shoulder.

"He knew my real name before yesterday," she said. "Since the gala. He kept it quiet. At the summit he moved me off the main screening line because he knew somebody there could connect the cover to Eden."

Loid went still in the disciplined way that meant his mind had started moving too fast.

"How much does he know."

"My name. School. My family." She swallowed. "Not the rest."

The pause stretched.

"I'm due at Station in forty minutes," he said. "Sylvia will want Falk's access chain before dawn, and I'm not taking your explanation in fragments while half of WISE is still on fire." He looked at her then, direct and cold. "You get one stop. Desmond. You tell him whatever he needs before other people start filling in the blanks for him. Then you come back here and tell me all of it."

"Dad..."

"All of it," he repeated. "No disappearing."

The words landed with the same cold force as the street outside. Then, quieter and uglier for it:

"Crane is still dead. I'm not spending today chasing another surprise."

He left five minutes later with his coat half-buttoned and the kitchen light still on. Anya stood in it until the building door shut downstairs.

By the time she reached Damian's floor, Loid was on his way to WISE, Yuri's face on the terrace kept flashing through her whether she wanted it or not, and Crane was not getting any less dead.

She stood outside his door for four minutes before she knocked.

Four minutes gave her three neighbors through plaster and pipes: the woman downstairs arguing with herself about whether to call her sister, the man two doors down rewriting a complaint about the hallway lights until the wording got nasty, somebody above coughing wetly into a sink. It was normal apartment stuff from normal-size brains. Anya put her palm on Damian's door, caught the low hum of him on the other side, and jerked back before the thought shaped itself.

*Not like this. Not before the knock.*

She knocked.

The lock turned after one exact pause. Surprise hadn't done that. Decision had.

When the door opened, Damian was wearing a dark sweater with the sleeves shoved up. He looked like he'd been sitting still too long.

"Hi," she said.

"Hi."

*She's here. Okay.*

That one came through simple and flat, like he'd set it on the table between them.

He moved aside. She went in.

His apartment was neat in the boring, lived-in way. Books were lined up straight. One glass was drying beside the sink. Policy journals were stacked on the side table with a pen clipped to the top one. The couch still had the dent from his usual spot. One shoe lay half under it and the other had been left by the door. Last time she'd been here she read the place like a floor plan, exits and corners and signal strength. Tonight it just looked like where he took off his shoes and forgot about them, and where he'd spent an hour failing to do anything that counted as work.

The childhood box sat open on the coffee table.

The class photo had been pulled out and left beside it. Beside the photo lay a ministry notepad with one sentence started and then gouged out so hard the paper had torn.

Anya stopped.

It wasn't because of the photo. That part wasn't new. Since Becky's gala she'd heard the schoolyard flashing through him in pieces: pink hair, Becky crying, a six-year-old punch, the almost-name catching and slipping away. The bad part was the box open right now. He had been in there tonight.

He didn't offer a drink. Neither of them sat.

"I need to tell you this," she said.

*Yeah. I figured.*

His thoughts were already crowding around the wrong guesses: that the alliance was over, that there was new fallout from the summit, that she was leaving the city. None of it was close.

"Okay."

He leaned against the counter and crossed his arms. It might have passed for casual if you didn't look at the shoulders.

"It's not about the operation," Anya said. "It's about me. About what I can do."

She had rehearsed this in the bathroom mirror that morning with toothpaste on her lip. She'd rehearsed it on the walk over too. Every version sounded stupid. There was a clinical one, a dramatic one, and a tiny one. *I can kind of hear people.* That one made her want to throw herself into traffic.

"I'm a telepath," she said. "I read minds. I've been doing it since I was little. Four, maybe five, I don't know. I've never told anybody. Not my parents, not Becky, not WISE. Nobody."

Silence hit the room hard enough to feel.

*What.*

Then, a beat later:

*She said she reads minds.*

He drew one careful breath that barely moved his shoulders. Thoughts started firing past in broken pieces: the state dinner, the service corridor, Bretz, the safe house, every time she'd answered too fast, every time she'd known facts she shouldn't.

*She read my thoughts. All this time.*

"At the state dinner," he said. "That first night. You were reading my thoughts then too."

"Yes."

"Everything in that room. About the operation. About..."

The rest flashed before he said it: her smile, Eden Academy, a pink-haired girl.

"Yes," Anya said again.

His face changed, not because of the confession but because she'd answered the thought.

"Stop doing that." Sharper now. "Can you stop?"

"I can stop reacting. I can't stop hearing it. It's like sound."

He pushed off the counter and crossed to the sink, then back again, as if distance might blunt it. It didn't. The thoughts kept arriving from the same radius, only sharper now because he was listening to himself listening.

*Chair. Faucet. Window latch. Don't think about the safe house. Don't think about anything with a face.*

Anya looked at the floor.

"You can hear me trying not to think about it."

"Yes."

He squeezed the bridge of his nose until the skin blanched. "Jesus Christ."

*Every thought. God. The negotiations. The club. The park. The safe house.*

He caught the edge of the counter hard enough to click the glass beside the sink. Then he let it go and scrubbed a hand over the back of his neck. He had nowhere to put his hands. He had nowhere to put any of it.

*The kiss.*

That thought came up jagged and hot.

*The safe house. She was in my head when I kissed her.*

"Yes," Anya said quietly.

Anger arrived all at once. It wasn't loud, which made it worse. Every word came out too clear, clipped into the politeness he used when he was closest to fury.

"So every conversation we've had. Every time I thought I was telling you anything because I chose to tell you. You already had it."

"Yes."

"You used it on purpose."

The answer had to come fast or it would sound like she was shopping for one.

"Yes."

His mouth flattened.

"Say it properly."

Anya swallowed.

"I answered questions before you finished asking them. I changed what I was going to say because I already knew the answer sitting on top of you. Sometimes I stayed because I heard you wanted me there. Sometimes I left because I heard you were about to ask for more than I could take cleanly."

He stared at her.

"How many times is sometimes."

"Enough that I can't make it small."

His gaze jerked away again, like the sight of her made the fact worse.

"The safe house. My father. My mother. Things I said because I was trying to be honest."

"I already knew."

He looked away fast, like looking at her hurt. Two steps took him to the window. Both hands braced on the sill.

"My mother," he said to the glass. "At the reception."

"The marigolds on the terrace," Anya said. "Before she got sick."

His breath hitched out of him.

*I never said that out loud.*

Outside, lights kept turning on across Berlint like nothing in this room mattered to anybody else.

Anya let him have the anger. There was no excuse to offer, no speech about not choosing the ability, no little pile of true things that would still be useless here.

"How far does it go?" he asked.

"Surface thoughts. What's on top. If a memory is live, I get that too because you're thinking it right then. But I can't go digging. I can't pull up things you aren't having."

"Just the surface."

*As if the surface isn't the part that counts.*

He wasn't wrong.

"And it's not truth exactly," Anya said. "That's part of why it's such a mess. Surface thoughts lie all the time. People rehearse things, panic about things, imagine things. I get access. I don't get God."

He turned from the window enough to look at her.

"That's not better."

"I know."

"It's worse in a different way."

"Yeah."

His hand dragged down over his mouth.

"Does anybody else know?"

"No."

"Nobody."

"Nobody."

That landed differently. It didn't ease anything. It made the room stranger.

*I'm the first.*

Anya kept her face still. She had expected fury, revulsion, even a door in her face. She had not expected to hand him the ugliest thing she owned and realize he was the first person who had ever actually touched it.

"I'm sorry," she said. "And I'm not going to do the thing where I tell you not to be angry. Be angry."

"Don't manage it for me."

"Okay."

*Good.* A beat. *No. Not good. I don't know what I want.*

The next wave came through messier. It wasn't clean anger now. It was sick realization: every ugly little private thought handed over without consent, every soft one too.

Then, under all of it:

*She knows the bad parts and she still came here.*

Anya kept her face still. For once she let the thought stay where it belonged, even though she'd heard it.

"I don't know what to do with this," Damian said. His eyes were red around the edges. "I genuinely don't know what to do with the idea that every private thought I've had in front of you was..."

He stopped.

His gaze had dropped to the coffee table, to the box, to the photo lying beside it.

*Tell her.*

The thought came through clean as a bell.

He crossed the room, picked up the class photo, and held it up.

"Second row," he said. "Third from the left. Pink hair. That's you."

Anya looked.

She was six years old, mouth open in a grin at the photographer, one hand blurred from waving. Damian was in the third row, second from the right, already looking annoyed in exactly the way he'd been born to be.

"I know who you are," he said. "Anya Forger. Eden Academy, Class Three. You punched me when we were six because I called Becky a crybaby. I've known since the gala."

The shock wasn't *who*. It was *when*.

It was November, this apartment, the photo under his lamp, months stacked behind his voice.

"The gala," she said. "I knew you were close then. I heard that part. I didn't know you went home and made it certain."

"A classmate said your name. I came back here, found this, checked. It was you."

*She caught the circling. Not this room. Not the part where it became a fact.*

That hit harder than the school photo: his desk lamp, this table, his hands on the picture. She hadn't been here.

"At my parents' apartment," Anya said. "When you met my dad. At the summit yesterday. All that time."

"Yes."

"Why didn't you say anything?"

Damian took longer with the next breath.

"Because the operation still mattered. Because saying it in November would've blown everything sideways. And because I kept taking one more day instead. Then another."

He looked back at the photo, not at her.

"That's not noble. It's just what I did."

The notepad with the torn line sat between them. One more day sat there too, written into the paper until it failed.

"And the summit," Anya said. "The badge. East Gallery."

His head came up.

"What about it."

"You moved me there before plenary. Better sight lines, separate entrance, all that. I knew you were keeping me off the main line. I never knew why."

The pause this time was answer enough before he gave the words.

"The main press intake was under ministry screening after Kreuzfeld. Security was cross-checking credentials for anyone near the central galleries. One of my father's aides was sitting in that room, and he knew Eden rosters well enough to be dangerous. If you'd gone through the main line wearing borrowed papers, somebody had a real chance of putting the cultural attaché and Anya Forger in the same file before the afternoon session. East Gallery used the foreign overflow desk and a side stair. Less scrutiny. No Desmond staff. I kept you off that screening line."

The room tipped a little.

"Because you knew."

"Because I knew."

"You were protecting my cover."

"I was trying to keep it from getting worse."

There was no clean version of that. He knew it. She knew it.

"Why not tell me then," Anya asked, "if we were already standing in the same fire."

"Because if I said it there, in the middle of the summit, we would've had this conversation with half the ministry still watching the doors. And because..." He stopped, the correction visible before it happened. His eyes stayed on the photo, and his thumb worked harder over the paper edge. "Because I didn't want to lose you."

*Both. But mostly that second thing.*

Air went out of Anya in one rough push. Her knees gave up before the rest of her did. She sat on the floor beside the table because that was where her body had decided she belonged.

"So we both did it," she said. "Hidden information. Kept going."

Damian lowered himself onto the couch instead of staying over her. That was good. The distance helped.

"Not the same thing," he said.

"No. What I did is worse."

"Maybe."

"No. It is."

He didn't answer. The photo stayed in his hands. Two children stared up from glossy paper while the adults who used to be them failed to say anything useful.

"When you came to my parents' apartment," Anya said after a while, "what did you think you were doing."

His laugh was short and dead on arrival.

"Trying not to stare at your family photos. Trying not to act like I'd shown up at someone's dinner table carrying a second conversation nobody else could hear."

That made her chest hurt in a new place.

"You could've walked."

"Yes."

"You didn't."

"No."

His thumb moved over the edge of the photo without touching either of the children on it.

"I kept waiting for the girl from school and the woman from the ministry to separate into two different people. They didn't. That did not make any of this easier."

Anya folded her arms around her knees.

"No."

That was the nearest they got to agreement before the room ran out of first-hour words.

---

The second hour went quieter.

At some point Damian had put the kettle on and forgotten it. One mug of tea went cold beside the sink. Her water glass sat untouched and sweating on the table. Damian was back in the dent on the couch. Anya had climbed up to the far cushion with her knees tucked under her chin. The box stayed open between them.

The anger was still there. So was the part underneath it.

"When you say it's like sound," he said at last, "what does that actually mean."

"If it's one person and they're close, it's clear enough to hurt," Anya said. "Crowds smear. Strong feelings punch through walls. Distance helps. Not enough."

"And there's no way to shut it off."

"No."

He nodded once at the window, like the dark outside had just offered him a new category of bad.

"Does anybody else know?"

"No."

That landed differently.

"I'm the first."

"Yeah."

He looked back at her.

"And with people you care about."

She pressed her thumbnail into the seam of the cushion.

"I stay far enough back that it doesn't matter."

"Did that work."

"Until you."

The room held that without helping either of them.

"Then why now."

"Because Loid's waiting at home for the full account," she said. "Because Crane's still dead. Because after the gala you knew my name and still didn't say it, and yesterday I was still taking from you. I was done doing that."

His jaw moved once.

"Can I ask the ugly part now."

"Yeah."

"When we kissed in the safe house. Was that you, or was that me hitting you hard enough that you couldn't tell the difference."

Anya let out one thin breath.

"I don't know how to split it clean," she said. "In the safe house, when we kissed, I was reading you. I was trying not to, but you were too close and feeling too much. I could feel what you wanted. My body answered before I'd figured out whether I was answering you or me. That's the part I still can't sort."

He sat very still.

"Meaning maybe you wanted me because I wanted you badly enough for both of us."

"Meaning maybe the signal hit first. Not all of it. Not none of it. I don't know."

He looked down at his hands.

"Did you ever hear anything from me and leave it alone."

"Yes."

"What."

"You don't want that one."

"No," he said. "I probably don't."

But he stayed where he was, waiting. She fixed her eyes on the rim of the water glass.

"In the safe house bathroom, before you came back out, you were thinking that if you put your hands on me again you wouldn't stop where you should. You hated yourself for it and still wanted it. I didn't use that either."

His eyes closed.

*I asked. Christ. I asked for that one.*

He set the tea down too hard. A little spilled over his knuckles. Then he was moving.

"Anya."

She didn't answer. Her throat had gone useless.

"I'm not saying this makes sense."

"I know."

His hand came up, hovered once at her jaw, then settled there warm and unsteady. Her pulse kicked hard against his thumb. Thought and touch hit at the same time, rough enough to make her breath catch.

*Still want you. Still. Even now.*

He kissed her.

It went wrong before it went right. Her teeth clicked lightly against his. Wool scratched her palm when she grabbed his sleeve. His breath was hot and uneven against her cheek, and the want coming off him was so bright it hurt, tangled with shame and anger and the same stubborn refusal to let go.

*Don't make this clean. Don't make it mercy.*

When he pulled away, he stayed there a second with his forehead against hers, both of them breathing like they'd run for it.

"I can go," Anya said.

He looked at her, eyes wrecked open now.

"I can leave right now. I won't stay in the hall listening. If you want me back in the room after that, you ask."

For a long moment he said nothing. Then he sat down beside her instead of going for the door.

The couch dipped under the added weight. Their shoulders didn't touch. The class photo lay face-up on the table. In the kitchen, the kettle clicked as the metal finished cooling.

After a while he scrubbed a hand over his mouth.

"I need time," he said. "Not because I'm done. Because I need to think about this somewhere you can't hear me doing it."

"Okay."

"And when you leave, don't stay outside trying to measure it."

"I won't."

"I'll call you."

He believed the promise maybe halfway. It was enough for tonight.

Anya got one arm into her coat. He walked her to the door and stopped with his hand on the knob.

"Anya, wait."

She looked up.

"I'm glad you told me."

*I'm glad you told me.*

Her hand stalled on the other sleeve.

"Goodnight," she said.

"Goodnight."

The hallway light flattened everything. The place where his thumb had pressed under her ear kept beating under the cold. She took the stairs toward Loid's kitchen light without leaning back toward the door.


# Chapter 17

On the tram to WISE, Anya could tell Berlint had started thinking about other things.

The air still wanted a coat and gloves, but the thoughts didn't. All winter the city had been thinking about arrests, investigations, borders, who would take the fall and who would get paid to bury the embarrassing parts. Now the noise had thinned into ordinary stuff: paint samples, grocery lists, a man worrying about his mother's hip surgery, somebody trying to remember whether the butcher closed at six. The Renewal Front had gone where scandals went after the shouting, into courtrooms and committees and procedural language. Both governments were doing accountability in public and burial work in private. Damian still showed up in the papers, but in the tired middle now, in consultations and threats and reform panels. He was not a hero and not a disgrace. He was inconveniently real.

Inside WISE, the restructuring showed. Falk's desk in the analyst pool had been cleared within days of his arrest, but the gap still held its shape. There were new faces and new security checks. One junior officer at the checkpoint was comparing signatures as if ink itself had turned treasonous. Sylvia's review board had gone through the place hard enough that even the coffee tasted more cautious.

Sylvia had a file open on her desk when Anya came in. She closed it before Anya could read the label. The thought on the surface was quick and sour: *record. gaps. should've seen it sooner.*

"Sit down."

Anya sat.

"The review board has completed its assessment of your record. Your field status is reinstated, effective immediately. The cover identity remains intact. The summit exposure stayed in political channels, not intelligence ones."

She said it like a weather report. It wasn't cold or kind. It was final.

Anya let out a breath she had not wanted Sylvia to hear. "That's it?"

"No." Sylvia opened the file again, not enough for Anya to read, enough to make the paper sound deliberate. "That is the part you needed. This is the part I need. Your reports will be complete. Your improvisation will stop where institutional damage starts. And if you ever decide on your own what this service can survive not knowing, I will take your field status back so fast you will think this morning was a clerical error."

"Understood."

Sylvia looked at her for a long second over the rim of her glasses. *Too young for this. old enough to know better. both can be true.*

"You are very good at your job," she said. "That is not the same thing as being harmless."

Anya swallowed. "I know."

"Good. Then I don't have to waste time making you say it twice." Sylvia slid a badge wallet across the desk. "You're on rotation next week. Desk brief this afternoon. If I change my mind before then, you'll hear about it."

The leather felt familiar in her hand and not familiar at all. It held the old access card, her name, and her cover face. It was the little institutional rectangle that meant doors would open again.

"Thank you," she said, because she had already said the wrong thing once.

"Do not thank me. Do the work."

Outside, the sky over the diplomatic quarter had that late-March brightness that looked warmer than it was. She tucked the badge wallet into her coat and started walking.

The easy part, now, was calling Damian and knowing he would answer.

That had not been true in January.

The first call had come after a week. She was halfway back from a miserable briefing and thinking about dumplings when an unknown number lit up her phone.

"It's me," Damian said, and before she could say anything else he added, "I'm outside your building. Across the street. In the car. I know that sounded terrible."

He was standing beside the sedan when she got there, hands in his pockets, wind dragging at his coat. They didn't do the whole conversation from the top. He said the silence had started lying for him. He said if he needed a thought to stay his, he'd say so out loud. If he meant her to have it, he'd leave it where she could hear.

At the corner he looked at her on purpose and let one sit between them.

*I missed you.*

That had been enough to move the winter forward. After that there had been calls, bad tea in paper cups, one museum afternoon where neither of them cared about the paintings, and a week in February when Damian had to disappear into committee hearings and sent three-word messages at midnight like somebody learning not to make absence do all the talking for him. It had not become simple. It had become real enough to use.

By March, she could call him on the walk home and know he would answer.

"Six o'clock," she said when he picked up.

"Six. I'll be there."

"Don't bring wine. Papa will judge the label."

A pause. "I wasn't going to bring wine."

"You were absolutely going to bring wine."

Another pause, more wounded this time. "What kind does he drink?"

Anya laughed. It came easy now, easier than it used to, because the laugh no longer had to drag a private audit behind it. Damian knew about the telepathy. He knew and had stayed, not cleanly, not magically, but on purpose.

"Red," she said. "Nothing with a year on it he might actually recognize."

"That's an impossible category."

"I believe in you."

"You shouldn't."

She hung up smiling and walked the rest of the way home through streets full of people thinking about dogs and trams and paint and dinner, and for once none of it felt like weight she had to carry alone.

---

The Forger apartment smelled like onions pushed a little too far and hot oil full of cumin.

"Mama made curry," Anya said as she hung up her coat.

She could tell from the smell. She could also tell from Loid's thought: *The potatoes are raw in the middle. I checked one when she wasn't looking. The rice is ready. The rice will save us all.*

"Your mother worked very hard on it," Loid said from the kitchen.

He was setting out bowls, the ceramic set they used when somebody was coming over. There were four bowls and four place settings. He had angled Bond's water dish away from the table and left enough open floor near Damian's chair for Bond to appoint himself part of the evening without pretending to be a diner.

He glanced up at her once. *How did Sylvia put it. Don't ask too fast.*

"How'd it go?"

"I'm back in."

A small pause. "Good."

He put down the last spoon. "Did she make it sound like a favor."

"No. More like a threat with paperwork."

"That sounds right."

Anya stepped into the kitchen proper. The stove was doing several things at once. Rice steamed in one pot. Curry thickened in another. Yor's handwriting on the recipe card had acquired new oil stains and one frantic underline under the word *stir*. On the counter beside the sink sat a stack of clean plates on a dish towel.

Loid had his reading glasses on and was slicing a cucumber into exact clean coins.

"Help me with the tea things," he said.

She took down the tray because it was easier than saying no.

"I read the board abstract," he said after a minute.

There it was.

"Of course you did."

"It was on my desk."

"That feels worse somehow."

He did not answer that. He set the knife down in a straight line against the cutting board and looked at the cups instead of her.

"They confirmed enough," he said. "About Falk. About the blind channel. About the report."

"Yeah."

"Do you understand what I mean when I say enough."

Anya lined the cups up on the tray one by one. "I know what my report opened."

"That's not what I asked."

The answer wanted to come out defensive. She bit it back because that would have turned him into her handler and she was tired of that being the easiest version of him to fight.

"Yes," she said. "I understand."

Loid was quiet for a beat. *Good. She should.* Then, under that: *Too young. Not an excuse. Still true.*

"The board kept calling it improvisation," he said.

"It wasn't."

"No."

He rinsed the knife though it did not need rinsing yet. "It was a choice."

She leaned a hip against the counter. "I know that too."

"Do you."

"Stop asking like I don't."

His mouth tightened, not angry exactly. He looked more tired than that.

"I'm asking because dead people don't care whether the mistake was deliberate," he said. "Crane doesn't. Falk certainly doesn't."

The name hit like a bruise you had forgotten until somebody pressed it.

"I wasn't trying to get anybody killed."

"I know." He turned off the tap. Water kept ticking down the drain. "That is part of why this is hard to be angry about cleanly."

She looked at him.

He reached up, took off his glasses, and set them beside the sink. "The worst part isn't that you lied into the system. It's that I know exactly how a person gets to that lie."

Her face must have changed because he looked away first.

"You thought you could hold the cost yourself," he said. "You thought if you managed it well enough, the damage would stay local."

"You don't get to sound like you weren't there," Anya said.

"I'm not."

"You trained me."

"I know."

"You taught me how to read rooms and move people before they noticed I was moving them."

"I know that too."

The words landed between them with no way around them.

From the stove, curry burped ominously. Neither of them moved.

Loid set his hand on the edge of the counter. "This isn't me pretending I'm innocent because I'm older. It isn't me pretending I don't recognize my own methods in you. I do. That's the problem."

Anya swallowed hard enough it hurt.

"So what," she said. "Now what."

He looked down at the counter again, more carefully than he needed to.

"Now you don't do the next one alone."

She frowned. "The next what."

"The next impossible call where somebody you care about and the work stop fitting in the same sentence." He met her eyes then, direct enough to make it uncomfortable. "If you decide protecting somebody is worth lying into the system again, you tell me before I find out from the wreckage."

"What if you try to stop me."

"I probably will."

"That doesn't make the sales pitch stronger."

"I'm not selling it. I'm telling you the new terms."

She looked down at the tea tray because the porcelain gave her somewhere to put her eyes.

"Are you saying this as my father or my handler."

"I'm saying it as the person who got very tired of discovering how much those jobs have been the same job."

That one hit harder than she wanted, not because it was cruel but because it wasn't.

The kitchen door bumped open with Yor's hip. She came in holding a wooden spoon like she had forgotten where she'd meant to take it.

"Oh," she said, looking between them. "Did I interrupt something."

"No," Loid said at once.

"Yes," Anya said at the same time.

Yor blinked. *They're doing the quiet sharp thing. Please not tonight.*

"I wanted to ask if we have enough tea," Yor said. "And also if the curry smells normal or if I should start over. Not all the way. Not from the beginning. Maybe from the point where it became... this."

Loid crossed the kitchen in two steps and kissed her temple in passing on his way to check the pot.

"It smells good," he said.

"You are a very loving liar."

"I'm a very loving husband."

That got the corner of her mouth up, which had obviously been the point.

Anya carried the tray out to the table while Yor followed with the spoon and the thought-noise of somebody trying not to have a ruined evening by sheer force. Halfway to the sideboard Yor stopped, head turning toward the apartment door at a sound in the hall. Footsteps passed. Two voices moved by. There was no knock.

Her shoulders stayed high until the sound moved on.

*Not him. Or maybe good if not him. I don't know anymore.*

Anya pretended to fuss with the napkins so she would not have to look directly at her mother while hearing that.

Loid came out with the cucumber plate and set it down.

"Not before six," he said quietly to Anya as he passed.

"That was already more than before six."

"Yes."

"Was that the whole thing."

"No."

Before she could answer, the buzzer went at six-oh-three.

Yor's thoughts came in a rush: *He's here. Is there curry on my face. Thank god it's him. Why am I relieved it's him.*

Loid opened the door.

Damian stood in the hall in a dark coat with his hair combed and a bottle of wine held in both hands like evidence.

*Don't think about the wine. You brought the wine. She told you not to bring wine. Don't think about it.*

"Damian," Loid said, holding out his hand.

The handshake was exact. Loid's were always exact. Under the politeness, his assessment clicked into place: *Firm grip. Nervous. New coat. He dressed for this anyway.*

"Thank you for having me, sir. Loid. Mr. Forger." Damian's face tried to recover and did not quite get there. *Pick one. You practiced this. You practiced this on the walk over.*

"Come in," Loid said.

Bond got there before Yor did, lumbering up from the rug to inspect Damian's cuffs with grave household seriousness.

"Oh," Yor said, appearing from the kitchen with her sleeves shoved up past her elbows and a streak of curry on her cheek. "Damian! You came. And Bond likes you already. That's a good sign. Oh, and you brought wine. That's so thoughtful."

Loid took the bottle and looked at the label.

Damian's mind went white-hot with panic. *He's reading it. She told me he'd read it. Think about anything else. The couch. Dog hair. Very normal family. Extremely normal family dog hair.*

"It's a nice bottle," Loid said.

Whether that was true or diplomatic was impossible to tell.

They sat. There were four human plates and four cups. Bond stationed himself near Damian's chair with the patience of an animal who had been in this house long enough to know food eventually found a floor.

Yor served the curry with the hopeful pride of somebody who had spent all afternoon on a thing and needed it to count. The sauce was thick and dark. The potatoes were hard in the center. The spice hit fast enough that Anya's eyes watered on the first bite. She reached for the rice. Loid had made it perfectly, of course.

Damian took one bite and his thoughts turned into a full-body emergency. *Oh. Oh god. My mouth. Okay. Smile. This is Anya's mother's cooking. You are going to eat this and you are going to survive it.* He reached for his water. Then panic doubled back on itself. *Don't reach too fast. People drink water. Normal dinner water. Why am I thinking about how to drink water. She can hear me thinking about water.*

He caught her catching it.

For a second his face blanked like he meant to force every thought back down where she could not get at it. Instead he looked at her over the rim of the glass.

*It's bad. Don't laugh. I'm still glad I came.*

It landed differently. The water thoughts had spilled out of him. This one was placed. She had not taken it. He had offered it awkwardly, like he had set a fragile thing on the table between them and was pretending not to watch whether she would pick it up.

Anya bit the inside of her cheek hard enough to keep her face steady.

"This is really good, Mrs. Forger," Damian said.

His voice held. His eyes did not.

"Oh! Really? You don't think it's too... Loid thought maybe the chili was a bit much..."

"It's got character," Loid said.

He kept eating with the durable calm of a man who had been married to Yor for fourteen years and understood that this was one of the forms love took in his house. *Better than last month. Potatoes are a problem. Sauce is better.*

"I used less chili this time," Yor said brightly.

Damian took another bite with the grim focus of a diplomat entering hostile territory. *I am finishing this plate. I am not disgracing myself in front of her parents. If my tongue dies, it dies in service.*

Anya looked down at her rice before the laugh could get out.

"You're too thin," Yor told him.

"I..."

"She says that to everyone," Anya said.

"I don't say it to everyone. I said it to Becky once and she was very pleased."

"That's different. Becky hears it as a compliment."

"Well, with Damian I mean it nutritionally." Yor pushed the curry closer to him. "Have more."

Damian did not look at the serving spoon like it was a weapon. He deserved credit for that.

"Thank you," he said, with the bravery of a condemned man.

The conversation settled into small things. Yor asked about work. Damian gave the polished version, careful and qualified. Loid asked about the new oversight committee in the same tone he might have used to ask someone to pass the salt. Damian answered. Loid lined up another question in his head. *Who actually chairs the subcommittee. See which name he gives.* At the exact same time Damian thought, *If he asks for committee names I'm dead,* and Yor thought, *Nobody is eating enough rice.*

"Nobody is talking about committee chairs at dinner," Anya said.

Three faces turned toward her.

Loid set down his fork. "I hadn't asked about committee chairs."

Heat climbed her neck too fast. "You were about to. You get that face."

"What face?" Yor asked.

"The interview face," Anya said. "The one where he acts normal and then suddenly you're explaining procurement law for half an hour."

Damian made a noise into his water glass that might have been relief trying not to become laughter.

Loid looked at her for a beat. His thought flashed first. *One evening. Keep the promise.* Then, aloud, he said to Damian, "I'm interested in your friend's work."

"He's not my friend."

"Your colleague."

"He's not my colleague either."

Loid took a sip of Damian's wine. "What term would you prefer."

Anya opened her mouth and immediately regretted it. Damian's thoughts were no help at all for a second. *We do not have a term. We read each other's minds and kissed in a safe house and I have no term for that. There is probably not a term in any language.*

Then, quieter and very deliberately, he gave her the only answer he could get away with.

*Please don't let me answer that in front of your father.*

She nearly choked on rice.

"Damian's fine," she said, coughing once into her hand. "Just Damian."

Loid inclined his head. He did not ask about committee chairs.
